Preface

tensen. The report is conducted at the Department of Transport of the Technical Univer-
sity of Denmark in the period January to June 2015. The report constitutes a total of 60
ETCS points, 30 for each author. The ofﬁcial supervisors of the project have been Pro-
fessor Otto Anker Nielsen, Professor Carlo Giacomo Prato and Assistant Professor Marie
Karen Anderson.

==PAGE:7==
Abstract
The subject of this thesis is transfers in public transport route choice models.
Understanding travellers’ behaviour in public transport is important in order to attract
more passengers to public transport. A tool for understanding travellers’ preferences is
route choice modelling. Studies show that many travellers ﬁnd transfers inconvenient
when using the public transport system.
The thesis at hand seeks to dissect the transfer penalty into separate pieces in order to
be able to explain in more detail what the travellers perceive when transferring. A great
deal of attention has so far been drawn towards route choices in uni-modal public trans-
port systems, whereas research regarding multi-modal public transport networks is still
limited. The present thesis will focus on a multi-modal public transport network in the
Greater Copenhagen Area. The foundation of this thesis is based on previous research
within this ﬁeld of study, which show that transfers in public transport are a burden for
travellers using the public transport systems. Literature is presented, where different
transfer attributes has been examined in order to determine travellers preferences. The
review was also used as an inspiration for which types of attributes that could be inter-
esting to test for the study in this thesis.
The observed data in this thesis is based on revealed preference data and is collected
through the TU survey (Transportvaneundersøgelsen) in the Greater Copenhagen Area.
The public transport network within the area of the Greater Copenhagen consists of no
less than ten different transport modes, which makes it a very complex network with
many possible transfers. The choice set used for this thesis is generated in Anderson
(2013) and contains close to 200 possible routes for each OD pair, where some routes
makes more sense than others.
In the selection of attributes a number of attributes were examined on order to choose
the ﬁnal four attributes. Based on the literature review ten attributes were selected for
further evaluation: appearance, detour, seats, safety, security, shops, level changes, oc-
cupancy, shelters, and information level. Each of the ten attributes were evaluated and
rated according to four criteria in order to select the ﬁnal attributes. The criteria used
vii
==PAGE:8==
VIII
for evaluating the attributes were validity, reliability, measurability, and data availabil-
ity. The evaluation resulted in the selection of four attributes for estimation; shop, level
change, shelter, and information level.
The approach for collecting information about the chosen attributes was to divide the
transfers into two groups; the 20 largest transfer stations within the Greater Copenhagen
Area and the remaining stations and stops within the area. The 20 largest stations were
examined in great detail in order to collect information on each possible transfer at all
stations. The procedure for the remaining stations and stops was more general but still
kept on a station level to obtain as detailed information about the transfers as possible.
After the data collection, the data was analysed to see how the attributes were actually
distributed in the data and to describe the differences between the observed data and
the generated choice set. Furthermore, in order to include the transfer attributes in the
observed data from TU, additional data processing was required.
The starting point of the estimation of route choice was to estimate Multinomial Logit
models in order to get an overview of the validity of the estimated parameters. However,
the MNL models do not take into account the similarity among routes, why the Path
Size Correction factor was added to the MNL model and all future models. Each of the
four attributes were tested separately in order to ﬁnd the best approach for estimating
the parameters. Following that, the attributes were combined into one model. The ﬁnal
model was furthermore tested divided on purpose and with a reduced choice set. The
results showed that all of the chosen attributes proved signiﬁcant in explaining the trans-
fer penalty. The results, for instance, showed that waiting for a bus without a shelter is
worse than waiting under a large cover, and that having a shop available at a transfer
is perceived positively by the traveller. It was also seen that the information level when
transferring is highly signiﬁcant to the traveller.
The results discovered throughout the thesis can be very useful for future planing and
improvement of terminals. The application of results can be considered in three steps; the
estimated parameters provide general knowledge about transfers in public transport, re-
sults can be implemented for route choice modelling in the Danish National Transport
Model, and the outcome of the route choice model can be used as a tool to improve trans-
fers within public transport network. Hopefully this will increase the number of public
transport users.

Resum´e
Emnet for dette speciale er skift i kollektive rutevalgsmodeller.
Det er vigtigt, at forst˚a de rejsendes adfærd indenfor kollektiv transport for at være
i stand til at forbedre den kollektive transport og dermed forh˚abentlig tiltrække ﬂere
brugere. Rutevalgsmodeller benyttes til at forst˚a de rejsendes adfærd i kollektiv trans-
port. Tidligere studier viser, at mange rejsende ﬁnder skift særligt besværlige n˚ar de
rejser med kollektiv transport.
I dette speciale vil der blive kigget nærmere p˚a den s˚akaldte skiftestraf der angiver hvor
slemt rejsende opfatter et skift indenfor kollektiv transport. Det er forsøgt at opdele
skiftestraffen i mindre dele for at give et mere præcist indblik i, hvilke aspekter af skift
som rejsende ﬁnder ubelejlige. Indtil nu har størstedelen af tidligere studier fokuseret
p˚a rutevalg indenfor unimodale kollektive netværk og kun meget f˚a studier omhandler
multimodale kollektive transportnetværk. I nærværende speciale fokuseres p˚a et multi-
modalt kollektivt netværk i Storkøbenhavn. Grundlaget for specialet bygger p˚a tidligere
studier indenfor kollektive rutevalgsmodeller som har p˚avist eksistensen af en skiftestraf.
Litteraturen, der gennemg˚aes i denne rapport, giver et indblik i hvilke skifteattributter
der tidligere er blevet undersøgt, samt giver inspiration til hvilke skifteattributter der
kunne være interessante at undersøge i dette speciale.
Det observerede data er baseret p˚a direkte præference-data (RP) som er indsamlet gen-
nem Transportvaneundersøgelsen indenfor Storkøbenhavn.
Det kollektive transport-
netværk i Storkøbenhavn best˚ar af intet mindre end 10 forskellige transportformer, hvilket
gør netværket yderst komplekst og et netværk med mange skiftemuligheder. Valgsættet
der er benyttet er genereret i Anderson (2013) og indeholder op imod 200 alternativer for
hvert OD-par.
For at udvælge de endelige attributter er en række forskellige attributter blevet gen-
nemg˚aet og evalueret i forhold til ﬁre kriterier. Først blev der, p˚a baggrund af den gen-
nemg˚aede litteratur, udvalgt 10 attributter: fremtoning, omvej, siddepladser, tryghed,
sikkerhed, butikker, niveau skift, belægning, læskure og informations niveau. Disse ti at-
tributter blev hver især evalueret i forhold til følgende ﬁre kriterier: validitet, p˚alidelighed,
ix
==PAGE:10==
X
m˚albarhed og datatilgængelighed. Dette førte til den endelige udvælgelse af følgende ﬁre
attributter: butikker, niveauskift, læskure og informationsniveau.
To fremgangsm˚ader for at indsamle det nødvendige data blev benyttet; ´en fremgangsm˚ade
for de 20 største skiftestationer i Storkøbenhavn og ´en fremgangsm˚ade for de resterende
stationer og stop i omr˚adet. De 20 største stationer blev gennemg˚aet meget detaljeret for
at sikre at alt nødvendig information omkring hvert enkelt skift p˚a hver enkelt station
blev medtaget. De resterende stationer og stop blev gennemg˚aet p˚a et mere overord-
net, men stadig stationsbaseret, niveau. Det indsamlede data blev analyseret for at ﬁnde
ud af hvorledes attributterne fordelte sig i det observerede data samt i valgsættet. Det
krævede en del databehandling før det var muligt at inkludere den indsamlede informa-
tion vedrørende de valgte attributter i det observerede data samt i valgsættet.
Med det indsamlede data blev en række rutevalgsmodeller estimeret. Som udgangspunkt
estimeredes en MNL model for at veriﬁcere parametrene. Dog tager en MNL model ikke
højde for overlap mellem ruterne, hvorfor en PSC faktor blev tilføjet for de resterende
modeller. Hver af de ﬁre undersøgte attributter blev testet hver for sig for at for at ﬁnde
den bedste og mest præcise m˚ade at inkludere dem p˚a. Herefter blev attributterne kom-
bineret med hinanden en efter en, hvilket resulterede i en endelig model indeholdende
alle ﬁre attributter. Den endelige model blev derefter estimeret for forskellige rejseform˚al
samt testet p˚a et mindre valgsæt. Resultaterne fra den endelige model viser at alle at-
tributter har en indﬂydelse p˚a, hvordan rejsende opfatter et skift. Det ses blandt andet at
det er værre at vente p˚a en bus n˚ar der ikke er et læskur fremfor at vente p˚a en bus, hvor
der er et stort læskur. Ligeledes viser det sig at en kiosk har en positiv indvirkning p˚a
skiftet og at informationsniveauet har stor betydning for rejsende n˚ar de skifter.
De fundne resulter kan anvendes p˚a tre m˚ader; først og fremmest kan de estimerede
resultater forbedre det generelle kendskab til skift i kollektiv transport. Derudover kan
de implementeres i Landstraﬁkmodellen og dermed forbedre resultaterne af nuværende
rutevalgsmodeller. Denne forbedring af eksisterende rutevalgsmodeller kan traﬁkplan-
læggerer benytte ved planlægning af skift i det kollektive transportnetværk. Dette vil
forh˚abentlig øge antallet af brugere af den kollektive transport.

Chapter 1
Introduction
Never before has the focus on sustainable solutions been as substantial as it is now. The
growth and development on earth has led to intensiﬁed search for sustainable fuel alter-
natives and research in sustainable technologies.
In Denmark one third of the CO2 emissions stems from the transport sector and over
the past decade the transport sector has furthermore been the only sector with growing
emissions (Energistyrelsen, 2013). It has therefore been decided by the Danish govern-
ment that the goal is to have a transport sector free of fossil fuel by 2050 (Transportmin-
isteriet, 2014a). One of the four main focus areas, for achieving this, is to move people
from private to public transport modes. Not only will this beneﬁt the environment of
the globe by decreasing the CO2 emissions, it will also positively affect air quality in the
cities and increase the efﬁciency of workers who can focus on other things than driving
on their commute. The question is therefore not whether or not to move travellers to
public transport, the question is rather how to do it.
To most people a public transport trip is perceived a lot more cumbersome than the same
trip would have been by car. This can be changed in two ways - by worsening the utility
that travellers experience from car trips or by making public transport trips more attrac-
tive. The ﬁrst can be done by creating obstacles for car travellers with mobility manage-
ment or road pricing. These suggestions, however, tend to have a negative effect on the
general growth. To obtain a more attractive public transport the ﬁrst step is to consider
why public transport is unattractive. In the research of this transport models are of utter
importance.
In order to attract more travellers to public transport it is important to know which parts
of a public transport trip that creates the unattractiveness and why the travellers choose
the route they do. A route choice model is a tool that helps understanding travel be-
haviour and unveil the preferences of travellers. This knowledge is crucial in order to
explain travellers’ preferences and to predict trafﬁc ﬂows under different scenarios. The
1
==PAGE:16==
2
INTRODUCTION
models can then be used for explaining what initiatives that should be taken in order to
move more travellers to public transport so that the government can reach their 2050 goal.
Transfers in public transport
The present thesis will focus on a route choice model of the multi-modal transport net-
work of the Greater Copenhagen Area. While a growing body of literature has concen-
trated on route choices by public transport users within a given public transport system,
limited efforts have been posed towards multi-modal public transport networks because
of their inherent complexity and challenges (Hoogendoorn-Lanser, 2005).
Focus in the thesis is especially on transfers in public transport route choice models. It
is a well-known fact that transfers cause disutility for travellers and a lot of research has
gone into the so-called transfer penalty. The transfer penalty basically explains that the
disutility experienced from a transfer is greater than the time spent on transferring and
that a two minute transfer is not twice as good as a four minute transfer but rather some-
what the same. Travellers prefer to travel several minutes more in their transport mode in
order to avoid a transfer. Compared to the more easily accessible information concerning
travel and transfer times, detailed information about the attributes of public transport
terminals is more difﬁcult to collect, and the signiﬁcance of these attributes in the route
choices of public transport users is relatively unexplored in the literature. Due to this fact
the planning of stops and stations lack a clear conceptual framework. Burdens related
to transfers receive less attention from transit system managers than the in-vehicle travel
experience (Iseki and Taylor, 2009).
The main purpose of this thesis is to dissect the transfer penalty into smaller separate
parts and thereby be able to explain in detail what is causing the disutility of a transfer.
This will be done through thorough selection of a number of attributes which are thought
to affect the transfer penalty. Existing literature is used as inspiration for the selection of
the attributes.
Overarching purpose
The possible applications of the results of this thesis are threefold. Firstly, it will con-
tribute to the general knowledge of travel behaviour in public transport. Secondly, it
will improve existing route choice models for public transport in which it will be pos-
sible to add more detailed information. Thirdly, and perhaps most importantly, it will
enable transport planners to improve public transport and thereby attract more users.
As mentioned above one of the priorities for the government is to have a fossil fuel free
transport sector by 2050 and one of the means to achieve the goal is to encourage a shift
from private to public transport. In order to do so, detailed knowledge about travellers’
preferences in route choice are vital. Especially the transfer penalty which has so far not
==PAGE:17==
CHAPTER 1
3
received that much attention from researchers and understanding what affects it is there-
fore highly important. In the end this knowledge could be used as a mean for attracting
more travellers by improving transfer facilities cost-efﬁciently. This will contribute not
only to the goal of the Danish government, but also to support a more sustainable devel-
opment in general.
The ofﬁcial learning objectives for this thesis can be found in Appendix A.
1.1
Report Structure
The present thesis is structured in a way that follows the progress of the project chrono-
logically.
In chapter 2 the background of the present thesis is presented. This includes a section
on the theoretical background as well as examples of real cases that further justiﬁes the
project. The chapter contains a review of the existing research concerning transfer penal-
ties.
Chapter 3 concerns the framework of the thesis. Here the geographical context of the
Greater Copenhagen Area is described along with an introduction to the the public trans-
port network. Furthermore, the data foundation is presented along with the ﬁrst prelim-
inary analyses, where some of the differences between the observed data and the choice
set are pointed out.
In chapter 4 a long-list of possible transfer attributes are presented based on the literature
reviewed in chapter 2. Each of the attributes are evaluated with respect to four criteria
and ﬁnally four are selected. Following that, is a deﬁnition of the chosen attributes.
Chapter 5 describes how the registration of the attributes has been carried out. This in-
cludes an example where it is described in detail how the data is collected. Finally, an
analysis of the collected data is conducted to get an overview of how the attributes dis-
tributes themselves.
In chapter 6 the data processing is brieﬂy presented. Due to some data issues a lot of work
was put into aligning the data to make it ready for model estimation.
Chapter 7 presents the result of the thesis. Firstly, the theory behind the models is ex-
plained along with a discussion of a number of route choice models. Secondly, the esti-
mated models are presented, which includes testing of how the new attributes are best
included in the model. The models estimated are MNL and PSC models and further are
the variables tested for heterogeneity. Finally, a discussion of all added parameters is
==PAGE:18==
4
INTRODUCTION
conducted.
In chapter 8 the possible applications of the results are discussed. This includes a general
suggestion to a possible implementation in the Danish National Transport Model and
also how planners could apply the found results.
Chapter 9 presents a discussion of the thesis and sources of error. Also included here is a
suggestion for future work that could be done in continuation of the thesis.
Finally, chapter 10 contains the conclusion.
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2
Background
This chapter will serve as a background for the subject addressed in the present thesis.
A brief introduction to the domain of transfer penalties in public transport will be con-
ducted to underline the importance of the subject. The main idea of this thesis is to dissect
the transfer penalty into more detailed attributes through which it will be possible to ex-
plain in detail why transfer penalties occur. To some extent, attention has already been
brought to these transfer attributes and a literature review in this chapter will present the
preliminary research in the area. To justify the need for estimation of these transfer at-
tributes, three actual cases will be presented where it is clear that changing the layout of
a transfer can lead to a decrease or increase in the number of travellers using the transfer.
The chapter is split in two parts where the ﬁrst will describe the theoretical background
including the newest research and the second will describe relevant actual cases.
2.1
Theoretical foundation
In this section the transfer penalty will be introduced based on the research done in the
ﬁeld. This ﬁrst contains the theoretical basis of what a transfer penalty is and furthermore
a brief introduction to the domain of planned and unplanned transfers. Finally, a review
of the current literature on the area is conducted.
2.1.1
Transfer penalties
In a public transport network travellers perceive the time differently based on how the
time is spent. It is for example well-known that in Denmark, most people prefer travel-
ling by train compared to travelling by bus (Fosgerau, 2007). This can be due to several
factors such as comfort and reliability which are both perceived more inherent to travels
by train. Another known fact is that a transfer in a public transport system does not only
add disutility to the trip proportional to the time spent on the transfer (Nielsen et al.,
2001), but includes a constant disutility part which is only affected by the number of
transfers on the trip. Intuitively it makes sense because a transfer that takes four min-
utes does not incur twice the disutility as a transfer that takes two minutes, but rather
5
==PAGE:20==
6
BACKGROUND
somewhat the same. The disutility rather stems from the inconvenience of alighting and
boarding, which is high especially if the traveller is carrying bags, a baby stroller or a
bike. Furthermore, a transfer entails an increased uncertainty because the ﬁrst or the sec-
ond transport vehicle might have experienced delays. That is what is called the transfer
penalty.
Anderson (2013) presents literature that estimates the transfer penalty scaled to in-vehicle
time (IVT) in route choice models. This can be interpreted as the number of minutes trav-
ellers are willing to spend in a transport mode in order to avoid a transfer (with zero wait
and walk time). In Table 2.1 some selected estimations of transfer penalties are found.
Due to the fact that some of the parameters that are estimated in this thesis concern the
wait time at a transfer, the wait times estimated in the literature is also presented. The
table is based on section 6.1.7 in Anderson (2013).
Table 2.1: Table showing the estimated transfer penalty in different studies.
Estimated by
Comment

Furthermore, it should be mentioned that the Danish National Transport Model has an
estimated parameter of 4 minutes for commuters and business travellers and 6 minutes
for leisure travellers.
Overall it seems fair to state that the time penalty of a transfer correspond to between
5 and 20 minutes of in-vehicle time and that it is generally agreed upon that such penalty
exists. The transfer penalty probably also varies from location to location depending, for
instance, on how the traveller perceive the reliability of the transport system. The wait
time at a transfer location is estimated to be between 0.5 and 2.2 minutes of equivalent
in-vehicle time. This support the assumption that travellers do not perceive an increase
in transfer wait time as much when they have already ”received” the transfer penalty.
The next step in the research of the transfer penalty includes some of the tasks that are
attempted carried out in this thesis. This includes extending the degree of detail that a
==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER 2
7
transfer is described in, so that the transfer is dissected into smaller parts instead of just
being one large bulk in the utility speciﬁcation. In chapter 7 the found aggregated range
of transfer penalties in this thesis will be compared to what is mentioned in Table 2.1.
2.1.2
Planning for transfers
A highly relevant justiﬁcation for uncovering detailed estimation of what parts of a trans-
fer that has the highest value for travellers, is the possible planning aspect. If policy-
makers are aware of the factors that play a role in the route choice of the traveller it is
possible to plan changes at the transfer locations and calculate the impact on travellers
route choice and thereby the loads. This would permit for transport economists to con-
duct a more precise socioeconomic evaluation of investments.
Chowdhury et al. (2013) introduces the concept of planned and unplanned transfers.
The study deﬁned a ’planned’ transfer to be a connection which has been in-
tentionally designed by policy-makers and planners in the planning stage of
the multi-modal PT network development to improve service efﬁciency and
convenience to users. An ’unplanned’ transfer was deﬁned to be a connection
which has been created by the users from available PT services without any
additional guidance from planners and operators on how to make the con-
nection.
(Chowdhury et al., 2013)
Without going into further detail on this subject it is worth considering this division when
planning public transport. The main objective is to deﬁne in a detailed manner how to plan
a transfer. Furthermore, it is of high importance that the planners are able to secure that
the transfers that are planned are actually the transfers that are also being used. To plan a
transfer, an elaboration of what characterize a planned transfer, is necessary:
1. Network integration
2. Integrated timed transfer
3. Integrated physical connection of transfers
4. Information integration
5. Fare and ticketing integration
The ﬁrst type concerns the actual physical network in which a given number of possible
transfers is present. The second type is whether the schedule of the transport mode are
coordinated. The third concerns the physical layout of a transfer whereas number four
evaluate real-time information and maps at transfers. The last is about whether the same
ticket can be used for all systems.
==PAGE:22==
8
BACKGROUND
This project is mainly concerned about number 3, namely how the transfers are laid out,
and what physical attributes should be considered at a transfer location. The fourth type
is also touched upon since the information level at transfers will be assessed. In a way
this project can be said to aim to describe, in a detailed manner, how to obtain integrated
physical connection of transfers.
2.1.3
Studies on transfer attributes
The literature review presented in this section has two overarching purposes; ﬁrstly, it
will serve as an inspiration for which type of attributes that could be relevant to mea-
sure for model estimation, secondly, it will provide an overview of the detailed transfer
attributes that have already been estimated in the literature. The difference between the
two purposes is that several of the papers presented have proposals to what attributes
that are considered relevant for estimation but have only estimated a subset of these. The
model estimations presented here will be compared to the ﬁndings in the present thesis
in chapter 7.
As previously mentioned, not much research has until now been conducted on detailed
transfer attributes in multi-modal networks (Anderson, 2013) and the scale as well as the
degree of detail in this project can be deﬁned as novel.
Iseki and Taylor (2009)
Iseki and Taylor (2009) discuss and argue that walking and waiting during a transfer can
be more or less burdensome and can be perceived differently depending on a number of
attributes. The emphasis of the paper is to understand how transfer attributes inﬂuence
the transfer penalty and thereby enable identiﬁcation of relevant improvements in order
to minimize the transfer penalty. The argumentation and discussion throughout the pa-
per are based on results estimated in previous studies.
The paper points out three main factors contributing to the transfer penalty:
1. Operational factors
2. Physical environment
3. Passenger factors
As operational factors can be mentioned headway and reliability and these are consid-
ered to inﬂuence the wait time. The physical environment is related to appearance, safety,
security, and comfort, which again has an impact on the wait time. Where the operational
factors inﬂuence the actual wait time, the physical environment factors are more related
to the perceived wait time which can vary from traveller to traveller, Iseki and Taylor
(2009) state.
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2
9
The passenger factors are related to the individual passenger and can inﬂuence both
the wait time and the walk time. Walk time can be minimized with the correct infor-
mation level; a passenger, who is familiar with the transfer, and knows the way between
alighting and boarding, is assumed to have a shorter walk time, than a traveller, who
is unfamiliar with the transfer. Whether a passenger is familiar with the facilities at a
transfer inﬂuence the actual walk time, whereas easiness to ﬁnd direction relates to the
perceived walk time, Iseki and Taylor (2009) conclude.
Raveau et al. (2011)
Raveau et al. (2011) consider a route choice model for public transit network in the San-
tiago metro system. Aiming to understand the behaviour of public transit users when
choosing a route, the paper emphasises three main hypotheses.
1. Penalizing routes that deviate from the direct path from O to D.
2. Transit users prefer routes that are better known and more heavily travelled.
3. Other factors than traditional variables have an impact on the travellers choice of
route.
The knowledge of the routes is included as a proxy depending on the number of travellers
on the route. The penalization of routes that deviate from the direct path from origin to
destination is included as a dummy. The paper presents two levels of deviation, where
each level is considered as a dummy:
• Turning back towards origin.
• Turning away from destination.
Other factors, which are considered to possibly have an inﬂuence on the transfer includes:
comfort in vehicle and facilities on stations. Raveau et al. (2011) study the inﬂuence of
level changes including escalators and occupancy rate for transferring mode. For the
occupancy, two levels are deﬁned:
• Less than 15% indicates that the passenger can ride seated.
• Above 85% indicates reduced comfort and small chance of riding seated. Further-
more there is a possibility that the passenger will be unable to board due to high
density.
The data for this study is based on route choices gathered for the metro system in Santi-
ago. The data consist of 28.961 users divided on 1365 OD pairs. The parameter values are
estimated as a Multinomial Logit model (MNL). The results for the third hypothesis re-
garding other factors which could have an impact on a transfer, is presented in Table 2.3.
The additional travel time a passenger is willing to travel in order to avoid the type of
==PAGE:24==
10
BACKGROUND
transfer is estimated. Table 2.2 shows the estimates of all the variables proposed in the
paper scaled to in-vehicle time. The main result from this is that the variables that are
usually used for explaining route choice can be elaborated a lot further by adding more
variables.

The results also showed that the detour factor had an impact on the travellers choice of
route for both levels of deviation. For the level where the traveller felt that the route
turned back towards the origin, the rate of substitution showed that this was perceived
as the same as 4.3 minutes of extra in-vehicle time and the level where the passenger con-
sidered the route to turn away from destination was considered the same as 4.2 minutes
of extra in-vehicle time.
Guo and Wilson (2011)
Guo and Wilson (2011) examine the importance of several transfer attributes in the Lon-
don Underground in order to get a better understanding of the transfer behaviour and
how important transfer behaviour is in public transport.
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2
11
The case studies of this paper are conducted for the London Underground system, which
is one of the largest and and most complex public transport systems in the world. The
data used for the study is from the Rolling Origin and Destination Survey (RODS). Guo
and Wilson (2011) investigate 16 different factors of which ﬁve are included in the ﬁnal
models:
• Escalator availability
• Number of stairs
• Horizontal distance
• Ramp length
• Even transfers
In addition to the above mentioned ﬁve factors Guo and Wilson (2011) also considers fac-
tors such as waiting room, toilets, information, commercials, ticketing halls, overcrowd-
ing, and service reliability but due to lack of information these were not included in the
ﬁnal models
The results regarding transfer times and transfer environment are presented in Table 2.4.

Chowdhury et al. (2013)
As mentioned above (subsection 2.1.1) Chowdhury et al. (2013) examine the importance
of planned and unplanned transfers. Based on a stated preference (SP) survey a case
study was carried out in order to ﬁnd out whether some factors inﬂuence the transfer
behaviour.
==PAGE:26==
12
BACKGROUND
The SP survey was conducted at two stations in Auckland, New Zealand and all partic-
ipants were frequent users or commuters. A planned and an unplanned transfer station
were considered in order to capture the transfer behaviour for both situations. The North-
ern Busway is considered a planned transfer and Britomart is considered an unplanned
transfer. 125 passengers participated in the survey at the Northern Busway and 131 par-
ticipated at the Britomart. In the survey the participants were asked to decide, what type
of factors that were important for a transfer in order to choose to transfer and thereby
save 10 minutes of travel time. The survey considered wait time, walk time, shelters and
information as factors for the transfer. The availability of seats was also considered for
this survey.
The results of the survey showed that the most important attributes for people are travel
time, wait time, and transfer walk times.
Table 2.5: Estimated parameter coefﬁcients for shelter an information attribute (Chowdhury et al.,
2013).

Table 2.5 indicates, that when people were asked whether they would use a transfer that
provides travel time savings, the information level and shelter play a role in whether
they are willing to change their route to include a transfer. The odds ratio indicates that
a traveller is 2-3 times more likely to make a transfer in order to save 10 minutes travel
time if high-quality information is present. The odds ratio for the presence of shelter is
12 and 5 for the two cases respectively.
Raveau et al. (2014)
Raveau et al. (2014) presents a route choice model that examine the variables related to
the transfer experience for the metro system in Santiago and London Underground.
The variables considered in the model are:
• Ascending levels
• Descending levels
• Even levels
• Assisted (made completely using escalator or lift)
• Semi-assisted (made partly using escalator or lift)

two different cities in order to make a comparison between the two. In Santiago 28.961
trips and 1365 OD pairs were considered and in London 17.073 trips and 2127 OD pairs.

Anderson (2013)
Anderson (2013) present behavioural models for passengers route choice within multi-
modal public transport networks based on revealed preference (RP) data collected in the
Greater Copenhagen Area.
In addition to the the traditional transfer attributes (walk time and wait time) Anderson
(2013) examines the travellers’ preference for which transport modes to transfer between.
The attribute for number of transfers is replaced by four new variables:
• Transfers from bus to bus
• Transfers from bus to train
• Transfers from train to bus

• Transfers from train to train

Overview of the estimations
In this section a brief summary of the presented literature will be presented as seen in
Table 2.8. As described along the way, most of the parameters are not measured on the
same data types and the deﬁnitions are not always comparable. The estimates by Guo
and Wilson (2011), for instance, seem rather low, which can be explained by the fact that
they also estimated a station penalty depending on the transfer station. The measurement
methods in Raveau et al. (2011) and Raveau et al. (2014) are comparable but measured for
Santiago Metro and London Underground respectively. As seen, there is no real tendency
in the estimated parameters which is mainly caused by the fact that there is not yet an
agreement on how to measure the variables. Therefore, this table serves as an overview
rather than an actual base for comparison.

2.2
Real cases
The literature review presented in subsection 2.1.3 introduced the papers in the literature
where special attention was brought to the details of transfers. This was done to provide
an introduction to the kind of attributes that are the turning point of the present thesis.
Not only will the detailed transfer attributes add to the accuracy of route choice models
in public transport, it will also be a tool for policy-makers who will be able to make in-
formed decisions about the layout of transfers. Therefore, these actual cases will serve
as background and justiﬁcation for the planning side of the project. The section includes
three examples of real case scenarios, where the aim is to improve the transfer. Taastrup
Station is an example of a completed renovation project where the purpose of the renova-
tion was to improve the functionality and design of the station including the accessibility
for buses and the facilities for the bus stops. Where Taastrup is a completed project the
remaining projects are potential future scenarios, which are not ﬁnalized. Herlev and
Lyngby are both stations for the future light rail and it has in that context been desired to
improve the transfer possibilities at both stations. None of the plans for either Herlev or
Lyngby Station has been ﬁnally decided upon yet.
==PAGE:30==
16
BACKGROUND
2.2.1
Taastrup Station
Taastrup Station is located outside Copenhagen and serve the S-train line B as well as
a number of buses. Until the renovation of Taastrup Station all buses were gathered in
front of the station and the distance between the buses and the train was less than 50
meters. To improve the facilities at the station as well as improving the surrounding area,
a larger renovation of Taastrup Station ﬁnished in May 2012 (DSB, 2012). As a part of the
renovation a new bus terminal opened in August 2012.
The new bus terminal was designed to improve many attributes and facilities for the
buses, such as waiting facilities, information level, parking areas, and access possibili-
ties. Furthermore, the new bus terminal would improve the safety for the passengers
by gathering all the bus stops around one circular waiting area, where passengers could
wait for the buses. However, the new bus terminal was moved 200 meters away from
the station and the distance between the buses and the train increased to 250 meters. In-
formation screens with location of the buses, and countdown functions for the departure
times were put up in order to guide passengers towards the new bus terminal.
Table 2.9 below gives an example on how the facilities for a transfer between train and
bus have changed after the renovation.
Table 2.9: Attributes for transfer between train and bus before and after the renovation.
Before renovation
After renovation
Attributes
Train-bus
Train-bus
Walking distance
50 meters
200-250 meters
Facilities
Before the renovation Taastrup Station
was characterised by old facilities for the

tion
After the renovation all facilities for the
waiting area for the buses were up-
graded and the buses are now located
around a trafﬁc island. Furthermore, the
nearby area has been modernised
Even though the renovation of Taastrup Station resulted in a new and improved bus
terminal where the facilities at the bus stop were improved, it also resulted in a longer
walking distance between train and bus when transferring. To see whether the longer dis-
tance had an effect on the passenger load for the buses a small analysis was conducted.
The analysis only considers the passenger loads for the buses. Passenger load for buses
can either be passengers transferring between two buses or passengers departing from
or arriving to Taastrup Station.
The passenger load will therefore not give a direct result about how the transfer is af-
fected by the new bus terminal. Furthermore, there is no information about whether
passengers are now transferring from another bus stop near Taastrup Station because
they ﬁnd it easier than using the new bus terminal or perhaps the opposite situation.
==PAGE:31==

However, the passenger load for the buses will still give an indication about whether the
new bus terminal has attracted more passengers, which indirectly will indicate whether
the number of passengers transferring at Taastrup Station has been affected.

before and after the renovation of the bus terminal. Movia has provided data about pas-

There is, though, still no guarantee that the decrease in passengers is caused by the in-

however, the initiative does not seem to compensate sufﬁciently for the longer walking
distance.
Taastrup is a very good example of a station, where facilities have been improved in the
hope of attracting more passengers, but with the exact opposite result. The buses at Taas-
trup Station actually have a lower passenger load than before the renovation. A possible
explanation could be the increased distance between bus and train, which is the only fac-
tor that has been aggravated whereas all other factors has been improved.
It is therefore reasonable to assume that some aspects of a transfer has higher importance
than others. In this speciﬁc case it seems like the improved facilities from the renovation
cannot compensate for the longer walking distance between the transport modes.
2.2.2
Herlev Station
Another example is at Herlev Station where the planned light rail will have a stop. The
light rail, which will be ﬁnished in 2021, is planned in a circular corridor around Copen-
hagen which will connect several of the radial train lines in the Greater Copenhagen
Area. The light rail will have three stops within the city of Herlev. One of these will be
Herlev Station which make up for one of the centres of the light rail, where it connects
to the buses and S-trains that Herlev Station is already serving (Hovedstadens Letbane).
The light rail stop near Herlev Station will be placed at the Ringvejs-bridge (circular road
bridge), which crosses the rails for the S-trains approximately 300 meters from Herlev
Station. Figure 2.1 shows the distance from the platform to the bus stop at the Ringvejs-

bridge, where the new light rail stop will be located as well.

approximately 300 meters away.

at the Ringvejs-bridge. As the situation is today the distance between Herlev Station
and the Ringvejs-bridge is, as mentioned, approximately 300 meters. This long distance
between two stops has a tendency to complicate the transfer seen from the passengers
perspective. The facilities for the transfer from S-train to the bus at the Ringvejs-bridge

2.2.3
Lyngby Station
Lyngby Station is also one of the main stations for the new light rail. Lyngby Station is
serving two S-train lines (A and E) and a number of buses, which makes Lyngby a large
station with many possible transfers. When the light rail is ﬁnished in 2021, it will start
in Lundtofte in the outskirts of Lyngby and have an additional ﬁve stops in Lyngby. One
of these stops will be at Lyngby Station, where there will be an opportunity to transfer to
other transport modes. It is expected that Lyngby Station will be one of the largest light
rail stations, why it will be important to locate the light rail stop as convenient as possible
in order to ease the transfer to other transport modes (Hovedstadens Letbane).
As Figure 2.2 indicates, the light rail stop is planned to be located north of the station, on
the same side of the station as the buses (marked with red on Figure 2.2). Furthermore, a
new access to the S-train platform will be build in order to shorten the walking distance
between light rail and S-trains. This location of the light rail will secure the best possible
access/egress and transfer conditions (Ringby-Letbanesamarbejdet, 2013).
==PAGE:35==

the current access to the station whereas the red line mark where the future light rail stop is
planned to be located and the planned new access ways.
2.3
Summary of the chapter
The purpose of this chapter was to provide a nuanced perspective on the background
for the present thesis. The focus was put on the two-sided use of the estimation results
that will be found in the project. Firstly, it will add to the current research on transfer
attributes which is not overwhelming when it comes to multi-modal route choice mod-
els. Secondly, the estimations can play a role in the policy-makers knowledge about the
factors that inﬂuence the route choices of public transport users.
The literature review presented an overview of the existing literature in the area and the
estimation results from these papers were presented. Only Anderson (2013) considers
multi-modal networks which will be worked with in this thesis, whereas the remaining
papers consider uni-modal networks. In chapter 7 a comparison between the ﬁndings in
this thesis and the model estimations presented above will be made.
The second part of this chapter presented a number of real case scenarios that to some
extend deal with the issue of transfers and how to make a transfer as attractive and easy
as possible. Two cases from the light rail project have been presented, both cases about
central stations of the new light rail, where the transfers are taken into account when
designing and locating the new light rail stations. Herlev and Lyngby are both future
scenarios, where the aim is to optimize and improve the stations in order to simplify
==PAGE:36==
22
BACKGROUND
transfers and attract more passengers. Where the project at Herlev Station includes a
major renovation of the station by moving it closer to the Ringvejs-bridge, the project at
Lyngby Station does not require the same level of rebuilding. In both cases, the main goal
is to make transfers less complicated since both stations will be central to the new light
rail. At Herlev Station the distance between the station and the Ringvejs-bridge and the
upcoming light rail stop has a negative effect on the transfer conditions, why it is con-
sidered to move the station. At Lyngby Station it is less demanding and simply implies
to ﬁnd the most optimal location for the light rail stop including smaller renovations in
order to optimize the transfer conditions such as information and distance.
Another example is from Taastrup Station and gives a clear idea about how some trans-
fer attributes have a great inﬂuence on travellers choice of route and others do not. It is
very clear that the renovation of Taastrup Station had en negative inﬂuence on the direct
transfer between buses and S-trains. This indicates that it is reasonable to assume that the
walking distance as well as the information level has an inﬂuence on passengers choice
of route
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 3
Geographical context and data
foundation
This chapter will ﬁrst introduce the geographical context which this thesis is built on. A
brief overview of the area will be provided along with a description of the public trans-
port network found in the region. Included in this is a presentation of transfer possibili-
ties along with typical routes choices involving different kinds of transfers.
The ﬁrst part of the chapter seeks to provide the reader with an overview of the different
public transport systems in the Greater Copenhagen Area and how they are connected.
Following that, the focus of the chapter will move from considering the geographical as-
pects to considering the network which is representing the area, including how transfers
are constructed. After the context of the network has been described, the focus will move
to the data of this speciﬁc thesis. Here the existing data is described including an ex-
planation of how it was collected, map-matched, and ﬁnally, how the alternatives of the
choice set were generated. The structure of the chapter should therefore be considered as
an overview including the geographical context followed by a description of the speciﬁc
network used in the thesis, and ﬁnally, provides a closer view on the data foundation of
the thesis.
3.1
Geographical context
The data in this thesis comes from data collected in an area called the Greater Copen-
hagen Area (GCA). The borders of the region do not coincide with the borders of the po-
litically determined capital region (Region Hovedstaden) of Denmark. Instead it consists
of the three former counties Frederiksborg Amt, Københavns Amt, and Roskilde Amt.
When the municipal reform was implemented in 2007 (Økonomi- og Indenrigsminis-
teriet) the counties were abandoned and instead regions were created. Due to this change
the GCA today covers the political capital region except Bornholm as well as the six mu-
nicipalities of Roskilde, Køge, Solrød, Greve, Lejre, and Stevns. The Greater Copenhagen
23
==PAGE:38==
24
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
Area has a total area of approximately 2,800 km2 and inhabits 1,970,076 people. That
is 6.5 % of the total area and 35 % of the entire population in Denmark. Evidently, the
Greater Copenhagen Area is very densely populated, however, half of the population is
concentrated in the city of Copenhagen. This means that some parts of the GCA can be
categorized as rural and the variety in types of trips in the area is therefore great. It is
also the most complex area of the country regarding public transport as well as the area
with the most daily passengers which makes it suitable for a large scale model. Figure 3.2
show the area that is covered by the data used in this thesis.
The Greater Copenhagen Area was planned after the famous Fingerplan suggested in
1947, see Figure 3.1. The idea of this plan was to create urban development in ﬁngers
departing from Copenhagen. Between each of the ﬁngers it was decided to have recre-
ational areas, and several of today’s green areas in the GCA were planned in connection
to the Fingerplan. This type of urban planning was also meant to prevent the city from
growing as belts around itself and thereby grow completely without green areas. The
ends of the ﬁve ﬁngers are the ﬁve bigger cities in the Greater Copenhagen Area, namely
Roskilde, Køge, Frederikssund, Farum, and Hillerød. Later on, a sixth ﬁnger was at-
tached, namely the ﬁnger to Helsingør. Each of the six ﬁngers are served with both trains
and highways as it was planned originally. As it is today, these corridors somewhat con-
stitutes the built area in the Greater Copenhagen Area.
Figure 3.1: The original Fingerplan as it was laid out in 1947 (www.dn.dk).
==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER 3
25
Figure 3.2: Greater Copenhagen Area where major roads and railroads are shown.
As described the main corridors in the Greater Copenhagen Area are radial, going out
from Copenhagen. In order to facilitate trips from one radial corridor to another, several
circular roads have also been build. As for the public transport only one circular train
route has been built which is located rather close to the Copenhagen centre and high fre-
quent express buses serve the circular roads further away. This creates a general problem
==PAGE:40==
26
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
for people travelling by public transport between ﬁngers - should they go by train into
the center and out again, or should they rather take the express bus that cuts across be-
tween the ﬁngers? An example of this dilemma will be presented later. On Figure 3.2
a map of the Greater Copenhagen Area is shown, including all train lines and the ma-
jor roads. It is clear that the Fingerplan has played a signiﬁcant role in the location of
built area. Although it can be seen that in the area closest to Copenhagen, the ﬁngers are
less distinct which indicates that these areas have been built out despite the Fingerplan.
The presence of houses and workplaces in areas like these increases the need for public
transport on locations where it was initially not intended.
3.1.1
Transport modes in the GCA
The transport modes in the Greater Copenhagen Area can be grouped into ten different
types depending on their frequency and where they run. The different types of mode can
be seen in Table 3.1. Furthermore, a map showing the buses and the trains in the area can
be found on Figure 3.3.
Figure 3.3: Show the public transport network within the Greater Copenhagen Area.
Due to the many different modes, a rather high number of transfers are available in the
Greater Copenhagen Area. As mentioned in Table 3.1, regular buses are found even in
the most rural areas of the region. These are also the only kind of buses that drives
within towns that are smaller than the main cities. The S-buses have intercity routes
==PAGE:41==
CHAPTER 3
27
and these are typically either radial or circular, whereas the A-buses are high-frequent
buses with short distance between stops. There are ﬁve different train systems. The re-
gional trains and the IC-trains usually run along the same corridors with the exception
of the regional train running on the east coast north of Copenhagen which also connects
to Sweden. The S-trains represent the ﬁngers quite well except, that there is no S-train
to Helsingør and the one heading towards Roskilde terminates at Høje Taastrup Station.
Furthermore, there is an S-train, the so-called F-line, that constitutes the only train not
radial on Copenhagen. Besides the S-trains, the central Copenhagen is also serviced by
a metro which runs through the city and continues to the airport. In the Greater Copen-
hagen Area ﬁve local trains are found and these are mainly located in the very north of
the region connecting to larger stations such as Hillerød and Helsingør. Lastly, Table 3.1
lists the harbour buses, that are found in the center of Copenhagen crossing over the har-
bour. Evidently, the public transport network of the Greater Copenhagen Area is rather
complex and a large amount of transfers are possible and meaningful to make. In the
following the possible transfers between modes will be listed with a description of where
in the network these transfers exist.
