3.1.2
Transfers
This section constitutes a description of the transfers that are found in the GCA and how
the different train and bus systems interact with one another. This is done in order to
provide an overview of the network and how it is being used. A schematic view of the
train system in the GCA can be seen on Figure 3.4. There are several transfers available
that serves as transfers between different train systems as well as within each system.
==PAGE:42==
28
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
Figure 3.4: Schematic map showing all train lines in the Greater Copenhagen Area (www.dsb.dk).
The regional trains and the IC trains do not have that many transfer possibilities within
their own systesm. A transfer would mainly take place in order to change to or from a
service with more stops along the way. The S-trains on the other hand have a lot of possi-
ble transfers but not all of them are meaningful. This is the case for the stops in the centre
of Copenhagen (Svanemøllen, Nordhavn, Østerport, Nørreport, Vesterport, Copenhagen
Central, Dybbølsbro) where most of the lines share the same stops. However, travellers
coming into Copenhagen on one ﬁnger and leaving on another, might transfer there. The
F-train (the yellow line on Figure 3.4) has several meaningful and used stops that encour-
age changes within the S-train system. These stops are:
• Hellerup (changes to C, E and B and to regional trains)
• Ryparken (changes to A and H)
==PAGE:43==
CHAPTER 3
29
• Flintholm (changes to C and H and to the two metro lines)
• Ny Ellebjerg (changes to A and E)
A transfer within the S-train system is typically characterized as a rather easy transfer
where the platforms are located close to one another. Furthermore, the S-train has sev-
eral stops that connect the system to other train systems. Below is a list showing which
stations provide what transfers to which systems:
• Transfer stations between regional/IC trains and S-trains
– Nørreport Station
– Copenhagen Central Station
– Østerport Station
– Valby Station
– Hellerup Station
– Høje Taastrup Station
– Klampenborg Station
– Køge Station
• Transfer stations between metro and S-trains
– Nørreport Station
– Flintholm Station
– Vanløse Station
• Transfer stations between local trains and S-trains
– Hillerød Station
– Jægersborg Station
– Køge Station
– Ølby Station
The transfers between S-trains and regional/IC trains can most often be done with the
same ease as if it had been a transfer within the system. On the other hand the three
transfers between S-trains and the metro are slightly different due to the fact that the
two systems are not entirely integrated because of the difference in time of construction.
Transfers between local trains and S-trains are often characterized by not having any
level changes. This is caused by the fact that the local trains are mainly connected to end-
stations of the S-train lines.
Although the S-train network is the dominant network in the Greater Copenhagen Area,
==PAGE:44==
30
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
there are also a few other transfers that are possible (and meaningful) between the other
train systems. The types of transfers as well as the station at which they are present are
listed below:
• Transfer stations between regional/IC trains and local trains
– Helsingør Station
– Roskilde Station
• Transfer stations between regional/IC trains and metro
– Nørreport Station
– Ørestad Station
– Copenhagen Airport
These transfers are characterized by the fact that the change is between two different train
systems.
Finally, a high number of buses cover the entire area of Greater Copenhagen and es-
pecially the city is covered by a dense grid of bus lines including the A-buses as seen on
Figure 3.3. The transfer between buses and one of the train systems are in general rather
cumbersome due to the fact that it in most cases will contain a level change and that bus
stops are not always easily found.
To sum up, the complex public transport network of Greater Copenhagen provide plenty
of possibilities for trips with transfers. In many situations a transfer can shorten the total
length of the trip and it is therefore worth considering several choices.
Transfer example
An example of a trip in the network where a transfer could shorten the length of the trip
could be a trip between Vanløse Station and Emdrup Station. There are three options on
how this trip could be performed which are listed below.
1. The H-line of the S-train network directly. 26 minutes with 0 transfers.
2. The C-line for one stop to Flintholm Station, then the F-line between Flintholm Sta-
tion and Ryparken Stations and ﬁnally the H-line from Ryparken Station to Emdrup
Station. 19 minutes with 2 transfers.
3. The metro to Flintholm Station and then bus 21 to Emdrup Station. 21 minutes with
1 transfer
On Figure 3.5 the three possibilities are shown and it is clear that with the information
given it would be hard to determine which route most people would choose. This is
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 3
31
due to the well-known fact that transfers are perceived inconvenient and most people
would prefer to avoid one. On the other hand, the route without transfer is substantially
longer than both the routes that include transfers. Relating it to this project it could also
be worth to consider the types of transfers that are included. In route 1 the two transfers
both require two level changes (one ascend and one descend) whereas the transfer in
route 3 only require one descend. It is hard to guess what kind of inﬂuence that these
factors would have on the route choice, but on the other hand it is quite certain that the
number of transfers and the design of the transfers will play a role.
Figure 3.5: Map showing three possible routes when travelling from Vanløse Station to Emdrup
Station.
3.2
Network speciﬁcation
In the present section a brief introduction to the network, from which the data is built
upon, will be given. After having provided an overview of the geographical context in
the previous section, this section aims to place the model network in that context. This
will be done with a description of multi-modal trips followed by a brief introduction to
the network at hand.
==PAGE:46==
32
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
3.2.1
Multi-modal trips
The network used is a so-called multi-modal network that contains multi-modal trips. A
multi-modal trip is deﬁned as a trip with at least one transfer between two mechanized
modes (Hoogendoorn-Lanser, 2005). By this deﬁnition a trip that includes a walk as
access and then only one mode of transportation will not be considered multi-modal but
instead uni-modal. A multi-modal network by deﬁnition has to both consider public
and private transport modes due to the fact that access and egress modes relating to trips
using public transport are private. This induces that networks used for multi-modal trips
are often rather complex with large amount of links and nodes (Anderson, 2013). This
is also because public transport services are characterized by having different schedules
and stop patterns that can vary between runs and time of day. In multi-modal networks
there is also a great attention towards journeys or tours that are comprised of several
trips (Hoogendoorn-Lanser, 2005). In this thesis, no attention will be paid to this since
the main focus is on transfers independent of journeys. The data used in this thesis is
based on the data collected in Anderson (2013) and car as an access mode is not taken into
account. If a car is used as an access mode, the route choice is probably more dependent
on the road network than on the public transport network.
3.2.2
Network
As described in the above the network considered in this model is based on the geo-
graphical area of Greater Copenhagen. This project has mainly been concerned with the
estimation of attributes regarding transfers and the result have yet to be implemented
in the network. On the other hand, the network was both used when the observed data
was map-matched and when the choice set was generated. In the following, section the
network and how it is built will brieﬂy be introduced. When transfers are performed
so-called transfer edges are used and since these are of special interest a thorough intro-
duction to them can be found below.
Features
The network that was used is the Danish National Transport Model (NTM) which cov-
ers the entire country as well as parts of Europe. In this case only the part of the model
that covers the Greater Copenhagen Area was considered. The model has a number of
nodes that each represent a train station or a group of bus stops. Bus and train lines are
represented in the model as lines which each has a number of line variants related to
them. This means that a line represents a certain bus or train line in the public transport
network and the line variants then represent variations of the given line. These varia-
tions can differ in stop patterns, frequency and/or terminal stops. In practice one edge
between two stops can therefore consist of several lines (if more trains or buses serve the
same stretch) and possibly a very high number of line variants.
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 3
33
The network is schedule based which is not important when estimating the variables
as it will be done in this thesis, but has had an inﬂuence on the choice set generation
as well as the map matching. The network does neither consider capacity restraints nor
does it consider delays in the system.
Due to the main focus on transfers this section will thoroughly introduce how transfers
are used in the network. Each time a transfer between two modes is possible and mean-
ingful a transfer edge is found in the network. These edges represent transfers, often
done walking. It should be noted that only meaningful transfers have transfer edges. If a
bus runs between two stops a transfer edge between these two stops will not be present
if the travel time is shorter with the bus. The time that is spent walking the distance of
the transfer edge is an attribute of the edge. The distance between two stops is in general
rather short and often affected by the topology of a station. Often one has to walk to the
end of the platform in order to get to the staircase that will lead to another platform. Be-
cause of this, the length of a transfer edge cannot be used to determine the time spent on
it, and the transfer times are therefore considered separately. The data describing these
edges are found in the network of the Danish National Transport Model which is used in
this thesis. The travel time is then associated with each change edge as an attribute and
is used by the model to compute the travel time. Due to the fact that the network does
not consider delays, the waiting time at a stop will always equal the time between the
scheduled arrival of an incoming transport mode and the departure time of the follow-
ing transport mode subtracted the transfer time. This implies that it is not possible for a
traveller to minimize transfer time by running in situations where he/she is aware that
the transfer is tight. Furthermore, the model works in a way where it is assumed that
the traveller will always do the walk part of the transfer before the wait part, ie. the wait
time will always be spent where the next transport vehicle will depart from. This fact is
also used in the registration of the data. This thesis will present how more attributes for a
transfer edge would have to be added in order to accommodate the additional informa-
tion about transfers.
3.3
Existing data
After having provided an overview of the geographical context followed by a brief intro-
duction of the network, this section seeks to zoom further in on the data foundation. The
existing data that the models in this thesis are build upon will be presented here. There
are two parts of this; the map-matching of the data and the generation of the choice set.
In order to estimate a route choice model both the chosen routes and a number of alter-
natives have to be known to the modeller. The data collection and the map-matching of
the observed routes as well as the choice set generation was done in Behavioural Models
for Route Choice of Passengers in Multimodal Public Transport Networks (Anderson, 2013).
Firstly, the observed data will be presented including a brief summary of how it was col-
==PAGE:48==
34
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
lected. Here, a few routes actually carried out will also be illustrated. Secondly, the choice
set will be introduced including a presentation of the choice set generation method. Fi-
nally, a preliminary data analysis of the choice set data and the observed data respectively
is conducted.
3.3.1
Observed data
The data used in this thesis was collected through the TU survey (Transportvaneun-
dersøgelsen). The TU survey is a national on-going collection of travel diaries. The
socioeconomic background of the respondents is also described and based on this, the
respondents are given a weight in order to represent the entire population (Christansen
and Skougaard, 2015). The TU survey is unique for Denmark due to its detailed informa-
tion about the trips that are performed, including coordinates of the destinations for all
trips, the quality and amount of the collected data and the fact that the data collection is
continuous (Anderson, 2013). Especially regarding public transport the level and amount
of information about performed trips are extremely high.
The observed data was collected from the TU survey between February 2009 and May
2010. To have the respondents describe their public transport trips in detail was a new
addition to the TU survey which is thoroughly described in Anderson (2013). In the sur-
vey the respondents both had to ﬁll out their origin, the modes they travelled with, their
access and egress modes and their destination. For each of the modes they were asked to
speciﬁcally describe the line number of the bus or train they travelled with as well as ﬁll
out the time they spent on each activity.
In this thesis a lot of attention is paid to transfers and it will be registered whether there
is a shop (or an entire shopping centre) at a transfer location. It should although be noted
that the TU survey does not in general consider it as one contiguous trip if a visit has
been paid to a shop. The only exception to this is if what is bought in the shop is meant
for a person at the trip destination, such as ﬂowers for someone hosting a dinner party.
This can imply that the signiﬁcance of the presence of a shop at a transfer location will be
hard to evaluate. It will, however, be included due to the fact that the opportunity to go
into a shop can play a role in the choice of route.
Map-matching observed routes to the network
In order to use the observed routes as the chosen route in a model, the data must be
matched to the network. This was done with the data at hand in Anderson (2013) where
an algorithm was used to match the answers from the TU questionnaire to the GIS net-
work described in section 3.1. The map-matching was done using an algorithm from
Anderson (2010) that could:
• Identify the stations used.
==PAGE:49==
CHAPTER 3
35
• Identify the bus stops used.
• Map observations onto the relevant links with knowledge of lines used and points
travelled through.
This was done by determining a set of assumptions that will not be described in detail in
this thesis. These assumption include that the traveller will always choose the stop clos-
est to the origin/destination to board/alight a public transport line stated. Furthermore,
it was assumed that travellers that transfer between two modes will always board/alight,
for instance, at the bus stop closest to the station. As described in subsection 3.2.2 these
are moreover the only stops with transfer edges. A thorough description of how the al-
gorithm was developed and implemented on the data is described and carried out in
Matching observed public route choice data to a GIS network (Anderson, 2010). Further explo-
ration of map-matching is out of the scope of the present thesis and will not be touched
upon any further. The main purpose in this thesis is to make use of the data found by
map-matching.
To provide an example of how the data can be visualised in ArcMap after the map-
matching of the routes, a few routes with The Technical University of Denmark (DTU)
as origin, are shown on Figure 3.6. The map to the the left shows four routes that go
from DTU to the northern part of Copenhagen/Frederiksberg. Here it is clear, that some
travellers choose to take the direct bus along the highways, whereas other choose to go
to Lyngby Station to catch a train. On the right hand map all three routes terminate on
the island of Amager and it is obvious that the choice is once again between catching the
bus along the highway or going to Lyngby Station and then board a train from there. The
example shown presents the type of data that the framework is build upon which will
not be explained in further details.
3.3.2
Choice set
This section will brieﬂy introduce the purpose of choice set in route choice models as well
as present the issues related to the generation of choice sets. Furthermore, the method
that was used for choice set generation for the existing data will be touched upon but
mainly the focus will be on analysing the choice set at hand which builds upon the choice
set generated in Anderson (2013). Choice set generation as a ﬁeld of research is rather
large and the theories of it is not within the scope of the present thesis.
In route choices, choice sets represent the set of routes perceived available to the indi-
vidual, from which he or she can choose from to reach the destination. In theory an
individual can choose from an inﬁnite number of routes to reach a destination. But since
it is not realistic that all routes are considered, a lot of research has been done in the gen-
eration of choice sets. The choice of a route entails a much higher complexity than for
example choice of mode. This is due to the fact that routes are harder to deﬁne, and there
==PAGE:50==
36
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
Figure 3.6: Maps showing a selection of map-matched routes between DTU and Copenhagen.
are often a high number of different routes that are considered. Because of this the choice
set generation needs to be done in a separate model before the route choice assignment
begins (Prato, 2009). It can although be discussed what the size of a suitable choice set
is. The actual choice set of a traveller is a subset of the universal choice set and is the
so-called consideration set (Bovy, 2009). According to Bovy (2009) this set does not have to
include all relevant alternatives as long as it is well-sampled. This argument does, how-
ever, only seem to hold when MNL or other modiﬁcations are estimated (Prato, 2009).
A number of techniques for choice set generation have been tested by Bovy (2009) and
Prato (2009). These include deterministic shortest path-based methods such as link elim-
ination and link penalty that both ensure dissimilarities between the routes in the choice
set. The problem that raises is that if a link is a part of a lot of feasible routes, some
of these might not be added to the choice set due to the high impedance or eliminated
links. On the other hand the chosen links still stay in the network and adding dissimi-
lar routes to the choice set is encouraged. Constrained enumeration methods provides a
subset of the universal choice set which is enumerated while being subject to a number
of thresholds (Prato and Bekhor (2006) in Prato (2009)). The method usually contains ﬁve
rules; a directional threshold, a temporal threshold, a loop threshold, a similarity thresh-
old, and a movement constraint. These behavioural thresholds ensure that not too many
unattractive routes are included in the choice set. However, it can not be determined
==PAGE:51==
CHAPTER 3
37
whether all relevant routes are generated and the threshold has not yet been subject to
thorough sensitivity analyses (Prato, 2009). A third group of methods for choice set gen-
eration is the stochastic shortest path-based methods that are based on repeated searches
for the shortest path, where impedances are randomly drawn from a distribution. This
can be done by varying the link impedance in a Monte Carlo simulation or in a doubly
stochastic generation function. The latter was what was used to generate the choice set
at hand. The doubly stochastic generation assumes that not only do travellers perceive
path costs differently, they are also heterogeneous in their perceptions. The method is a
schedule-based stochastic transit assignment model presented by Nielsen and Frederik-
sen (2006). The advantage of the model is that the generated alternatives are ensured to
be heterogeneous and it is computationally efﬁcient (Prato, 2009). On the other hand the
method could produce irrelevant and unrealistic alternative paths.
The choice set in this thesis was build on the observed routes described in subsection 3.3.1.
For each of the observed routes a number alternatives were generated in order to make
the route choice a discrete choice. The method used for generating choice in the data used
in this thesis is described in Anderson (2013) and can be stated as Equation 3.1 where both
an error term and en error component are present:
Ui = ∑
j
(βj + ξj)tij + εi
(3.1)
where Ui is the utility of alternative i, βj is the the coefﬁcient that expresses the individ-
ual’s preference for a certain time component, ξj is the variation of these preferences, tij
are the time components of alternative i perceived by individual j, and εi is the error term
related to the alternative. Equation 3.1 explains the utility on link level and for an entire
path the utility will be the sum of the contained links. For a single individual the utility
of an alternative is deﬁned as:
Ui =βwalktime · TTwalktime,i + βwaittime · TTwaittime,i + βtrans f erpen · TTtrans f erpen,i
+ βconntime · TTconntime,i + βzonewait · TTzonewait,i + βIVT,train · TTIVT,train,i
+ βIVT,ICtrain · TTIVT,ICtrain,i + βIVT,Strain · TTIVT,Strain,i
+ βIVT,bus · TTIVT,bus,i + βIVT,metro · TTIVT,metro,i
(3.2)
where βwalktime is the value of time of walking to the public transport mode, βwaittime is the
value of time of waiting for the public transport vehicle,βtrans f erpen is the penalty added
for each transfer, βconntime is the value of time access and egress to and from the pub-
lic transport mode, βzonewait is the value of time of waiting at the origin before leaving,
βIVT,train is the value of in-vehicle time for regional and local trains, βIVT,ICtrain is the
value of in-vehicle time for IC trains, βIVT,Strain is the value of in-vehicle time for S-trains,
βIVT,bus is the value of in-vehicle time for bus, and βIVT,metro is the value of in-vehicle
time for metro. Anderson (2013) explores 18 scenarios of variation of the doubly stochas-
tic path generation algorithm both with error components, error term, and both. The one
==PAGE:52==
38
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
that performs the best in terms of coverage of the observed trips is with both error term
and error component and with highest variation. The error components are drawn from
a log-normal distribution whereas the error term is drawn from a Gamma distribution.
The reason that a Gamma distribution is used for the error term is that the utility of using
a link will never become positive which would not comply with microeconomic theory.
For the same reason a log-normal distribution is chosen to represent the heterogeneity in
the population, because it is assumed that nobody would have a positive marginal utility
of neither time nor cost.
Anderson (2013) mentions how the chosen method for the choice set generation adds
routes that are not necessarily likely to be chosen by the traveller. But the presence of
these routes in the choice set implies which attributes the traveller ﬁnds important and
which preferences the traveller has for different routes. If all the routes in the choice set
are very similar to the optimal route the traveller preferences will be harder to determine
due to the fact that the reason for choosing the observed routes would only be based on
small differences. In subsection 7.2.3 of the present thesis a model will be tested where
the choice set is reduced in order to test the importance of the choice set size.
Analysis of choice set
The choice set developed from Anderson (2013) has been generated with 200 iterations
of the choice set for each of the observed routes. Following that, the routes that were not
unique or coincided with the chosen route were removed from the choice set. The lowest
number iterations in an OD-pair is 18 and the highest is 200. The distribution can be
seen on Figure 3.7 where it is noted that not many OD-pairs have less than 50 iterations.
Because of the method for the choice set generation, several of the routes in the choice
set are rather meaningless and would not appear in the consideration set of the traveller.
There are examples of trips in the choice set that moves back and forth on the same train
line passing the same stations several times. This also leads to a high amount of transfers
in the choice set compared to the observed data which will be described further below.
The assumption here is, as previously mentioned, that irrelevant routes in the choice set
will not affect the estimation results, but only provide variance in the data (Bovy, 2009).
However, the ﬁnal model will be tested with a smaller choice set to test the assumption.
There are also examples of trips that moves outside the Greater Copenhagen Area which
creates complication for the data collection in the present thesis. Due to the fact that this
project focuses on collecting highly detailed data for transfers, all possible transfers in the
choice set has to be registered. The approach of the data collection is explained i chap-
ter 5 where it will be evident that including the transfers outside the Greater Copenhagen
Area would not be straightforward. On Figure 3.8 it can be seen how some of the routes
in the generated choice set pass stops that are quite far away from the Greater Copen-
hagen Area. It should, furthermore, be noted that none of the trips in the observed data
moved outside the GCA.
==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 3
39
Figure 3.7: Distribution of OD-pairs with a given number of iterations in the choice set.
The way that this issue was dealt with was to consider the percentage of iterations in an
OD-pair that included trips with transfers outside GCA. It can be problematic if a high
number is affected because it can rule out a group of possible routes between the origin
and the destination and therefore distort the preferences. To avoid this issue, it was de-
cided that for OD-pairs where more than 20 % of the iterations were affected, the entire
OD-pair would be removed. This only amounted to a total of 13 OD-pairs. For the re-
maining trips that had transfers outside the GCA were similarly removed from the choice
set. This amounted to a total of 7559 trips divided on 2701 different OD-pairs. The max-
imum number of iterations removed from an OD-pair was 33, but as mentioned above,
it was ensured that this number was less than 20 % of the iterations within an OD-pair.
On average the number of iterations removed from an OD-pair was 2.8 iterations and the
95th quartile was 11.
It should be noted that this did not affect the choice set a lot, it was rather a way to
ensure that all data registration regarding transfers would be as detailed as possible. On
Table 3.2 it is seen how the two choice sets are rather similar.
Table 3.2: Distribution of number of alternatives for each OD-pair before and after removing trips
outside the GCA.

GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
Figure 3.8: Stops outside the Greater Copenhagen Area which are passed or transferred at in the
choice set.
Of course it could be argued that some routes might not have been entirely irrelevant if
the transfer was placed close to the border of the Greater Copenhagen Area. With the
method used to remove the trips from the choice set it is, though, expected that this will
not be an issue. Furthermore it should be emphasised that the time constraint on the
present project did not allow to collect data for transfers outside the Greater Copenhagen
Area. In chapter 7 in this report a number of models will be estimated and the above
described choice set will be used which is why it is considered part of the data foundation
for the thesis.
==PAGE:55==

3.3.3
Transfers in the observed data and in the choice set
The main focus of this thesis is on transfers in a public transport network and this short
section will therefore present the difference in the number of transfers between the ob-
served data set and the generated choice set.
Figure 3.9: Distribution of number of transfers in the choice set. The maximum number of trans-
fers on a trip is 196.
Figure 3.10: Distribution of number of transfers in the observed data. The maximum number of
transfers on a trip is 4.
As previously described, some of the trips in the choice set can seem meaningless. This is
because the variation of the time components can cause that the cost of a transfer is almost
zero and therefore unimportant to travellers. Furthermore, up to 200 routes between the
same origin and destination will inherently cause some unrealistic routes. As seen on
Figure 3.9 the maximum number of transfers on a single trip in the choice set is 196.
It should although be noted that 95 % of the trips have less than 27 transfers. These
numbers are in striking contrast to the number of transfers per trip in the observed data
==PAGE:56==
42
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
(see Figure 3.10) where the maximum is 4 and that is just one single trip. Another notable
difference is the share of trips without transfers. This number is 12 % for the choice and
an astounding 60 % for the observed data.
3.4
Summary of the chapter
In this chapter the framework of the report was presented. The structure of the chapter
was to ﬁrst introduce the overall geographical context of the thesis followed by a brief
description of the network used in the thesis. Zooming in even further, the data foun-
dation of the thesis was presented as well. The last part also included some preliminary
data analysis of both the observed data and the choice set.
The ﬁrst part introduced the public transport network in the Greater Copenhagen Area
which is build on the basis of the so-called Fingerplan (Figure 3.1) which was laid out
in 1947. The plan ensures that the infrastructure is build in radial transport corridors
going out from Copenhagen. This structure of the network provides a high number of
choices when travelling from one location to another especially in areas where housing
is constructed even though it was not intended initially. Furthermore, the mode types of
the area was presented in order to provide an overview of the system. With this knowl-
edge the transfer options for the train systems were mentioned and a ﬁctional example of
three possible routes between Vanløse Station and Emdrup Station were presented. This
showed how the transfer attributes could be expected to play a role in the route choice.
Following that, a brief introduction to the network was given. This mainly served as
background knowledge since it is not within the scope of this thesis to test the estimated
parameters on the network. It is, though, important to know how the network is built to
be able to collect data that matches the network.
In subsection 3.3.1 the observed data was presented as well as an introduction to how
the map-matching was done with the data in this thesis. The map-matching based itself
on an algorithm that, with the use of several assumptions on travel behaviour, was able
to connect the trips from the TU survey to the network. This also made it possible to
visualise the observed routes which was done as an example in subsection 3.3.1.
In order to estimate a route choice model each observed route must have an appertaining
set of alternatives to enable a discrete choice between routes, known as the choice set.
There are several ways that a choice set can be generated and in this case it was done
with a doubly stochastic generation method. This meant that a utility function with all
the attributes of a public transport trip was set up and an error term was added. Apart
from the error term which provides variation in travellers’ perception of the path cost an
error component was added which enable heterogeneous perception of each component.
==PAGE:57==
CHAPTER 3
43
This method of generating a choice set means that a lot of trips that does not make sense
to perform in reality can be found in the choice set. Initially, this was not considered
a problem, but as it turned out, a number of routes had transfers outside the Greater
Copenhagen Area and these transfers were not registered. It was therefore decided to
remove all these trips from the choice set. In order to not distort the alternatives left in
a given OD-pair, it was decided to discard the entire OD-pair when the number of itera-
tions affected was above 20 %. For the remaining trips, only the affected iterations were
removed. Finally, the chapter presents the distributions of transfers both in the choice set
and in the observed data. These differ substantially but as mentioned before this should
not be a problem with regards to the estimation of the model.
==PAGE:58==
44
GEOGRAPHICAL CONTEXT AND DATA FOUNDATION
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 4
Selection of attributes
This chapter presents the approach used for selecting the attributes that will be estimated
in this thesis. The attributes are chosen among a long-list of attributes that are all de-
scribed in the ﬁrst section of this chapter. The attributes will be chosen based on a num-
ber of criteria, that all help to deﬁne which would be the best of the considered. The
attributes that are considered were all mentioned in the literature presented in chapter 2.
Collecting data for the attributes is not a trivial task and the process will therefore be
described thoroughly. The ﬁnal part of the ﬁrst section will present the attributes that
were actually chosen, and the deﬁnitions of the chosen attributes will be presented in the
second part.
4.1
Attributes
In this section all considered attributes will be reviewed. These are all found based on
suggestions from the literature presented in chapter 2. In order to select the best suitable
attributes, the attributes have been evaluated according to four criteria:
• Validity
• Reliability
• Measurability
• Data availability
The validity criterion deﬁnes how well an attribute measures what it is suppose to mea-
sure. In this case, how much impact the attributes have on the transfer. The reliability
criterion considers whether two independent registrations, of the same attribute and un-
der the same circumstances, would give the same result.
The measurability criterion indicates how easy the attribute is to measure and ﬁnally the
data availability criterion gives an idea about how easy the registration and data collection
45
==PAGE:60==
46
SELECTION OF ATTRIBUTES
will be for a given attribute (Joumard et al., 2010). Each attribute on the long-list will be
examined below based on the criteria in order to select the attributes that will be used for
model estimation later on. The long-list is as follows:
• Appearance
• Detour
• Seats
• Safety
• Security
• Shops
• Level changes
• Occupancy
• Shelters
• Information level
The ten listed attributes will all be evaluated based on the four mentioned criteria.
Appearance
Appearance is connected to the transfer station or stop and covers the entire transfer
from arriving with the ﬁrst mode to departing with the next mode. The appearance can
among other depend on the cleanliness and lighting, which both affect the travellers gen-
eral impression of the surroundings at a given transfer station or stop. The attribute is
mentioned by Iseki and Taylor (2009) as a factor that should be considered when looking
at the physical environment. The difﬁculty about appearance is that it can be very subjec-
tive, why the reliability criterion of the appearance as an attribute fails. The appearance
attribute also performs badly with regards to the validity criterion, because appearance
of a transfer locations does not complicate a transfer directly. Appearance could affect a
transfer by making the transfer less pleasant and thereby travellers would perhaps tend
to avoid transfers at a given stop or station. The appearance would furthermore be dif-
ﬁcult to measure and would require a scale of range, where each station and stop would
have to be evaluated on. This would again be very subjective. In order to collect the
information it would be necessary to examine all stops and stations physically, since the
information from Google Maps or similar would be insufﬁcient.
Detour
The detour attribute describes a situation, where a transfer is located further away from
the destination than the origin or differs from the direct route from origin to destination.
This entails that the traveller feels that he or she is taking a detour in order to reach the
destination. This could be a transfer, located further from the destination than the origin
where the traveller will consider the route as too much of a detour even though it is
==PAGE:61==
CHAPTER 4
47
faster in time than the direct route. In general, it implies all routes where the route turn
back towards the origin or turn away from the destination. The attribute is introduced
in Raveau et al. (2011) and the deﬁnition was further developed in Raveau et al. (2014).
For this attribute the validity and the reliability criteria go hand in hand. This is due
to the fact that almost all travellers would have different opinions about when a transfer
causing a detour would be an additional burden for the given route than the transfer is in
itself. The attribute could be measured by how much of a detour it would be in either time
or distance. But if it for instance were measured in distance, it would still be necessary
to consider the time aspect as well since a detour in distance could potentially save the
traveller some time overall. The data collection would require that all combinations of
routes and transfers were considered. Furthermore, this attribute is linked to to the entire
route, why it is not enough to only consider all transfer stations and stops.
Seats
This attribute has not been estimated in the literature presented but was mentioned by
Chowdhury et al. (2013) as an attribute that could inﬂuence the willingness to transfer.
The attribute concerning seats is linked to the availability of seats at the transferring sta-
tion or stop, that is; does the traveller have the opportunity to sit down while waiting
for the next transfer. And further, how many seats are available. A large transfer station
would require more seats than a small stop. This attribute can be considered in two ways:
1. Availability of seats at transfer stations and stops. Are there seats available at a
station or stop? This would be considered as a dummy.
2. How many seats are available at each station or stop in comparison to how many
travellers transfer at the given station or stop. This would be considered as a count.
Both options would be evaluated high within most of the criteria. However the perfor-
mance in the validity criterion can be questioned, since the availability of seats should
only be considered for transfers with a wait time of a certain length where the need for
a seat is higher than transfers with a low waiting time. Furthermore it is very few, who
actually use seats even though they are available. The attribute would be easy to measure
and the reliability of the measurement would be high. The data collection would require
that all transfer stations and stops were examined physically by visiting the stations and
stops, which would be very time demanding. The advantage of the second option is that
it is possible to take into account whether the number of seats available is suitable for
the number of transfers made at a given station or stop. The data collection would be
more or less the same for both options, since both would require a visit to the stations
physically. The second option would require more work after the data collection in order
to estimate whether the number of seats matter given the size of the station or stop.
Safety
Safety is an attribute that indicates whether the traveller feels safe during the transfer. It
can imply that the traveller feels comfortable and safe at the waiting area or, if there is a
==PAGE:62==
48
SELECTION OF ATTRIBUTES
small walking distance between the two modes at the transfer, that the traveller feels safe
while walking. Iseki and Taylor (2009) mention that this attribute could help quantify
the physical environment. Factors that could affect the traveller’s sense of safety could
be the lighting in the evening, larger stations with more people or a kiosk. Factors that
could inﬂuence the traveller feeling unsafe could be grafﬁti, tunnels, darkness, no kiosk
etc.
Whether people feel safe is very individual and can also differ from place to place even
though conditions are very similar. A station that a traveller is very familiar with tend
to appear more safe than a transfer station never visited before. Furthermore, safety also
depends very much on time of day. In the evening and night where it is dark, many
stations and stops tend to feel more unsafe than they do during the day. The validity
of the safety attribute will therefore vary from traveller to traveller, which also affects
the reliability of the attribute. Each traveller considers safety differently and thereby the
reliability will also become very low. Furthermore, the measurement would be a scale
of range, but the deﬁnitions and borders between levels of the scale would be very hard
to identify. The data collection would require physical visit to each stop and station and
further all possible transfers should be physically examined at each station in order to
evaluate the safety. In order to be as precise as possible, the model should furthermore
account for time of day.
Security
The attribute security is related to the trafﬁc safety during the transfer and mostly related
to the walking distance between two transport modes in a transfer. This could among
others be whether the traveller has to cross a road without pedestrian crossing, crossing
rails or whether there is a fence separating the waiting area from a bike lane or road. How
secure a transfer is has been described by Iseki and Taylor (2009) as a factor affecting the
physical environment.
Security at a transfer station or stop does not have any direct impact on the transfer.
However, if the walking distance within a transfer require the traveller to cross a con-
gested road, a pedestrian crossing or a trafﬁc light would have a positive impact on the
transfer. This could for an example secure that the traveller avoided unnecessary wait
time at the road. Some travellers might tend to avoid transfers that include a physical
risk such as crossing a major road. A transfer at the same platform would not be affected
by trafﬁc safety, where a transfer with a longer walking distance could be affected by
trafﬁc safety measures such as pedestrian crossings etc.
When the the types of trafﬁc safety measures have been deﬁned, the reliability and the
measurement criteria would perform ﬁne, since it would be a question of whether the
given trafﬁc safety measures were present or not. The data collection would require the
==PAGE:63==
CHAPTER 4
49
same amount of work and time as the attribute for safety since the station and all transfers
would have to be examined physically.
Shops
The attribute for shops indicates whether there is access to shops at a transfer. Access
to a shop could make the wait time feel shorter if the traveller spend the wait time in a
shop instead of just standing and waiting. The possible signiﬁcance of a shop or a kiosk
at a transfer was mentioned by Anderson (2013). In this speciﬁc case the data collection
method entail an issue with this attribute. As mentioned in chapter 3 it is only considered
as one trip in the TU survey if what was bought in the shop was for the trip, like coffee,
tickets or magazines. If a traveller spends the wait time at a transfer for grocery shopping
or in a mall next to a station the trip will be split into two separate trips.
The validity of the shop attribute is high, since the presence of shops is expected have
a positive inﬂuence on the transfer penalty. Even though the traveller will not always
visit a shop, the fact that the opportunity is there, will most probably count for some-
thing. The shop attribute performs well under the reliability criterion and the measure-
ment and data collection are both straightforward. Furthermore, the data collection does
not require physical visits at each stop, since information about shops at stations can be
found at the DSB web page or by using Google Maps.
Level change
Level changes is an attribute that considers whether a transfer includes ascending or de-
scending stairs walking between the two transport modes. This attribute was estimated
by Raveau et al. (2011), Raveau et al. (2014) and Guo and Wilson (2011) as a factor that
ought to be considered. They further all account for whether the level changes are as-
sisted with an escalator or an elevator for travellers that are walking-impaired (or lazy).
These attributes can be registered in two ways:
1. If a transfer requires level change or not, registered as a dummy.
2. If a transfer requires level change, the registration would be a count of how many
level changes.
For both options it can be further detailed by separating the registration into ascending
level changes and descending level changes as well as ascending and descending level
changes assisted with escalator and/or elevator.
The validity of this attribute is very high, since a transfer with level changes without
any kind of assistance from either escalator or elevator would force walking-impaired
travellers to choose another route and it also have a direct impact on the walking dis-
tance between two transport modes. Furthermore, it is exhausting to climb stairs and
a transfer without level changes could easily be assumed to be preferred by an average
==PAGE:64==
50
SELECTION OF ATTRIBUTES
traveller. When it has been decided how level changes should be registered, the attribute
performs well within the reliability and measurement criteria, since the registration re-
gards physical facilities at a transfer station or stops. The data is easily accessible, which
makes the data collection easy, but it will be time demanding since the stations needs to
be examined and all transfers at each station have to be identiﬁed.
Occupancy
The occupancy attribute describes the occupancy rate for the next transport mode fol-
lowing a transfer. The occupancy can be deﬁned as the probability of getting a seat at the
next transport mode which was done by Raveau et al. (2011) and later on by Raveau et al.
(2014). Schm¨ocker et al. (2011) furthermore introduced a so-called fail-to-sit probability
and estimated route choice models that included a variable that measured how likely it
was to ﬁnd a seat. This attribute will very much be inﬂuenced by the time of day; during
rush hour the probability of getting a seat at a transport mode is very low whereas the
probability outside the rush hours will be much higher. The performance within the mea-
surement criterion would likewise depend on time of day as well as frequency for each
transport mode. A high frequency would result in not only higher probability of getting
a seat, but the traveller would be willing to accept a lower probability, since the following
departure for the transport mode will be shortly after. The data collection would be very
difﬁcult, since the probability should be calculated for each transport mode, each line and
each line variant at each departure at each stop, which makes it very demanding. This
could be avoided by collecting the data through a simulation. The measurement would
be to count the number of seats and the number of occupied seats, which can differ from
departure to the departure. The reliability of the attribute is still considered high, since
the exact same departure would be measured identically. The validity of the attribute is a
bit more complex, since a traveller is assumed to avoid a transfer or take another route, if
the occupancy rate is high. However, many travellers would still have to transfer during
rush hour, where the occupancy is high due to the fact that they are going to work, school
or other places where they have to be at a certain time.
Shelter
The shelter attribute is related to the wait time at a transfer and describes whether the
waiting area for the next transport mode at a transfer has a cover or not. The inﬂuence of
shelter at transfer stations was estimated by Chowdhury et al. (2013). The type of shelter
can differ very much from stop to stop, why it is interesting to look further into. This
will especially have an inﬂuence on the larger transfer stations, where a high number of
travellers are waiting and thereby a larger cover would make sense.
The shelter attribute performs well in all criteria, since it has an impact on the wait time
at a transfer. It is easy to measure and the reliability is good since the result of the mea-
surement repeatedly would give the same result. The data availability is good and most
data collection could be done by using Google Maps and a few ﬁeld trips.
==PAGE:65==
CHAPTER 4
51
Information level
Information level as an attribute is linked to the walking distance between two transport
modes at a transfer and describes how well the information at a station or stop is. The
information level can be good if there are many signs to navigate the traveller from one
stop to another or if the risk of making a wrong turn during the walk between two stops
is low. A train station can be very confusing, which affects the information level in a
negative way. If time is short at a transfer between two transport modes, the informa-
tion level can be very important, since a low information level can result in missing the
next mode at a transfer. Iseki and Taylor (2009) state that the information level will affect
both the actual and the perceived walk time whereas Chowdhury et al. (2013) estimate
the information level independent of the actual walk time. In general, a transfer seems
much more appealing if it is easy, and the information level has a high inﬂuence on how
easy a transfer is. However, the information level can be very subjective and depends
very much on whether the traveller is familiar with the transfer or not. The measurement
would require a scale, where each speciﬁc transfer is rated on the scale, which can be
indicated in a number of levels. The data availability is considered average, since it is ac-
tually quite difﬁcult to measure, but the signs at stations support the level of informations
and provides a good starting point for the data collection.
4.1.1
Evaluation of attributes
Each attribute has been examined and this section gives an overview of how each at-
tribute has been rated within each criterion in order to select the ﬁnal attributes. Table 4.1
shows the ratings of each attribute.
Table 4.1: An overview of how each attribute has been rated according to the four criteria.

The ratings show that three attributes has been given the best score in all four criteria.
These attributes are shop, level change and shelter. All of these are considered as very
relevant for a transfer, easy to measure with a high reliability and the availability of data
is good. All three attributes are included in the ﬁnal attributes. The attribute concerning
seats was also rated high in almost all criteria, but fails a bit on the validity. A interesting
==PAGE:66==
52
SELECTION OF ATTRIBUTES
discussion is therefore whether travellers actually use seats when they are available and
from own experiences this is not always the case. Furthermore, the seats and shelters can
be correlated, since most seats at stations or stops are covered by a shelter. Based on these
reasons, the attribute concerning seats has not been chosen as a ﬁnal attribute.
A few attributes are rated very low in most categories, why these are eliminated without
further investigation. These attributes are; appearance, detour, safety and security. The
remaining attributes, occupancy and information level, both have a good and bad ratings.
Occupancy scored very low in the data availability because of the fact that the measure-
ment differs from departure to departure and many aspects are affecting the data. Since
the data availability is so low, the attribute was eliminated. The information level does
not score low in any criteria, but only scores high in the validity. However the validity
is one of the most important criteria, since the model estimation later on has an aim of
determine which factors has an inﬂuence on transfers. The information level can be very
subjective and the measurement would be a scale, where each transfer has to be exam-
ined in order to place it on the scale. However the attribute is considered to have a great
inﬂuence on the transfer, why it is included in the ﬁnal attributes for model estimation.
In conclusion the four ﬁnal attributes chosen for model estimation are:
• Shops
• Level changes
• Shelters
• Information level
These four attributes are deﬁned in more details in the following section.
4.2
Deﬁnition of selected attributes
This section gives a clear and detailed deﬁnition of the attributes chosen for the model es-
timation. The attributes will be presented separately and each attribute will be described
in great detail regarding deﬁnition and registration. All of the chosen attributes are di-
vided into a number of variables in order to test the attributes as detailed as possible.
4.2.1
Shelter
For each transfer the type of shelter will be registered for the stop that the traveller is
transferring to, meaning that if it is a transfer from bus to train the shelter type at the
train stop will be registered. It is assumed that the traveller is waiting at the departing
stop for next transport mode when transferring, which also is the case in the NTM, where
travellers always walk from the stop node and along the change edge before waiting at
the departing stop for the next transport mode. Following options are registered for type
of shelter:
==PAGE:67==
CHAPTER 4
53
• Bus stop without shelter
• Bus stop with a small shelter
• Bus stop with a large shelter
• Train station without shelter
• Train station with a small shelter
• Train station with a large shelter
• Underground train station
• Overground metro station
• Underground metro station
Each of the shelter types will be registered as dummies since only one shelter type is
available at each stop. If more options are available the largest possible cover will be
registered. The metro station is only split into overground and underground since all
overground station has the same type of large cover. The deﬁnition of each shelter type
is brieﬂy explained in the following along with an example for each.
All shelter types are split into type of transport mode as well as type of shelter. This
is done in order to, hopefully, be able to capture whether the type of transport mode
the traveller is waiting for has an inﬂuence on the waiting time. According to Anderson
(2013) transfers between two buses are the most burdensome for the travellers followed
by transfers from train to bus. That is, transfers where the traveller is transferring to a bus
is considered the worst transfers. It could therefore be reasonable to believe, that wait-
ing for a bus is also considered worse than waiting for a train. This assumption will be
tested when the shelter types are split into which type of transport mode the traveller is
waiting for. However, ideally the parameter containing shelter should be detached from
the mode of transport and instead just explain the effect of the shelter detached from the
mode. Below are listed all the 8 shelter categories with a short deﬁnition and an example
of each.
Bus shelters
Bus, no shelter: Bus stop, where there is no shelter. A bus stop with a bench without cover
is also registered in this category. Example given i Figure 4.1.
Bus, small shelter: Bus stop with the standard small shelters as seen at Figure 4.2.
Bus, large cover: These are found at larger stations and cover several bus stops. This could
for an example be, where all stops are gathered at a trafﬁc island where the entire island is
covered by a large roof. An example of a bus stop with a large cover is given in Figure 4.3.

Train, large cover: Train stations, where the platform is partly or completely covered by a
large cover. Platforms where only half of the platform is covered is also registered in this
category. An example from Lyngby Station is given in Figure 4.7.
Train, underground: Train stations located underground. Since we are working with data
from within the Greater Copenhagen Area, there is only one underground train station;
Nørreport Station.

4.2.2
Shop level
The second attribute that was selected for estimation in the present thesis is availability
of shop at a given transfer. It was decided to consider 3 levels of shops which are the
following:
• No shop (level 2)
• A kiosk (level 1)
• Several shops (level 0)
No shop is deﬁned by a transfer without access to any kind of shop. This is especially
including bus-bus transfers away from stations, but also transfers at stations without any
kind of shop. The deﬁnition of a station with no shops is when no kiosk or shop exist on
the station ground. At stations without shop it applies that if the traveller does not pass
a kiosk/shop transferring from one mode to another, the transfer will be considered as a
transfer with no shop. See Figure 4.10 for photo example.
A kiosk is deﬁned by a small shop, where it is possible to buy snacks, drinks, magazines,
tickets etc. A grocery store will not be considered as a kiosk. Most S-train stations have
a kiosk as minimum. If a station has a kiosk all transfers at the station will be registered
as having a kiosk available, even though the speciﬁc transfer may not pass the kiosk it is
still available within a small radius. See Figure 4.11 for photo example.
Several shops are registered when there is more than a kiosk. This can be a grocery store,
a bakery, a materialist or any other types of shops in addition to the kiosk. Some stations
have a shopping mall right next to the station, transfers here are registered with several
shops, since there is direct access to the shopping mall from the station. As with the level
described just above it applies that if a station has several shops it will be registered for
all possible transfers at the station. See Figure 4.12 for photo example.
==PAGE:71==
CHAPTER 4
57
Figure 4.10:
Malmparken
Station is an example of a
station without any shop.
(www.wikipedia.org)
Figure 4.11: Hellerup Station
is an example of a station that
has a kiosk.
Figure 4.12: Lyngby Station
is an example of a station
with several shops.
4.2.3
Information level
The level of information for each transfer describes how confusing it is to ﬁnd the direc-
tion from the stop where the traveller arrives, to the stop where the next transport mode
is departing when transferring. As described by Iseki and Taylor (2009) the informa-
tion level can affect the perceived and actual transfer walk time. In this thesis these are,
however, considered detached and the effect of information level should ideally only be
captured in the information parameter estimate. However, due to the fact that a longer
walk exposes the travellers to more situations where they risk taking the wrong turn or
getting lost, the two parameters are considered somewhat correlated. The information
level is divided into four categories, where each category will be registered as a dummy
since a transfer will only be within one of the four categories.
Following options for level of confusion regarding information is available:
• No confusion (level 0)
• Little confusion (level 1)
• Moderate confusion (level 2)
• Total confusion (level 3)
No confusion is deﬁned by a transfer where the is no confusion about the direction be-
tween the arriving and departing stop. When a traveller alights at the arrival stop it is
easy to locate the direction for the departing stop for next mode within a few seconds.
The information level is good and the departing stop can be found by intuition. An ex-
ample of this could be a transfer from one train to another where the two trains arrive at
==PAGE:72==
58
SELECTION OF ATTRIBUTES
and depart from the same platform. An example of this can found on Hellerup Station
and Figure 4.13 shows this. Another possibility is a transfer between two buses at the
same bus stop.
Figure 4.13: An example of information level 0 can be found at Hellerup Station where the trav-
eller just have to cross the platform in order to get to board the next train.
Little confusion describes transfers where it takes more than a few seconds to ﬁnd the di-
rection for the next departing stop. However, after the direction has been located it is still
quite simple to ﬁnd the departing stop given the information signs. The distance from
arriving stop to departing stop is often relatively short because the distance is somewhat
correlated to the number of times a traveller risks getting lost. When the direction is lo-
cated the traveller will not risk getting lost, since the direction is straightforward. The
information level is still good, but it takes more than a minute to locate the departing
stop. This could for instance be the case when arriving in a bus at a train station and
making a transfer to the train. A real life example of this is found in Vanløse where you
are instantly aware which direction to go, to reach the train station in the red building (see
ﬁg Figure 4.13), and it takes little time to locate the correct transport mode (metro/train).
Moderate confusion indicates transfers where it is more difﬁcult to orientate. First of all it
is more difﬁcult to ﬁnd the right direction towards the next departing stop at a transfer
and further, when the direction is found, there are still risks of getting lost on the way.
Moderate confusion often include transfers where the distance between the arriving stop
and the next departing stop requires more than a few minutes walk. This is again caused
by the correlation between distance and risks of getting lost. Another possible transfer
with moderate confusion is where ﬁnding the direction corresponds to little confusion,
==PAGE:73==
CHAPTER 4
59
Figure 4.14: An example of information level 1 is seen at Vanløse Station where it is easy to locate
the direction but not entirely straightforward to get to the correct platform.
but when you reach the area for where the departing stop is, it is confusing where the
speciﬁc line is departing from - this could be ﬁnding the correct bus stop at a larger sta-
tion with many buses, ﬁnding the right platform for a speciﬁc train when there are many
platforms to choose from or other similar situations. An example of moderate confusion
can be found at Hellerup Station when transferring from bus to train. In that case the
ﬁrst obstacle is to ﬁgure out where to go to actually locate the trains. Following that the
traveller will have doubts on which of the ﬁve tracks that the train is leaving from. The
ﬁrst part of this example is shown on Figure 4.15.
Total confusion deﬁnes transfers where the information level is very low and/or the trav-
ellers ﬁnd it very difﬁcult to ﬁnd the direction when transferring from an arriving stop to
the next departing stop. Total confusion can also describe a transfer where the direction
is difﬁcult, and further, when you reach the location of the stop it can be confusing where
the speciﬁc line is departing from. The traveller will risk getting lost more than once
after locating the departing stop. There can possibly be many options when it comes to
ﬁnding the correct way, which also makes it confusing which way to go. There are not
that many transfers in the Greater Copenhagen Area that can be characterised as having
total confusion. However, they are found when transferring to buses at Lyngby Station
and at Flintholm Station. The latter is both the case when transferring between the buses
and the F-train towards Hellerup where it is necessary to ascend and descend to get to
the platform despite that the train is at ground level. When transferring from the S-train
to the metro it is also not obvious that even though both train lines are elevated from the
==PAGE:74==
60
SELECTION OF ATTRIBUTES
ground level, a traveller has to ﬁrst descend then walk straight and then ascend. Fig-
ure 4.16 gives an impression of the confusing layout of Flintholm Station.
Figure 4.15: An example of information level 2 could be at a transfer from a bus at Hellerup
Station to a train. First it is hard to locate where to go to ﬁnd the trains (red arrow on the photo)
and following that the traveller has to locate the correct platform.
Figure 4.16:
An example of information level 3 can be found at Flintholm Station
(www.damsohusene.dk).
The information level will always be be inﬂuenced by subjective opinions, since people
have different opinions about what is confusing. The registration is based on the deﬁni-
==PAGE:75==
CHAPTER 4
61
tions described in this section, but subjective opinions cannot be avoided completely.
4.2.4
Level change
The number of level changes per transfer describes how many times a traveller has to as-
cend or descend stairs for each transfer. Furthermore, it is registered whether the ascend
or descend is assisted by an escalator. Following options are registered for types of level
changes:
• Transfers without level change
• Number of ascending level changes
• Number of ascending level changes assisted by escalator
• Number of descending level changes
• Number of descending level changes assisted by escalator
Figure 4.17: Example of stairs, one level, at
Flintholm Station (www.dinby.dk)
Figure
4.18:
Example
of
escalator
in
two
levels
at
Kongens
Nytorv
Station.
(www.wikipedia.org)
Level changes differ from the other attributes by being registered as counts. For each
transfer the number of ascends, descends, ascends assisted by escalator and descends
assisted by escalator is counted. For example, many underground metro stations can
be up to three levels below ground level, where the ﬁrst descend is normally without
escalator and the last two descends are with an escalator. That counts for one descending
==PAGE:76==
62
SELECTION OF ATTRIBUTES
level and two descending levels assisted by escalator. If a level change has both stairs and
escalator, the best option will be registered, which in this case always will be the option
with an escalator. Transfers without level changes are registered as a dummy variable.
4.3
Summary and discussion of the chapter
This chapter has elaborated how the process of selecting there attributes was conducted.
The starting point was a long-list containing ten different attributes that had all been men-
tioned in the existing literature. Each of these attributes were then discussed based on
four predeﬁned criteria namely; validity, reliability, measurability and data availability.
In order to be able to quantify the attributes it was important that an attribute performed
well in the two latter criteria. The ﬁrst two criteria are considered important in terms of
deﬁning meaningful attributes for the purpose.
The four attributes that were selected based on the above mentioned criteria were:
• Shops
• Level changes
• Shelters
• Information level
These ﬁrst three performs very well in all four deﬁned criteria, whereas it can be dis-
cussed whether the information level as an attribute is reliable and how easy it is to
measure. Despite these concerns it was decided to include the information level and test
whether it is signiﬁcant in a route choice model.
Several of the attributes on the long-list were evaluated badly partly because of the time
demanding work of registration. Although doing this would deﬁnitely be out of the time
scope for this thesis, it should be noted that several of the data collection for several of the
attributes could be done simultaneously by paying a visit to all stations in the network.
A type of attribute that was not considered in this long-list was one seperate attribute
for each station as suggested by (Guo and Wilson, 2011). This would provide some sort
of explanation of the unexplainable in why some stations are preferred as transfer loca-
tions. On the other hand it could be considered an easy way out of not having to estimate
the details of a well perceived transfer.
The ﬁnal part of the chapter presented the exact deﬁnitions of the chosen attributes. With
photos of all instances it was documented how the data will be interpreted. The following
chapter will describe how the actual registration of the data was carried out.
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 5
Data collection
In the previous chapter ten possible attributes were analysed and based on four crite-
ria the most suitable for use were selected. Furthermore, it was speciﬁed exactly how
each attribute was deﬁned. This chapter will focus on how the data was collected. The
network is large and there is a huge amount of transfer possibilities; too many to be reg-
istered manually. The approach on how this issue was addressed will be explained in
this chapter. In this is also included a detailed example of how the registration was done
in practice. The last section presents how the added attributes are actually distributed in
the data, more speciﬁcally whether there is a difference between the choice set and the
observed data.
5.1
Registration
In order to assign the correct attributes to each station and thereby determine the design
of each transfer, information about all stations in the Greater Copenhagen Area has been
collected. The stations were divided into two groups, where the ﬁrst group of stations in-
cluded the 20 largest transfer stations including attached bus stops and the second group
of stations included the remaining stations and bus stops. The 20 largest transfer stations
cover 65 % of all transfers in the observed data and each of these have been examined for
very detailed information about every possible transfer. The possible transfers at each
station were determined based on the change edges in the NTM. For the remaining 35
% of the transfers a more general method was used to determine the transfer attributes,
but still on a station based level to obtain as detailed information about the transfers as
possible.
This section will present the registration process used for the data collection. First subsec-
tion describes the stopgroups created to deﬁne all transfers, the second section describes
the process for the 20 largest transfer stations in the Greater Copenhagen Area including
an example of one of the largest transfer stations. The third part deals with the method
used for the remaining stops and stations in the area.
63
==PAGE:78==
64
DATA COLLECTION
5.1.1
Stopgroups
In NTM, all stops are determined by nodes. Each station has a number of stop-nodes,
which are classiﬁed by the transport mode. This means that the S-trains are assigned to
one node, the regional trains to another node etc., all within a given station. This makes
it possible to create change edges between each stop-node to show the transfers at the
station. However, when adding attributes to the transfers these stop-nodes are not sufﬁ-
cient. Therefore new stopgroups have been created based on the stop-nodes from NTM.
The new stopgroups are deﬁned by station, transport mode and attributes. The reason
why the stop-nodes in NTM are insufﬁcient is due to the fact that transfers between two
transport modes (from bus to train) are not necessarily the same even though the line
variant of the given mode is within the same stop-nodes in NTM. An example of this
could be Hellerup Station, which is shown in Figure 5.1, where there is direct access to
the platform when transferring from a bus to a regional train going north (track 1), but
if you are transferring from a bus to a regional train going towards the center of Copen-
hagen (track 2) you have to access the platform by stairs both ascending and descending.
