In this speciﬁc case the stop-nodes for the regional trains have been divided into two
stopgroups - one representing the line variants going north (direct access to the buses)
and one representing the line variants going south (access to buses by stairs). In general
==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 5
65
it has been necessary to split up almost all stop-nodes for stations in NTM that has more
than one platform for trains, so that there is a stopgroup representing each platform. This
is also an advantage when considering the attributes related to a given stop, such as cover
of waiting area.
The same applies for buses, where the main difference within a stop-node is the cover
of the waiting area for the buses. An example could be a bus stop where there is a small
shelter at the bus stop on one side of the road but at the other side of the road, represent-
ing the bus stop in the opposite direction, there is no shelter. An example of such is given
in Figure 5.2. In these cases the stop-node has been divided into two stopgroups. One
for the line variants serving the stop with a small shelter and one for the line variants
serving the bus stop without a shelter.
Figure 5.2: Example of stop-node in NTM that needs to be split into two stopgroups due to
different cover facilities.
In general when creating these new stopgroups for each station, regardless of transport
mode, it has been utterly important to secure that the correct line variants go to the cor-
rect stopgroup. When all line variants are assigned to a stopgroup depending on sta-
tion, transport mode and attributes it is possible to set up the transfers between the stop-
groups. For each station all possible transfers have been investigated. A possible transfer
is deﬁned in the NTM, where a change edge between two stop-nodes represents a trans-
fer. Furthermore, all transfers within a stopgroup have been investigated as well. Each
transfer between or within a stopgroup is then assigned a ChangeID. This ChangeID rep-
resents a speciﬁc transfer between or within two stopgroups and a number of attributes
that have been assigned to this speciﬁc ChangeID. Thereby it is possible to link a transfer
==PAGE:80==
66
DATA COLLECTION
between two speciﬁc line variants at an exact location to a ChangeID and thereby link the
correct attributes to the transfer.
5.1.2
The 20 largest transfer stations
The 20 largest transfer stations are deﬁned by being the 20 stations that have the most
transfers based on the observed data from TU. The data from TU is described in sec-
tion 3.3. By processing the data received from the TU it was possible to identify all
transfers and thereby determine which stations were the largest transfer stations. The
20 largest transfer stations in the Greater Copenhagen Area cover 65 % of all transfers in
the observed data made within the area of Greater Copenhagen. Nørreport Station is by
far the largest of all transfer stations within the area, with good reason. Nørreport is a
central node in the public transport network where most transport modes that run within
the center of Copenhagen meet. As seen in Figure 5.3, many of the 20 largest transfer sta-
tions are located in Copenhagen or the suburbs just outside Copenhagen. Common to
them all is that several transport modes cross each other at the stations.
The data collection for the 20 largest transfer stations has been carried out in a very de-
tailed manner, where each station has been examined individually. Firstly the approach
will be explained in words followed by an example of data collection for Copenhagen
Central Station to make the approach more clear.
For each station all possible transfers have been identiﬁed and thereafter attributes have
been added. The data from TU is from February 2009 through to March 2010 why it is im-
portant that the added attributes matches the design of the stations as it was in that time
period. To do so, several approaches have been used; ﬁeld trips, Google Maps, Google
Earth, terminal maps of stations (Movia) and personal knowledge.
Google Maps has a time feature that enables going back in time to see how the design
of the stations and the locations of, especially the bus stops, was back in 2009/2010.
Depending on which years Google Maps has been ﬁlming the street view, it has been
possible to see almost all changes made from 2009 and until today. This has been very
helpful when identifying the design of the stations in the period of where the data from
TU is collected. An example of the feature is shown in Figure 5.4, with a street view of
the buses at Hellerup Station from June 2009. As the timeline shows it is also possible to
get pictures from September 2010 and May 2014.
By using the change edges in NTM it has been possible to identify all transfers made at
a given station. All change edges in NTM are deﬁned between two stop-nodes. There-
fore, all stop-nodes have been examined in order to see whether a stop-node should be
split into two or more stopgroups depending on the design and the other attributes that
will be added later. In general, all stop-nodes representing trains, either S-trains, regional
==PAGE:81==
CHAPTER 5
67
Figure 5.3: The 20 largest transfer stations in Greater Copenhagen Area.
trains, or local trains, should be split into the number of platforms at a station. If a station
has one stop-node for S-trains, but the station has two platforms from where the S-trains
run, the stop-node has been split into two stopgroups.
For stop-nodes regarding metro it would never be necessary to split the stop-node since
==PAGE:82==
68
DATA COLLECTION
Figure 5.4: Example of time feature in Google maps (www.maps.google.dk).
the metro always runs from the same platform and therefore the attributes would always
be the same within a station for both metro lines in both directions.
The stop-nodes for buses can be more complicated. For a given station there can eas-
ily be change edges to several bus stop-nodes located close to the station. This could be
because some buses do not stop at the station or it could be because the transfer is more
convenient as so. For each stop-node it is necessary to consider both the access to the
given stop and also the design of the stop. In most cases the access to a bus stop-node
is the same, but the design can be different within the stop-node. An example of this is
given in section 5.1.1, where one stop has a small shelter and another stop within same
stop-node in NTM does not have any shelter. In such case the stop-node would be split
into two stopgroups in order to match the correct attributes to the transfers. To minimize
the number of transfers between stopgroups, it is also possible to gather more stop-nodes
in one stopgroup if all criteria and attributes are equal. This is simply done to minimize
the number of transfers to examine since the correct line variant ID will always be con-
nected to the correct stopgroup and thereby a transfer between two line variants will
always be unique regardless of previous stopgroups. This is due to the unique ChangeID
that each transfer will be assigned.
In order to assign all stop-nodes, lines and line variants to the correct stopgroups a de-
tailed examination of each station has been conducted. A great share of this is based on
==PAGE:83==
CHAPTER 5
69
a combination of personal knowledge and ﬁeld trips to the stations supported by Google
Maps. When all stopgroups have been determined for a station it is possible to assign
attributes to the transfers between and within the stopgroups. For each station all pos-
sible combinations of transfers have been registered, regardless of whether the transfer
make sense or not. A transfer that leads to a U-turn is also registered as well as transfers
from and to the exact same bus stop and line variant. This is done in order to be sure that
all transfers are registered since the choice set contains many meaningless transfers. Fur-
thermore, no registrations are assigned to the access and egress station on a route. This is
based on the fact that this study deals with transfers only, why the registration will only
be related to these.
For each transfer a number of attributes have been examined; level changes (stairs/escalator/no
level change), presence of a shop or several shops, cover facility for waiting area and the
level of information to help ﬁnd next transport mode. Some attributes are registered as
dummies and some as counts. Level changes by stairs/escalator are registered as a counts
with number of stairs with and without escalator at a transfer. The presence of shop has
been registered as dummies, where each option (no shop, one shop, several shops) has
its own dummy. The cover facilities at waiting area have been registered as dummies as
well with three options: no shelter, small shelter and large cover. The cover has been split
into either bus, train or metro. Furthermore, it was registered for train and metro if the
station was an underground station. The information level at transfer has been ranked
on a scale from 0 to 3, where 3 is a level of total confusion for the information level.
Attributes such as number of stairs with and without escalator, the level of information
and to some extend the presence of a shop, are connected to the walk between the two
transport modes whereas the cover of the waiting area is connected to the time spend
waiting for the next mode of transport. Therefore, the cover facility related to the stop-
group transferring to whereas the rest of the attributes are more related to the design of
the station.
Example Copenhagen Central Station/Vesterport Station
This paragraph constitutes a walkthrough that explains how the registration described
above was actually carried out through an example. The example concerns Copenhagen
Central Station/Vesterport Station which is a rather complicated transfer location.
Copenhagen Central Station is the second largest transfer station in the Greater Copen-
hagen Area.
As seen in Figure 5.5 the station has a high number of change edges.
Copenhagen Central Station does not only have transfers within the station but also to
Vesterport Station and R˚adhuspladsen. R˚adhuspladsen is a large bus terminal close to
Copenhagen Central Station where many buses pass by. Both Vesterport Station and
R˚adhuspladsen are registered along with Copenhagen Central Station in order to cover
==PAGE:84==
70
DATA COLLECTION
all change edges.
Figure 5.5: Change edges at Copenhagen Central Station (København H), Vesterport Station and
R˚adhuspladsen.
First all stop-nodes have been examined in order to assign the correct number of stop-
groups. Each bus stop node is veriﬁed in order to investigate whether all conditions are
the same on both sides of the street. If so, the stop-node is assigned a stopgroup cor-
==PAGE:85==
CHAPTER 5
71
responding to the entire stop-node and if not, the stop-node is split into two - one for
each physical stop at each side of the road. If a stop-node is split into two stopgroups
all line variants have been examined in order to assign the correct line variants to the
correct stopgroup. An example of this could be stop 268 at Vesterport Station. This stop
has a small shelter on one side of the road and no shelter on the other side. Therefore the
stop-node for stop 268 has been divided into two stopgroups. Another possible situation
is that two bus stop-nodes are completely identical and thereby, for simplicity of the data
collection, the stop-nodes can be put into the same stopgroup. For Copenhagen Central
Station along with Vesterport Station and R˚adhuspladsen 12 bus stop-nodes were exam-
ined resulting in 8 different stopgroups.
The stopgroups for the buses are presented in Table 5.1, where each stopgroup contains
one or more stop-nodes. If a stopgroup contains all lines and line variants serving the
stop-node, then nothing is speciﬁed in the columns LineID and LineVarID. Later on all
the associated line variants will be added. Stopgroup 11807 and 11808 contains several
stop-nodes, where the stop-nodes have been split into two stopgroups. When a stop-
node is split into two stopgroups all LineIDs and LineVarIDs have been examined in
order to assign the correct line variant to the correct stopgroup.

Both S-trains and regional trains are running from the Copenhagen Central Station. In

In general, every stop-node for any type of train is divided into a number of stopgroups

running from. For Copenhagen Central Station the regional trains run from 4 platforms

Figure 5.6: Terminal map of Copenhagen Central Station. (Movia)
sidered. The line variants are assigned to stopgroups according to the direction in which
they leave the central station. The approach for the regional trains differ from the general
method. It is not possible to link a line variant to a given platform or track at Copenhagen
Central Station since it can vary what track a line variant departs from. Because of this it
is impossible to assign line variants to stopgroups for the regional trains. All attributes
are the same for the four platforms serving the regional trains, therefore it was decided to
keep all four platforms in one stopgroup and thereby assign all line variants to the same
stopgroup. This decision has been made due to the fact that if a traveller makes a rational
transfer between two regional trains, it would require a change of platform. Therefore it
is possible to deﬁne a transfer within the stopgroup as a transfer between two platforms.
Furthermore, a transfer to/from a regional train from/to any other transport mode - here
buses and S-trains- would always have the same attributes assigned regardless of which
platform the regional train runs from. A source of error in this regard is though, that the
routes in the choice set are sufﬁciently meaningless to have transfers that does not require
a change of platform because it is a U-turn or the like. This means that these transfers
will be assigned slightly wrong attributes.
At Vesterport Station all S-trains run from the same platform and there is no regional
trains serving the station. Therefore only one stopgroup has been created for the trains.
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 5
73
After deﬁning all stopgroups it is possible to determine all transfers between stopgroups.
The transfers between stopgroups depend on whether there is a change edge in the NTM.
If there is a change edge between two stop-nodes in NTM a transfer is deﬁned between
the corresponding stopgroups and attributes are assigned. Furthermore, transfers are
deﬁned within each stopgroup. For Copenhagen Central Station, Vesterport Station and
R˚adhuspladsen a total of 47 change edges have been deﬁned as transfers in both di-
rections. Further, there is a total of 12 stopgroups, that all have transfers within each
stopgroup. This results is a total of 92 transfers between and within stopgroups at Copen-
hagen Central Station, Vesterport Station and R˚adhuspladsen. For each transfer a number
of attributes have been assigned. An example is given in Table 5.2, where a transfer be-
tween two stopgroups for buses has been deﬁned. The transfer is deﬁned by a ChangeID
and for each ChangeID it was deﬁned to and from which stopgroup the transfer was
made.
Table 5.2: Example of transfer between two stopgroups with assigned attributes.

The attributes are the assigned to each ChangeID. The same-level attribute is a dummy
that indicates whether or not the transfer is performed without level change. Number of
ascending refers to the number of ascending levels within the transfer and the number of
ascending escalator refers to how many ascending levels, which are assisted by escalator.
Both are registered as a count. The same applies for number of descending levels with and
without escalator. Shop is registered on a scale, where 2 indicates no shop, 1 indicates one
shop and 0 indicates several shops. Shelter for buses applies when transferring to a bus
and is also a scale. 0 means no shelter, 1 means small shelter and 2 means large shelter.
The shelter for train accounts for the same and includes all types of trains and metro. The
registration of attributes regarding stops and shelters for bus and train are registered as
a scale, but will be changed to dummies for future use. Underground is a dummy that
indicates whether or not the stop transferring to is underground. This mostly applies for
the underground metro stations in Copenhagen. Last, the information level is a scale that
indicates how confusing it is to ﬁnd the stop transferring to. The scale goes from 0 to 3,
where 3 indicates the highest level of confusion.
==PAGE:88==
74
DATA COLLECTION
5.1.3
Remaining stations and stops
The remaining 35 % of all transfers in the observed data within the Greater Copenhagen
Area include both stations and larger bus stops. As the observed data do not cover all
possible transfers, all remaining stations and bus stops, besides the 20 largest stations,
are included in this group of transfer stations and stops.
In order to assign stopgroups for the remaining stations and stops, a more generalized
method has been conducted. All stations have been considered on an individual level,
but not as detailed as the 20 largest transfer stations.
All bus stops have been divided into two stopgroups: stops connected to a change edge
to either a train station or another bus stop in NTM, and bus stops without connection to
a change-edge. This division is done in order to minimize the combinations of transfers
between stations and bus stops and in order to assign attributes to stopgroups as correct
as possible. The attributes assigned for the two stopgroups only differentiate in the fact
that bus stops near a station are assumed to have access to a kiosk/shop and all other
bus stops are assumed not to be near a kiosk or shop. The attributes regarding the design
of the stop are assumed to be the same for the two stopgroups, where it is assumed that
all bus stops have a small shelter covering the waiting area. The attributes regarding the
actual transfer depends on where the transfer is going to and from.
The remaining stations within the Greater Copenhagen Area have been examined and,
depending on the design of the station, divided into stopgroups. 8 stopgroups have been
deﬁned for S-train stations, 4 stopgroups for metro stations, 8 stations for regional train
stations and 2 stopgroups for local trains. The stopgroups are deﬁned based on the dif-
ferent types of stations, where a station has been put in the most suitable stopgroup. The
deﬁnitions of the different types of stations are presented in the following.
Metro stations:
• Underground station one level below ground level with escalator, with kiosk.
• Underground station one level below ground level without escalator, with kiosk.
• Underground station two levels below ground level, access by both stairs and esca-
lator, with kiosk.
• Outdoor stations one level above ground level, large cover, access by stairs, without
kiosk.
S-train stations:
• Elevated railway, one platform without escalator.
==PAGE:89==
CHAPTER 5
75
• Elevated railway, two platforms without escalator.
• Elevated railway, two platforms, small shelters.
• Access to platform by stairs without escalator both ascending and descending.
• Direct access to platform (terminal station).
• Railway one level below ground level, without escalator, one platform.
• Railway one level below ground level, without escalator, two platforms.
• Railway one level below ground level, without escalator, two platforms, small shel-
ter.
All S-train stations are assumed to have a kiosk and a large cover unless else is mentioned.
Regional train stations:
• Elevated railway, one platform without escalator, large cover.
• Elevated railway, two platforms without escalator, large cover.
• Access to platform by stairs without escalator both ascending and descending,
small shelters.
• Access to platform by stairs without escalator both ascending and descending, large
cover.
• Direct access to platform, no level changes, small shelters.
• Railway one level below ground level with two platforms with escalator, large
cover.
• Underground station, two platforms, with escalator.
All regional train stations are assumed to have a kiosk.
Local train stations:
• Direct access to platform, no level changes.
• Access to platform by stairs without escalator both ascending and descending.
All local train stations are assumed not to have a kiosk or any shelter.
Each stopgroup is assigned attributes corresponding to the design of the station as men-
tioned in the list above. The attributes regarding the actual transfer, such as information
level and number of ascending and descending stairs, depends on whether the traveller
is transferring to/from a bus or another train or metro.
==PAGE:90==
76
DATA COLLECTION
The actual transfers are deﬁned by all possible combinations of transfers between all
stopgroups, where type of station and bus stop has been taken into consideration.
Transfers from bus to any type of train or metro station is only possible from the stop-
group containing bus stops with a change edge in the NTM. For the stopgroup containing
bus stops without a change edge it is only possible to transfer within the stopgroup.
For all stopgroups for either S-train, local train, regional train or metro it is assumed pos-
sible to transfer within the stopgroup but not to another stopgroup. This is done since
almost all stations only serve one train type. Since all stations are examined individually
it is secured that all possible transfers are deﬁned, by looking at the number of train types
at each station. If a station has more than one train type within the category; S-train, local
train, regional train, and metro, the speciﬁc transfer for this speciﬁc station is deﬁned as
well.
This method used for the remaining stations differ from the one used for the 20 largest
transfer station in the sense that only the overall design of the station is considered not
the individual line variants. For the 20 largest stations each line variant was considered in
order to assign the correct line variant to the correct stopgroup. Further, each stop-node
for each station was examined very detailed in order to create the correct stopgroups -
again to assign correct line variants to correct stopgroups. For the remaining stations
line variants are not considered. The main focus for the remaining stations has been the
design of the station in order to assign correct attributes overall.
5.2
Distributions of collected data
As described above the data was collected through thorough investigation of the actual
topology. The largest transfer stations were in great detail registered and the remaining
stations were registered based on their station design. To gain knowledge about the col-
lected data, this section will present a few tables describing how the transfers in the choice
set as well as in the observed data are distributed according to the chosen attributes. It
is important to notice that all numbers and percentages presented in the present section
are exclusively based on the transfers in the observed data and in the choice set. Trips
without transfers are not considered and one trip is represented as many times as it has
number of transfers.
Table 5.3 provides an overview of the types of shelters that are mainly used in trans-
fers. Both in the observed data and in the choice set it can be seen that the most used
shelters are the small bus shelters and large train shelters. This is of course due to the fact
that these two types of shelters are the most commonly found in the network. It should
==PAGE:91==
CHAPTER 5
77
also be noted that the percentage of transfers made to buses is substantially higher in the
choice set where 56 % of all changes are made to a bus as opposed to 39 % in the observed
data. As mentioned in chapter 3 there are a lot of irrelevant routes in the choice set and
a great part of these includes senseless transfers where the traveller alights the public
transport vehicle only to wait for the next scheduled one.

Another attribute that was registered as described in subsection 4.2.2 is the shop levels.
The distribution of the three shop levels in the choice set is not consistent with the one
found in the observed data (see Table 5.4). This disagreement is probably caused by the
fact that there is a higher number of transfers to buses in the choice set and these often
do not have any shops attached. As mentioned in subsection 5.1.3 the majority of bus
stops were registered as two groups - one being in connection to a train station the other
not. The largest group was for obvious reasons not in connection to a train station and
transfers from these stops were registered as having no possibility of going shopping.
Considering the average it is seen that a transfer in the observed data is most likely to
have one shop and second most likely to have several shops, whereas in the case of the
choice set the most likely is likewise having one shop but following that is having zero
shops.

The third attribute that was deﬁned in section 4.2 was the information level. This at-
tribute concerns whether a transfer is confusing or not and the levels of confusion were
deﬁned in subsection 4.2.3. In Table 5.5 it is seen how the distribution of information
levels for transfers in the choice set correspond quite accurately to the equivalent distri-
bution of information levels in the observed data. It is worth noticing that the share of
transfers with total confusion (information level: 3) is quite small (3.5 % and 3.8 % re-
spectively) which could later inﬂuence the estimate of this attribute because it is rarely
chosen. Compared to the other attributes, the information level stick out due to the fact
that the distributions for choice set and the observed data are so similar. Looking into
this fact it seems to be quite a coincidence. As mentioned several times, the bus to bus
changes are highly overrepresented in the choice set and they most often have the in-
formation level one. On the other hand, as it will be seen in Table 5.6, the transfers that
include one ascend and one descend as well as transfer with just one ascend or descend
are fairly overrepresented in the observed data. These kinds of transfers are often also
assigned an information level of one. So despite the difference between the choice set
and the observed data, the information levels are distributed similar.

5.3
Summary of the chapter
In the ﬁrst section, the registration process is explained. In order to collect the data it was
decided to split it into two groups and carry out the registration in two different manners.
Both of these approaches were examined in this section. For the group of data containing
the 20 largest transfer stations the registration was very detailed and each station has been
examined carefully in order to collect all attributes correct. For the remaining stations and
stops the method was more general, but still on a station based level, which secured that
the attributes assigned would still be as precise as possible. Each station was examined,
but on a more general level and assigned to the most suitable type of station. Further,
a more technical subsection regarding the stopgroups is also included in this section.
It is important to understand how the stopgroups have been created in order to fully
understand the registration process. One of the largest transfer stations, Copenhagen
Central Station, was used as an example on how the 20 largest transfer stations has been
registered to give a better understanding of the process.
==PAGE:96==
82
DATA COLLECTION
The last section provides an overview of the distribution of the collected variables. Here
it is seen how the large number of transfers between buses entails a difference in the
distribution of attributes such as shop level, shelter type, and level change between the
observed data and the choice set.
==PAGE:97==
Chapter 6
Data processing
This chapter will focus on the data processing that was necessary to do on the existing
data in order to be able add the collected data. Due to the fact that the level of detail in
the data collected in the present thesis was much higher than what had previously been
used for model estimation, the data processing was not a trivial task. The choice set was
generated on a network that differed from the one used for the map-matching, and the
alignment of these two networks demanded a lot of work which will also be described in
this chapter.
6.1
Choice set
The choice set was generated with the purpose of model estimation similar to the one
done in Anderson (2013). As described in chapter 5 this thesis focuses on transfers and
goes in depth with the attributes belonging to each transfer. In order to connect these
attributes to the existing trip attributes the following information is needed about each
transfer:
• Previously used LineVariantID.
• Next LineVariantID to board.
• StopID that the traveller arrives at.
• StopID that the traveller departs from.
The reason why such details about the transfers are needed is that the transfer attributes
can differ depending of direction of the vehicle and direction of the transfer. In the gener-
ated choice these information where not readily available and the data had to be handled
in MatLab to be able to provide information about the line variants used in connection to
each transfer. The size of the choice set is around 60 GB with almost 300,000,000 rows of
data, and it was therefore not a trivial task to handle in MatLab. The information about
the previous StopID was added from the previous line of the data using SAS.
83
==PAGE:98==
84
DATA PROCESSING
6.2
Network alignment
The network used in the map-matching of the observed routes as described in subsec-
tion 3.3.1 was the same network that was used in this thesis for data collection. However,
the network used for the choice set generation was a bit older and differed slightly from
this, which caused some problems when adding new attributes. Initially these differen-
tiations were only known to a certain extent but the more the data was used, the more
differences between the networks were revealed.
6.2.1
Updating StopIDs
In the newer network, nodes representing train stations have been split up depending
on the type of mode the stopID services. The split-ups were only done on stations were
several train service types were available. An example of this is shown on Figure 6.1
where Glostrup Station is depicted. Here it can be seen how the S-train, the regional
trains, and the buses that leave from Glostrup Station each has a separate stop-node. It
should although be noted that it is only in the model that the S-trains use a separate stop
node, when visualised all train lines use the same tracks. In the case of the network used
for choice set generation, the different type of train stops were not separated and they all
had the same stopID. This meant that the routes were generated using the same stopID
whether the traveller went by S-train or another kind of train. It was therefore necessary
to update all stopIDs that were in connection with certain line variants.
Furthermore, the F-line in the S-train network also have separate stopIDs, of course only
on stations with more lines going through them. This means that a station like Hellerup
has 3 different stop nodes for trains, that is one for regional trains, one for S-trains in
general and one for the F-train. These stop-nodes also needed updating. Finally, the train
stations with local trains passing through was also separated into several stop-nodes.
The updating of these stopIDs was rather complicated and demanded a great deal of
overview of the network. It was not possible only to update stop-nodes on the basis of
service types since both the S-train and local trains were split into several stops and the
updating therefore had to be done on line variant level.
6.2.2
Differences in change edges
As explained in chapter 3 the change edges in the data represent possible transfers where
it is necessary to transfer between two stop-nodes. These transfer edges are as mentioned
only added in places where it would make sense to do the transfer. It seems, however,
that this issue has been discussed while developing the network due to the fact that there
are found 56 transfer edges in the network used for choice set generation which does not
exist in the network used for map-matching and data collection. This meant that a lot of
registrations were missing, but even worse, that not all routes travelled in the choice set
==PAGE:99==
CHAPTER 6
85
Figure 6.1: Example of how a station (here Glostrup) is divided into several stop nodes depending
on service type.
would be possible in the observed data. To resolve the ﬁrst issue some analysis was done.
It showed that 90,000 trips were using these transfer edges and it was considered to han-
dle these similar to what was done with the trips that had transfers outside the Greater
Copenhagen Area. The load from the choice set trips on these edges were visualised and
examples of these loads can be seen on Figure 6.2. The most common difference was that
the network used in the choice set had change edges connecting a station to an additional
stop as it is seen with one stop on Valby Station and two stops on Svanemøllen Station.
The reason why these change edges had been removed in the newer version of the net-
==PAGE:100==
86
DATA PROCESSING
work was that they were redundant due to the fact that the nodes connected by a transfer
edge to stations were served by buses that all stop at the node closer to the station. How-
ever, the analysis pointed out that some of these edges were used substantially and this
fact could not be ignored.
An option for solving this issue would be to discard all OD-pairs where the percent-
age of iterations affected was above 30. This would mean taking out 121 OD-pairs and
then further discard all other trips that included a change edge only found in the choice
set. This was considered quite drastic and instead it was decided to collect data for the
missing change edges. This data collection was an expansion to the one described in
chapter 5.
Figure 6.2: Examples of loads on change edges only present in the network used for choice set
generation.
Despite this, the issue of travellers not having the exact route option in the choice set and
the observed data still remained. It is of course problematic that a traveller who has cho-
sen a given route was never presented to what is considered his or her choice set. It was
therefore assumed that it would never be an advantage (everything else equal) to use one
of the removed change edges. As mentioned above, the change edges that were removed
from the network in most cases have a travel time that is longer than the travel time on
the remaining change and the travel time saved in the bus (by alighting at a stop earlier
==PAGE:101==
CHAPTER 6
87
in the schedule) does not compensate for the longer walking time. These change edges
are used in the choice set because the variances when generating the choice set were very
high which could make travellers prefer ﬁve minutes walk instead of two minutes in a
bus. The assumption that was made here was therefore that the actual choices would
be made more rational and therefore the removed change edges would never have been
used even if they had been present in the observed routes. One of the assumptions made
when the data was map-matched was that travellers would always alight at the stop clos-
est to the station and therefore even if travellers had behaved irrationally in this case it
would not have been captured when map-matching the data. As mentioned in chap-
ter 3, it is assumed that a high number of irrelevant alternatives will not affect the model
estimation and therefore will the difference in change edges between the two networks
not affect the estimation. It should, however, be noted that on a few places, including
the stop Tingstedet near Valby Station (see Figure 6.2), the now removed transfer edge
could in some cases have been a shorter trip. This would of course affect the ﬁnal model
estimation because the chosen route could be ”worse” than a route from the choice set.
6.3
Summary of chapter
In this chapter it was presented how a large amount of data processing was necessary
in order make the choice set and the observed data ready for having added stopgroups.
Due to the fact that this thesis works with very detailed transfer data it was not possible
to simply just add the collected data to the existing choice set. It was therefore necessary
to perform some data processing in MatLab to be able to connect the data to the deﬁned
stopgroups.
Another issue that was encountered when working with the data was that the network
used for the choice set generation was slightly different from the choice used for map-
matching and data collection. One of the differences was that the stations in the network
had been altered to have a stop node for each service type found at the given station.
This meant that a substantial amount of stopIDs had to be updated depending on the
line variants that were used. A second issue that was encountered was that the network
used for the choice set contained 56 more change edges than the network used for data
collection. The way this was dealt with was to add transfer attributes to the collected data
for these previously undeﬁned transfers. However, this did not solve the issue that there
could be cases where the travellers would have route option in their choice set which had
not been present when they chose their actual route.

88
DATA PROCESSING
==PAGE:103==
Chapter 7
Model estimation
This chapter will present the results of all the models estimated in the thesis. Both the ac-
tual estimation results as well as the discussions of all the added attributes can be found
here.
As a part of this thesis one goal has been to explore the used route choice models and
explain the advantages of each of them. This is carried out in the ﬁrst part of the chapter.
Following that, is a section containing model results for a lot of the estimated models,
including models that test all attributes for heterogeneity among the travellers. Finally, a
discussion of each of the attributes and their estimated parameter values is carried out.
7.1
Theory
This section describes the theory behind a number of models used for route choice mod-
elling. First, models with Multinomial Logit structure will be presented followed by a
presentation of the Generalized Extreme Value models. Finally, a single model with pro-
bit structure will be reviewed.
The estimation of route choice models for this thesis is based on the Multinomial Logit
model (MNL) and the Path Size Correction Logit (PSC). The section is based on the writ-
ings of Prato (2009), Prashker and Bekhor (2004) and Anderson (2013).
7.1.1
Models with MNL structure
This section presents the Multinomial Logit model as well as some extensions to it.
Multinomial Logit
The MultiNomial Logit model (MNL) is an early route choice model. The original model
was developed by Luce (1959) (in Prato (2009)), where the error component follows a
Gumbel distribution. The standard Gumbel distribution is given by the cumulative dis-
tribution function, where µ = 0 and β = 1 and is presented in Equation 7.1.
89
==PAGE:104==
90
MODEL ESTIMATION
G(v) = e−e−v, v ∈R
(7.1)
The MNL is one of the most used models for estimating discrete choice models. The
disadvantage of an MNL is that is does not capture the similarity among alternatives,
why the MNL is not suitable for route choice estimation. The MNL is, however, very ad-
vantageous for use in mode choice models where the choices are mutually independent
because an assumption for the MNL is that all alternatives are independent. Another
advantage of an MNL is that it is fairly simple and the estimation of an MNL is also rela-
tively easy.
For the MNL the choice probability is given by the expression in Equation 7.2
Pk =
exp(Vk)
∑
l∈C
exp(Vl)
(7.2)
where Pk is the probability of choosing route k and Vk is the utility function for route
k. The choice probability of choosing route k is given as the the utility for route k in
proportion to the sum of the utility for all routes in the choice set C for a given OD pair.
In the discrete choice model for a multi-modal public transport network, the traveller
choose their route among a number of alternatives, where many of the alternatives will
overlap each other to some extend. Since the MNL does not take the similarity of routes
into account extensions of the MNL model has been developed.
C-Logit
Cascetta et al. (1996) (in Prato (2009)) presented the ﬁrst modiﬁcation of the MNL, called
the C-Logit. The C-Logit considers the similarity among routes within the given choice
set by a commonality factor, which measures the degree of similarity of each route com-
pared to the other routes in the choice set. The choice probability for the C-Logit is pre-
sented in Equation 7.3:
Pk =
exp(Vk + βCF · CFk)
∑
l∈C
exp(Vl + βCF · CFl)
(7.3)
where Vk is the utility function for route k, βCF is the parameter to be estimated and CFk
is the commonality factor for route k. The commonality factor captures the similarity
between route k and the other routes in the choice set by looking at the length of the
common links. Four different deﬁnitions of the commonality factor are suggested:
CFk = ln ∑

(7.7)
It applies that Lk and Ll is the length of route k and l respectively. Lkl is the length that
route k and l have in common, Γk is the set of links belonging to route k and La is the
length of link a. δal is the link-path incidence dummy equal to 1 if route l uses link a and
0 otherwise. Finally, γCF is a parameter to be estimated.
Each of the commonality factors deﬁne the similarity differently, where (7.4) only consid-
ers the common length between routes. (7.5) and (7.6) both consider the entire route and
give each link a weight according to importance proportional to the ratio between link
and route length. (7.7) also include the cost of the non-shared links.
Common for all four deﬁnitions is that they are always positive. If the commonality
factor is 0 it implies that the route is completely independent from the remaining routes
in the choice set.
Path Size Logit
Ben-Akiva and Bierlaire (1999) (in Prato (2009)) outlined the Path Size Logit (PS), where
the choice probability of choosing route k is deﬁned as presented in Equation 7.8:
Pk =
exp(Vk + βPS · ln PSk)
∑
l∈C
exp(Vl + βPS · ln PSl)
(7.8)
The Path Size Logit captures the overlapping of routes in the Path Size factor, which
reduces the utility of a route based on the number of other routes sharing the same link.
The Path Size factor is given in Equation 7.9:
PSk = ∑
a∈Γk
La
Lk
1
∑
l∈C
δal
(7.9)
where La is the length of link a and Lk is the length of route k. δal is still the link-path
incidence dummy representing whether or not route l uses link a.
The Path Size factor varies between 0 and 1 representing the amount of overlap with
other routes.
Path Size Correction Logit
The Path Size Correction Logit (PSC) takes into account the overlapping of routes by con-
sidering the length of the common links between routes. The PSC is an extension of the
==PAGE:106==
92
MODEL ESTIMATION
Path Size Logit.
The correction factor in the PSC replaces the Path Size factor in the PS. The PSC and
the PS are very similar, but the PSC is theoretically better and is presented by Bovy et al.,
2009 in Prato (2009). The choice probability for the Path Size Correction is presented in
Equation 7.10.
Pk =
exp(Vk + βPSC · PSCk)
∑
l∈C
exp(Vl + βPSC · PSCl)
(7.10)
where Vk is the utility function for route k. βPSC is the parameter to be estimated and the
PSCk is the Path Size Correction factor for route k. The Path Size Correction factor for
choosing route k is given by the following expression (Prato, 2009):
PSCk = ∑
a∈Γk
 
La
Lk
ln ∑
l∈C
δal
!
(7.11)
The Path Size Correction factor varies from −∞to 0, where a PSC factor of 0 represents
a completely independent route. A low value of the PSC factor indicates a link shared
by many routes. Compared to the PS factor, the PSC factor has a larger range. The PS
factor has an upper and a lower bound and varies between 0 and 1. Therefore the PSC
factor can indeed have a greater inﬂuence, since the value of the PSC factor only has an
upper bound and no lower bound. Intuitively it makes sense that the PSC factor is only
bounded in one end, due to the fact that it is representing the (dis)utility of having a lot of
overlapping routes. It is therefore easily recognized that there is a boundary that deﬁnes
a completely independent route with no overlaps but that no such upper bound exist for
the number of overlapping routes. Based on the ﬁndings in Anderson (2013) this thesis is
mainly focused on estimating Multinomial Logit models as well as Path Size Correction
models.
Many models have been developed further after the ﬁrst MNL model and according
to Anderson (2013), the Path Size Correction is the most suitable for route choice models
for multi-modal networks, since it is theoretically better than the Path Size Logit.
7.1.2
GEV models
Generalized extreme value (GEV) models consider the similarity among routes within
the stochastic part of the utility function. The most common GEV model is the Nested
Logit, but due to its tree structure which forces each alternative to be included in only
one nest, it is not suitable for route choice modelling and therefore the Nested Logit will
not be presented in this thesis.
==PAGE:107==
CHAPTER 7
93
Cross Nested Logit
The Cross Nested Logit allow each alternative to belong to more than one nest and was
presented by Vovsha (1997) (in Prato (2009)). A route is chosen within nests, where each
nest corresponds to a link in the network. Thereby a route is within a number of nests
equal to the number of links that constitute the route. The overlapping is captured in a
nesting parameter considering the shared links between routes. The choice probability is
deﬁned in Equation 7.12:
Pk = ∑
m
P (m) P (k|m)
(7.12)
where P (m) accounts for the marginal probability of choosing a nest m and P (k|m) ac-
counts for the conditional probability of choosing route k within nest m. The marginal
and conditional probabilities are presented in Equation 7.13 and Equation 7.14:
P (k|m) =
(αmkexp (Vk))1/µm

parameter and describes the degree of nesting for each nest. µmk is likewise deﬁned as
0 ≤µmk ≤1. It is important to notice that when the nesting parameter µml is equal
to 1 for all nests, the Cross Nested Logit acts as an MNL. The inclusion parameter αmk
indicates the percentage that link m constitutes of route k and it applies that the sum of
all inclusion parameters for a given route sum to one, as shown in Equation 7.15.

Paired Combinatorial Logit
The Paired Combinatorial Logit is another GEV model presented by Chu (1989), further
developed by Koppelman and Wen (2999) and adapted to route choice by Prashker and
Bekhor (1998) (all in Prato (2009)). A route is chosen among a number of alternatives
==PAGE:108==
94
MODEL ESTIMATION
within a choice set based on pairs of alternatives, where each pair corresponds to a nest.
For each alternative in a choice set n-1 pairs (nests) exist. The choice probability is deﬁned
in Equation 7.17:
Pk = ∑

dicates that the two routes in a pair is equal and 0 indicates that the two routes within a
pair are independent. If all pairs in the choice set has a similarity factor equal to zero, the
Paired Combinatorial Logit collapse into a MNL model.
Due to the pairwise comparison used throughout this model, the number of nests in-
creases fast concurrently with the number of alternatives within a choice set, which also
increases the computation time remarkably. It has therefore never been used for estima-
tion on real size networks.
7.1.3
Model with probit structure
Models with probit structure take into account not only the deterministic part of the util-
ity function, but also the stochastic part of the utility function when choosing an alterna-
tive.
==PAGE:109==
CHAPTER 7
95
Multinomial Probit
The Multinomial Probit (MNP) is proposed by Daganzo and Shefﬁ(1977) (in Prato (2009)).
For the MNP, the random component for the stochastic part of the utility function was
drawn from a normal distribution.
For the Multinomial Probit it applies that for a choice set containing N alternatives the
joint density of the error term has a vector of means with a length corresponding to N
and a covariance matrix with the length NxN.
The calculations of the choice probability of choosing a route among a number of alter-
natives are not that simple due to the fact that the cumulative normal distribution cannot
be expressed in a closed form. This is mainly due to the covariance matrix, which is nec-
essary in order to to calculate the choice probability. The speciﬁcation of the covariance
matrix is difﬁcult because of the error term the network parameters, which needs to be
related to the variance.
7.2
Models
This section contains a presentation of some of the models estimated in the present thesis.
The section will be built up in a way, where the models are presented chronologically, in
order to show the progress of the models. In Appendix C an overview presented as a ﬂow
chart of the estimated models can be found and provides an easy way of following the
progression in the model estimation. It is highly recommended to consult the ﬂowchart
while reading this chapter to maintain an overview of the estimated models. The results
of models found on the ﬂowchart but not in this section can be found in Appendix D. In
relation to this it should be mentioned that a very high number of models were tested
and not all made it to the ﬂow chart. A legend describing each of the estimated variables
is found in Appendix B.
First, a few MNL models were tested to provide an overview of the available attributes.
After this, the base attributes as found in Anderson (2013) were tested using a PSC
model. On top of this model the four collected attributes were tested with different
methods. These were combined into a larger model with all the parameters included. Fi-
nally, it was tested whether some parameters from the ﬁnal model tend to have a mixed
β−distribution in order to asses whether the estimated variables are perceived differently
by each traveller.
7.2.1
Reported results
All models were estimated using the software Biogeme (Bierlaire, 2003). For each of the
model estimations presented the last four lines report some basic model results that are
calculated by the software. Three of the reported results have something to do with the
==PAGE:110==
96
MODEL ESTIMATION
log-likelihood which is a measure of how well the model describes the data and the closer
to zero it is, the better the model. The ﬁrst one is the null log-likelihood which is the log-
likelihood of the model if all βs were equal to 0 and is calculated as
L0 =
∑
n∈sample
ωnln 1
Cn
(7.21)
where Cn is the choice set containing the number of alternatives available to the given
traveller n and ωn is the weight. In the models estimated in this thesis, constants are not
relevant for the utility function and the L0 is therefore an indicator of how good it would
be not having a model at all. Since all models (except one divided on purpose and one
on a smaller choice set) are estimated on the same data, the L0 will stay the same in all
estimations.
The ﬁnal log-likelihood is the log-likelihood of the sample for the estimated model. An-
other reported result is the Likelihood ratio which is calculated as
−2(L0 −L∗)
(7.22)
where L0 is the null log-likelihood and L∗is the ﬁnal log-likelihood. This test gives an in-
dication on whether the two models are different or not. In the unlikely event that they
are not different, the null-model is preferred. This test can also be used for testing two
models where one is a restricted version of the other. If the models are not different from
each other, the model with the smallest number of parameters will be preferred.
In the model estimation the adjusted rho-square is also reported and is calculated as
ρ2 = 1 −L∗−K
L0
(7.23)
where K is the number of parameters estimated. Compared to the regular ρ2 the adjusted
take the number of variables into account in order to not overestimate the ﬁt when there
are more variables. This is, though, still only an approximation.
In all models the t-tests for all parameters are furthermore stated. These numbers indi-
cates whether an estimated value is signiﬁcantly different from zero and thereby whether
the parameter has an inﬂuence on the choice probability or not. The main emphasis when
evaluating the estimated models will be put on the t-tests of the parameters. When the
models have a lot of parameters, adding an additional variable can decrease the t-test of
another and even if this remains high, the log-likelihood might decrease too. Therefore,
there are cases where it is more relevant to consider the t-tests.
==PAGE:111==
CHAPTER 7
97
7.2.2
Multinomial Logit models (MNL)
In this thesis the starting point of the model estimation was to estimate MNL models in
order to get an overview of the validity of the parameters. It was decided not to include
mode dummies as it was tested in Anderson (2013) and did not proof intuitively correct.
Furthermore, different layouts of in-vehicle time were tested and in this thesis the one that
turned out to be the best was used. This includes that the IVT for regional trains is as-
sumed piecewise linear where the ﬁrst 20 kilometres of the trip has a different marginal
utility than the following.
The ﬁrst model (model 0 in Table 7.1) only included the in-vehicle times for the various
transport modes. The deterministic part of the utility function can therefore be stated as:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
(7.24)
where βIVT,xxx is the coefﬁcient for the in-vehicle time for mode xxx that will be estimated
and IVTxxx,kn is the in-vehicle time in mode xxx for traveller n for alternatives k ∈1, 2, ...K
for the choice set Cn with K alternatives.
In the second model (model 0.1 in Table 7.1) the base attributes about transfers were added
to the previous model. This entailed:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn
+ βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βT,Wait · TWait,kn
(7.25)
where Txxx,kn is the time spent doing xxx and Nxxx,kn is the number of xxx.
==PAGE:112==
98
MODEL ESTIMATION
After these base attributes for transfers were added, all the detailed attributes for trans-
fers that had been collected (see chapter 5) were further added. This resulted in a third
deterministic part of the utility speciﬁcation, namely:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn
+ βAscends · NAscends,kn + βDescends · NDescends,kn
+ βAscendsEsc · NAscendsEsc,kn + βDescendsEsc · NDescendsEsc,kn
+ βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βS,In f o · SIn f o,kn + βS,Shop · SShop,kn
+ βT,WaitBusNone · (TWait,kn · NBusNone,kn) + βT,WaitBusSmall · (TWait,kn · NBusSmall,kn)
+ βT,WaitBusLarge · (TWait,kn · NBusLarge,kn) + βT,WaitTrainNone · (TWait,kn · NTrainNone,kn)
+ βT,WaitTrainSmall · (TWait,kn · NTrainSmall,kn) + βT,WaitTrainLarge · (TWait,kn · NTrainLarge,kn)
+ βT,WaitUnderground · (TWait,kn · NUnderground,kn)
(7.26)
where Sxxx,kn is the sum of xxx on trip level and TWait,kn · Nyyy,kn is the total wait time of
the trip multiplied with the number of transfers in shelter yyy.
The estimated parameter values of the three models described above can be found in
Table 7.1. Considering model 0 it is reassuring to see that the in-vehicle times are all nega-
tive and signiﬁcant. It is, though, counter intuitive that bus rates third best and the metro
barely better. However, since this is the very ﬁrst model, there is no reason to investigate
this further yet. The second model (model 0.1) likewise shows the correct signs on terms
of the added variables. All three transfer attributes are negative and so is the value for
access and egress time. Adding the transfer attributes also changed the values for IVT in
a way that made time spent in regional trains (the ﬁrst 20 km) the worst followed by bus.
It is clear in this model that the IVT for the metro is undoubtedly the least inconvenient
which was also expected.
Model 0.2 includes all the variables that have been collected in this project. The level
change variables that were added to model all have positive parameter values which
implies that travellers prefers climbing stairs and since the variable is a count, a trav-
eller could actually gain utility by walking up and down stairs several times. It should
though be noted that the level change variables are somewhat as expected compared
to each other. That is, that an ascend is worse than a descend and an assisted descend
causes the least disutility. However, the sign is not in accordance with microeconomic
theory and the level change attributes will be investigated further in a later model.
The shop level and the information level have both been measured as a sum where all the
shop or information levels passed by on a trip at a transfer are summed as one variable.

7.2.3
Path Size Correction models (PSC)
As described in subsection 7.2.2 this project is mainly focused on estimating Path Size
Correction Logit models based on the ﬁndings in Anderson (2013). After establishing
that the estimated values in model 0.1 in Table 7.1 all have the correct signs in accordance
with the microeconomic theory, this will be the model that the next models will be built
upon. First, the single new addition will be to add the PSC factor to model 0.1. This will
lead to the following utility function:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn
+ βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βPSC · PSCkn
(7.27)
where PSCkn is the Path Size Correction factor added to the utility function. Considering
the estimated parameters in model 1 in Table 7.2 it can be seen that they are all more or
less the same as in model 0.1. The overall model has a better ﬁt based on the increase in
the ﬁnal log-likelihood which based on a likelihood ratio test justiﬁes the addition of the
PSC parameter.

7.2.3.1
Information level
Based on the PSC model presented in Table 7.2, this part of the model estimation will
be focused on adding the parameter for information level at transfers. The variable was
registered in four levels where 0 was no confusion, 1 was little confusion, 2 was moderate
confusion and 3 was total confusion. For elaboration of the levels and what they imply,
please refer to subsection 4.2.3.
Methods
Initially it was decided to test three different approaches for estimating the parameter
value. These were:
• Sum method: The information levels on each transfer in a trip is summed to one
number.
• Max method: The maximum (worst) information level encountered at a transfer on
the trip.
• Average method: The average of all information levels at transfers encountered on
the trip.
The downside of these methods are that neither the max method nor the average method
captures the number of changes made. These methods therefore imply that a general
transfer penalty should also be estimated. Furthermore, a disadvantage of the max method
is that a route with one transfer with total confusion (level 3) and two transfers with no
confusion (level 0) is considered as bad as a route with three transfers with total confusion
(level 3). For the average method a route with two transfers with moderate confusion (level
2) are considered the same as a route with two transfers, where one is a transfer with little
confusion (level 1) and one is a transfer with total confusion (level 3). The sum method
on the other hand has the disadvantage that no upper bound can be deﬁned. Table 7.3
provides an overview of how the information level attribute is distributed with each of
the three methods. In the table it is clear how the value of the information level using the
sum method can become quite high.
Table 7.3: Minimum, maximimum and mean value for different registration methods for Infor-
mation levels (0: no confusion, 1: little confusion, 2: moderate confusion, 3: total confusion).
Observed data
Choice set
Method
Min
Max
Mean
Min
Max
Mean
Sum

As described above none of these three methods entirely seemed like a really good way of
capturing the information level and it was decided to add yet another method for testing,
namely the sum per level method:
==PAGE:116==
102
MODEL ESTIMATION
• Sum per level method: A count of the number of transfers made with each of the four
information levels.
This method would be linearly correlated to the number of transfers as the sum of the
four count variables would equal the number of transfers on a trip.
Utility speciﬁcations
The utility function for the model using the sum method will look like this:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βS,In f o · SIn f o,kn
(7.28)
Furthermore, the deterministic utility speciﬁcation of the model using the max method,
would need four dummies deﬁned:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βD,In f o0 · DIn f o0,kn
+ βD,In f o1 · DIn f o1,kn + βD,In f o2 · DIn f o2,kn + βD,In f o3 · DIn f o3,kn
(7.29)
where DIn f oX,kn is a dummy indication whether the maximum information level encoun-
tered is level X. The third method presented here would result in a utility function that
looks like this:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βA,In f o · AIn f o,kn
(7.30)
where Axxx,kn is the average value of xxx on the trip.
==PAGE:117==
CHAPTER 7
103
The last method, sum per level registers the information levels as counts as seen in the
utility speciﬁcation here:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βN,In f o0 · NIn f o0,kn
+ βN,In f o1 · NIn f o1,kn + βN,In f o2 · NIn f o2,kn + βN,In f o3 · NIn f o3,kn
(7.31)
where NIn f oX,kn is the number of transfers a traveller makes with information level X. In
Table 7.4 the estimated values of all the parameters using the four different methods can
be found.
Estimated parameters
First of all it should be noticed that all four models have an improved ﬁnal log-likelihood
compared to model 1 in Table 7.2, which indicates that these models do a better job in
explaining the data and thereby that the information level at a transfer does inﬂuence the
route choice. In a likelihood ratio test all four models prove to be different from the pre-
vious one and are therefore all preferred compared to model 1. None of the four models
seem to inﬂuence the existing parameters signiﬁcantly.
Model 2.1.1, testing the sum method, has the lowest value for transfer penalty which is
probably caused by the fact that this is the method where the information level variable
can obtain the highest value. It should be kept in mind that even if a trip has just one
transfer, the information level variable can take a value as high as 3.
In the second model, where the max method is tested (model 2.1.2 in Table 7.4), two of
the information parameters are not signiﬁcantly different from zero. This could be due
to the fact that if a trip has a maximum information level of 0 or 1 (no or little confusion)
it does not add to the disutility of a transfer. Model 2.1.3 where the average method is used
the log-likelihood is not as good as the others, probably due to the fact that only one pa-
rameter was added.
The fourth model (model 2.1.4), which is testing the sum per level method has highly signiﬁ-
cant estimated values for the information level parameters which is partly due to the fact
that these parameters replace the parameter for transfer penalty. The great advantage of
this model is that the number of transfers is implicit in the information levels and that no
knowledge about the distance between the levels on the scale is needed.
Common for all models is that when adding the information level to the estimated model

Rates of substitution
Table 7.5 presents the rates of substitution with IVT for bus as the base. This shows how
a transfer is worth around 8 minutes in a bus and hence, a traveller would rather stay 8
minutes in a bus than to perform a transfer.
The scope of this report is now to attempt to provide further detail about what part of the
transfer that creates the greatest disutility. With the variable estimated here it is seen that
in the best model (model 2.1.4) the transfer penalty varies between 8 and 13.2 minutes in
a bus depending on the level of information provided at the transfer.
==PAGE:120==

MODEL ESTIMATION
7.2.3.2
Shop level
Parallel to what was presented above regarding the information level, this section will
focus on ﬁnding the best way to add shop levels during a transfer to the data. The shop
level variable was registered 0 for several shops, 1 for one shop and 2 for no shops as de-
scribed in subsection 4.2.2. Intuitively this should have transfers with no shops causing
further disutility.
Methods
Similar to the variable for information levels, three methods of including the variables
were tested, these were:
• Sum method: The shop levels on each transfer in a trip is summed to one number.
• Minimum method: The minimum (best) shop level encountered at a transfer on the
trip.
• Average method: The average of all shop levels at transfers encountered on the trip.
As discussed with the information level only the sum method takes into account the num-
ber of transfers with shops. For the information level it did not intuitively make sense to
not have this factor included because more bad transfers should be worse than just one
bad transfer. With regards to shops, however, it is intuitively not necessarily a greater
beneﬁt to have more than one transfer with shops.
Table 7.6 presents how the three methods would distribute the shop level variable. It
is clear that the sum method has a substantially higher mean than the others which would
cause a difference in the estimated values.
Table 7.6: Minimum, maximimum and mean value for different registration methods for shop
levels (0: several shops, 1: one shop, 2: no shops).

Method

Mean
Min

Utility speciﬁcations
The deterministic part of the utility speciﬁcation changes accordingly when testing how
to include the shop level attribute. Once again the parameters are added to the model
shown in Table 7.2. For the sum method the utility speciﬁcation will therefore look like
this:
==PAGE:121==
CHAPTER 7
107
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βS,Shop · SShop,kn
(7.32)
The method where the minimum shop level is measured would have a different utility
function since a dummy for each of the three levels would have to be added:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βD,SeveralShops · DSeveralShops,kn
+ βD,OneShop · DOneShop,kn + βD,NoShops · DNoShops,kn
(7.33)
where Dxxx,kn is a dummy indicating whether the minimum shop level encountered is
xxx. The third utility function include the shop level variable as an average of the shop
levels at the transfers encountered.
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βA,Shops · AShops,kn
(7.34)
where Axxx,kn is the average value of xxx on the trip. In Table 7.7 the model estimation
results for the three methods of including shop level can be seen.
Estimated parameters
All three models improve the representation of the data based on a likelihood ratio test
against model 1 in Table 7.2. Even the minimum method despite the high number of added
variables outperforms the previous model. They also all have the expected signs on the
values. Both the sum method and the average method rely on the underlying assumption
that the scale of the shop (0-2) has the same ”distance” between the levels. In both meth-
ods having two transfers with shop level 1 would be the same as having one transfer
with shop level 0 and one with 2. Another disadvantage with the sum method is that it
has no upper bound and it is therefore a bit peculiar that the estimated value of the sum
parameter is more negative than the estimated value of the average parameter since the
sum parameter can obtain a much higher negative value than the average parameter.

On the other hand the minimum method only considers the best shop level and with this
it is ignored whether more transfers are made. As mentioned above it does, however,
seem intuitively feasible that it is not a matter of how many shops are encountered on
the trip but rather what is the best shop level encountered. In model 2.2.2 in Table 7.7 the
values for the levels 0 and 1 are positive which means that they increase the utility of a
trip. Since the upper bound of these variables is 1 and that they are only present on trips
with transfers, it corresponds to saying that having one or several shop somewhere on
the route will decrease the transfer penalty. Another fact worth noticing is that in model
2.2.2, the value for having a minimum shop level of 1 (one shop) is only signiﬁcant at a
89 % level of conﬁdence and the value could therefore be equal to zero. Despite of this
the parameter will be kept in the model since it is still unknown whether the addition of
more parameters could cause a change in the signiﬁcance level.

Rates of substitution
In Table 7.8 the rate of substitutions with IVT for bus as a base are shown. A way to
interpret these are that a traveller on a trip that already includes at least one transfer
would rather stay in the bus for 0.9 minutes more in order to have several shops available
at the transfer. And further would the traveller rather stay in the bus for 1.6 + 0.7 = 2.3
minutes if the minimum shop level on the trip was changed from no shops to one shop.
Considering the three methods that have been discussed in this section it was chosen to
continue with the minimum method. This choice is based on the fact that it has the best
log-likelihood and that it intuitively makes sense and can be argued for.

described in subsection 4.2.1. Table 7.9 gives an impression of how long the wait can be
expected to be in the different shelter types. Worth noticing is how the wait times for the
metro is signiﬁcantly lower than for other modes.

32.1
Methods
In order to test the signiﬁcance of the waiting locations in public transport route choice,
2 methods were suggested:
• Route level: The number of changes in a given shelter type as a proportion of the
entire wait time on the route.
• Leg level: The minutes spent waiting in each shelter type.
The route level method has the advantage of being extremely easy to compute whereas the
leg level method is more complicated. The main disadvantage of the route level method is
that the model does not capture that some waits are shorter than others. If a trip has two
transfers, one in a small bus shelter and one in an underground metro, the variable will
be interpreted as if the traveller spent half of the total wait time in each. As documented
in Table 7.9 this is unlikely to be true. With the leg level method the actual wait time in
each shelter type is registered and the variable becomes more precise. Even though the
model with the route level method is presented in Table 7.10 little attention will be paid to
the results due to these factors.
In Table 7.10 the results of four different model estimations are found. These are some-
what built on top of one another and will therefore be introduced as it is suitable. A
common factor for all models is that the wait time parameter which was present in Ta-
ble 7.2 is now replaced by a number of parameters that includes the wait time, but splits
it up based on shelter type. The table is ﬁrst shown and will be followed by a discussion
of each of the presented models.
