Model 2.3.1.1
Initially the shelter types were mainly divided into the actual physical appearance of the
locations for wait time which caused the metro and train stations to be considered as one.
==PAGE:126==
112
MODEL ESTIMATION
This caused the deterministic part of the utility function to look like this:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βPSC · PSCkn + βT,WaitBusNone · TWaitBusNone,kn
+ βT,WaitBusSmall · TWaitBusSmall,kn + βT,WaitBusLarge · TWaitBusLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitTrainNone · TWaitTrainNone,kn + βT,WaitTrainSmall · TWaitTrainSmall,kn
+ βT,WaitTrainLarge · TWaitTrainLarge,kn + βT,WaitUnderground · TWaitUnderground,kn
(7.35)
The result of this division can be found in model 2.3.1.1. Evaluating this it should be
noted that the log-likelihood decreases despite the higher number of parameters which
indicates that the model is not improved by adding the variables. However, all added
variables are signiﬁcant at a 95 % conﬁdence level.
Model 2.3.1.2
After it was established that model 2.3.1.1 was not an improvement of model 1 in Table 7.2
it was decided to test other combinations of shelter types. First, it was tested whether
it would make a difference to split train and metro shelters. The reasoning behind this
was mainly that Nørreport Station is actually the only underground train station in the
network and on top of that the station with the most changes. It was therefore reason-
able to believe that this station could differ from the underground metro stations. This
reasoning resulted in the following utility speciﬁcation:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βPSC · PSCkn + βT,WaitBusNone · TWaitBusNone,kn
+ βT,WaitBusSmall · TWaitBusSmall,kn + βT,WaitBusLarge · TWaitBusLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitTrainNone · TWaitTrainNone,kn + βT,WaitTrainSmall · TWaitTrainSmall,kn
+ βT,WaitTrainLarge · TWaitTrainLarge,kn + βT,WaitTrainUnderground · TWaitTrainUnderground,kn
+ βT,WaitMetroLarge · TWaitMetroLarge,kn + βT,WaitMetroUnderground · TWaitMetroUnderground,kn
(7.36)
As seen in the results for model 2.3.1.2 in Table 7.10 there turned out to be a substan-
tial difference between the estimated parameter values for the train and the metro with
the metro being around 7 times higher. In general, the parameter values for the metro
were extremely high which could be caused by the fact that the wait time in the metro is
usually really short (see Table 7.9) and hence each minute of waiting might be of higher
signiﬁcance.
==PAGE:127==
CHAPTER 7
113
Model 2.3.1.3
Taking into consideration that the the four parameters that represent transfer to a train are
physically somewhat the same, the estimated parameters are also similar. It was there-
fore tested whether the model could be simpliﬁed by adding together the train shelter
variables as one. The reasoning behind this was that they were rather similar but also
that in the clear majority of transfers to trains, the shelter is a large cover. As previously
mentioned there is only one station which is underground and the majority of train sta-
tions with no or small shelters are the local train stations where not many transfers are
made (see Table 5.3). Therefore, the third utility speciﬁcation looks like the following:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn
+ βPSC · PSCkn + βT,WaitBusNone · TWaitBusNone,kn
+ βT,WaitBusSmall · TWaitBusSmall,kn + βT,WaitBusLarge · TWaitBusLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitTrainAll · TWaitTrainAll,kn + βT,WaitMetroLarge · TWaitMetroLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitMetroUnderground · TWaitMetroUnderground,kn
(7.37)
Based on a likelihood ratio test model 2.3.1.2 outperforms model 2.3.1.3 (table Table 7.10)
but only just at a 95 % level of conﬁdence. It was chosen to continue working with the
parameters estimated in model 2.3.1.3 due to the fact that less parameters are preferred
and the difference between model 2.3.1.2 and model 2.3.1.3 is so small. It is, though, worth
noticing that adding the wait time parameters to model does not improve the overall ﬁt.
Model 2.3.2
In model 2.3.2 in Table 7.10 the route level method is tested and it actually results in a bet-
ter log-likelihood compared to the others. The route level method is only tested with the
variables corresponding to model 2.3.1.1 where train and metro are considered the same
for large cover and underground. The utility function with this method used would look
like this:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βPSC · PSCkn
+ βT,WaitBusNone · (TWait,kn · NBusNone,kn) + βT,WaitBusSmall · (TWait,kn · NBusSmall,kn)
+ βT,WaitBusLarge · (TWait,kn · NBusLarge,kn) + βT,WaitTrainNone · (TWait,kn · NTrainNone,kn)
+ βT,WaitTrainSmall · (TWait,kn · NTrainSmall,kn) + βT,WaitTrainLarge · (TWait,kn · NTrainLarge,kn)
+ βT,WaitUnderground · (TWait,kn · NUnderground,kn)
(7.38)
As mentioned earlier this method does not account for the difference in wait times in
==PAGE:128==

different shelters, and does therefore not provide as detailed data.

Rates of substitution
Table 7.11 presents the rate of substitution with IVT for bus as a base. These numbers
show, based on model 2.3.1.2, that given the fact that the traveller has a transfer he or
she would prefer to stay put in the bus for 0.3 minutes rather than wait for 1 minute for
a bus without a shelter. This also means that the person would rather wait 10 minutes
for a bus in the cover of a large shelter than to sit 1 more minute inside the bus. These
rates of substitution can seem rather small and it should therefore be kept in mind that
==PAGE:129==
CHAPTER 7
115
a transfer also includes a general transfer penalty. Furthermore, it was not investigated
whether travellers actually have a preference towards having a few minutes wait time in
connection to their transfer in order to not miss their next transport mode. This aspect is
discuss in section 7.3.4, where a piecewise linear utility function has been tested for wait
time.
In general adding the wait time parameters to the model did not seem to improve the
model in terms of the log-likelihood, but all estimated parameters are signiﬁcant at a 95
% level of conﬁdence. The speciﬁcations of wait time from model 2.3.1.3 will be kept in
the following models, since it is unsure how the combination with other parameters will
affect the estimated parameter coefﬁcients.
7.2.3.4
Level changes
Level changes are very common when making transfers in public transport and these
have therefore been included as attributes in this thesis. The variables are counts where
the number of ascends and descends are counted. In this way it is possible to go both
upstairs and downstairs in one transfer. Furthermore, data have been collected about
whether the descend or ascend is assisted, ie. with an escalator.
Guo and Wilson (2011), Raveau et al. (2011) and Raveau et al. (2014) all examine how
level change has an impact on passengers’ transfer experience as mentioned in subsec-
tion 2.1.3. Guo and Wilson (2011) examine the number of stairs, whereas Raveau et al.
(2011) and Raveau et al. (2014) examine an ascending transfer with reference to a de-
scending transfer. However, neither of the three papers indicate to have examined the
transfers combining both descending and ascending the stairs. In this thesis, a parameter
for each level change (ascend, descend ascend with escalator and descend with escala-
tor) have been estimated and thereby it is possible to add as many level changes, both
ascending and descending, as necessary to each transfer.

Model 2.4.1
Based on the estimated parameters in Raveau et al. (2011) only actual level changes were
added to the model and a same level transfer was considered to have zero inﬂuence on
the utility and is regarded as the reference level. This entails a utility speciﬁcation for the
deterministic part of the utility looking like this:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βAscends · NAscends,kn
+ βDescends · NDescends,kn + βAscendsEsc · NAscendsEsc,kn
+ βDescendsEsc · NDescendsEsc,kn
(7.39)
As seen on the estimated parameter coefﬁcients for model 2.4.1 in Table 7.13 all of the
tested attributes concerning level change are positive. As in the MNL model presented
in subsection 7.2.2 the relative values between the four parameters are intuitively correct;
an ascend adds less utility to the transfer than a descend, and the most utility is added
with a assisted descend. It is, however, due to the fact that the level transfers represent
counts and hence more is better, problematic that the coefﬁcients are positive.
Model 2.4.2
Based on the ﬁndings in model 2.4.1 above, it was decided to add a variable counting the
number of same level transfers. These transfers have so far been ignored with reasoning
in the fact that in the light of this attribute they should be representing a zero change in
the utility. The utility function would then take the following form:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βAscends · NAscends,kn
+ βDescends · NDescends,kn + βAscendsEsc · NAscendsEsc,kn
+ βDescendsEsc · NDescendsEsc,kn + βSameLevel · NSameLevel,kn
(7.40)
The estimated parameter coefﬁcients for model 2.4.2 can be found in Table 7.13, where it is
seen how adding the parameter for same level transfers caused all level change attributes
to contribute negatively to the utility except for assisted descends. It turned out that the
same level attribute is the most negative of them all. Looking at the numbers it could
seem like the same level coefﬁcient absorbs some of the previously estimated coefﬁcient
regarding number of transfers. This is supported by the fact that the correlation between
the estimated value for number of transfers and the estimated value for same level trans-
==PAGE:132==
118
MODEL ESTIMATION
fer is -0.885. It was therefore also tested how the model estimations would look like if
the parameter counting number of transfers was removed from the utility function. The
results of this test is found in model 2.4.2.1 in Appendix D. The relative value of transfers
with 2 level changes (most common in the observed data) stays the same when compar-
ing the two models. One possible explanation for the high value of the same level transfer
could be, that at lot of same level transfers are between two buses and that the coefﬁcient
represents this fact.
Model 2.4.3
Based on the ﬁndings in model 2.4.2 it was decided to test a model where the same level
parameter was split into two separate parameters, one to train and one to bus. This
caused the following utility speciﬁcation:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn
+ βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βAscends · NAscends,kn
+ βDescends · NDescends,kn + βAscendsEsc · NAscendsEsc,kn
+ βDescendsEsc · NDescendsEsc,kn + βSameLevel,Train · NSameLevelTrain,kn
+ βSameLevel,Bus · NSameLevelBus,kn
(7.41)
The estimated coefﬁcients for model 2.4.3 in Table 7.13 show that the suggestion about
transfers to buses being worse than transfers to trains was not supported. The two values
are practically the same.
Model 2.4.4
It could furthermore be discussed whether there is a difference between transfers from
bus to bus and transfers from bus to train despite the fact that they are all same level
transfers. This is therefore tested with the following utility speciﬁcation:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βPSC · PSCkn + βTrans f er · NTrans f er,kn
+ βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βAscends · NAscends,kn
+ βDescends · NDescends,kn + βAscendsEsc · NAscendsEsc,kn
+ βDescendsEsc · NDescendsEsc,kn + βSameLevel,B−B · NSameLevelB−B,kn
+ βSameLevel,T−B · NSameLevelT−B,kn + βSameLevel,B−T · NSameLevelB−T,kn
+ βSameLevel,T−T · NSameLevelT−T,kn
(7.42)
Once again the estimated parameter coefﬁcients (model 2.4.4 in Table 7.13) indicate that
there is no difference between the four different kinds of same level transfers. The fact
==PAGE:133==
CHAPTER 7
119
that the bus to bus transfers were not the reason for a high same level transfer brings up
the discussion of what the reason then is which will be considered in subsection 7.3.5.
As described above the difference between model 2.4.1 and 2.4.2 is actually not that sub-
stantial due to the fact that the value of the same level coefﬁcient is equal to the reduction
in the coefﬁcient counting number of transfers. Furthermore, it should be noticed that
the count for number of assisted descends is positive in all four models. In this network
there are no existing transfers with more than two escalators per transfer and the transfer
penalty can therefore not take a positive form. It was chosen to continue working with
model 2.4.2 because it is an advantage not to have too many parameters to estimate.
Rates of substitution
In Table 7.14 the rate of substitution is shown. Here it can be seen how the travellers
prefer to stay in the bus for 1.9 minutes rather than having an ascend on their transfer. If
it is a descend the rate of substitution is a little less.

MODEL ESTIMATION
7.2.3.5
Combining attributes
This section describes how several of the attributes are combined into one model. First,
it was tested which was the best way to add both the information level and the shop
level attributes to the model. Secondly, the wait time was added to the best of these, and
thirdly, the level changes were added to this model. Finally, this section will present how
one of the models was tested with a smaller choice set than the one that has been used
throughout the thesis. The model estimation of the four models that this section concerns
is seen in Table 7.15 and will be commented below.
Information and shop levels
As mentioned in previous sections three and four different methods were tested for shop
level and information level respectively. In both cases the best method was selected but
before that a few combinations were tested. The best methods were the minimum shop
level on the trip and the count of number of transfers with a given information level.
However both the combination of the minimum method for shop level and the maximum
method for information level as well as the combination where both attributes were mea-
sured with the average method were tested and the results can be found in model 3 and 4
Appendix D.
The methods that were considered the best were both added to the base model which
caused the utility speciﬁcation to look like the following:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βT,Wait · TWait,kn
+ βPSC · PSCkn + βN,In f o0 · NIn f o0,kn + βN,In f o1 · NIn f o1,kn
+ βN,In f o2 · NIn f o2,kn + βN,In f o3 · NIn f o3,kn + βD,SeveralShops · DSeveralShops,kn
+ βD,OneShop · DOneShop,kn + βD,NoShops · DNoShops,kn
(7.43)
where NIn f oX,kn is the number of transfers a traveller makes with information level X
and Dxxx,kn is a dummy indicating whether the minimum shop level encountered is xxx.
The resulting estimated parameter coefﬁcients can be seen in model 5 in Table 7.15. Here
it is seen how all values are signiﬁcant and in accordance with microeconomic theory.
Furthermore, this model performs better both in terms of log-likelihood and adjusted
rho-square compared to the model where only either information level or shop level was
included.

Adding wait time
After having combined information and shop level in model 5, the next step was to add
the detailed information about wait time. Several combinations were again tested and
the estimated parameter coefﬁcients of these models can be seen as model 6 and 7 in
Appendix D. The best of the tested combinations was to add wait time split into three
types of bus shelter, one type of train shelter and two types of metro shelters and then
add these parameters to model 5. This would entail the following utility speciﬁcation:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βPSC · PSCkn
+ βN,In f o0 · NIn f o0,kn + βN,In f o1 · NIn f o1,kn + βN,In f o2 · NIn f o2,kn
+ βN,In f o3 · NIn f o3,kn + βD,SeveralShops · DSeveralShops,kn + βD,OneShop · DOneShop,kn
+ βD,NoShops · DNoShops,kn + βT,WaitBusNone · TWaitBusNone,kn
+ βT,WaitBusSmall · TWaitBusSmall,kn + βT,WaitBusLarge · TWaitBusLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitTrainAll · TWaitTrainAll,kn + βT,WaitMetroLarge · TWaitMetroLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitMetroUnderground · TWaitMetroUnderground,kn
(7.44)
where TWaitxxx,kn is the wait time in minutes spend in a shelter xxx. Considering the re-
sults of the estimated parameters in model 8 in Table 7.15 it can be seen how adding the
wait time attributes affects the shop level in a way so that shop level 1 is not signiﬁcantly
different from 0. This implies that the presence of one shop can be perceived as the refer-
ence level.
Once again it is noted how adding details about the wait time leads to a decrease in
the log-likelihood despite the added variables. The adjusted rho-square also decreases
but stays at a high level. In spite of this it was decided to keep the wait time parame-
ters in the model due to the fact that they are all signiﬁcant and the values estimated are
sensible.
==PAGE:137==
CHAPTER 7
123
Adding level changes
The model described above (model 8) was a combination of information level, shop level
and wait time attributes. In this paragraph the level changes will be added to this model
which bring the following utility speciﬁcation:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βPSC · PSCkn
+ βN,In f o0 · NIn f o0,kn + βN,In f o1 · NIn f o1,kn + βN,In f o2 · NIn f o2,kn
+ βN,In f o3 · NIn f o3,kn + βD,SeveralShops · DSeveralShops,kn + βD,OneShop · DOneShop,kn
+ βD,NoShops · DNoShops,kn + βT,WaitBusNone · TWaitBusNone,kn
+ βT,WaitBusSmall · TWaitBusSmall,kn + βT,WaitBusLarge · TWaitBusLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitTrainAll · TWaitTrainAll,kn + βT,WaitMetroLarge · TWaitMetroLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitMetroUnderground · TWaitMetroUnderground,kn + βAscends · NAscends,kn
+ βDescends · NDescends,kn + βAscendsEsc · NAscendsEsc,kn + βDescendsEsc · NDescendsEsc,kn
+ βSameLevel · NSameLevel,kn
(7.45)
The results of the model estimation can be found in model 9 in Table 7.15. Earlier it was
seen how the addition of level changes to the model would affect the parameter for num-
ber of transfers, which is now replaced by number of transfers in each information level.
Therefore a similar effect is observed in this model, where the values for the information
level all decrease when level changes were added. The correlations between the informa-
tion level and the same level transfer are not at all as strong as it was between the number
of transfers and the same level transfers. The addition of the level change variables entail
an improvement in the model when considering a likelihood ratio test between the two
models despite the ﬁve added parameters.
Testing a smaller choice set
As mentioned several times in this report it can be questioned what the exact inﬂuence of
the choice set size is in a model estimation. The assumption in this thesis is, that irrelevant
alternatives will not affect the model estimation, only ensure variation in the alternatives.
There are, though, some issues with the level change attribute which are thought of as
partly caused by the large difference between the choice set and the observed data. This
will be discussed in subsection 7.3.5.
The discussion on the size of the choice set is on the verge of being out the scope of
the project and will therefore not be discussed very thoroughly, but with the data at hand
it was decided to test model 9 with a smaller choice set. The idea of creating a smaller
choice set was to get rid of insensible routes and all routes with ten transfers or more
were therefore removed from the choice set which caused a decrease in size of 25 %. The
==PAGE:138==
124
MODEL ESTIMATION
results of the estimation can be found in model 9.1 in Table 7.15. Overall it is seen that the
likelihood ratio test is best for model 9 whereas the adjusted rho-square is highest for the
model with the smaller choice set. This is due to the fact that the null log-likelihood is
smaller in model 9.1 due to the smaller choice set. The values for the estimated parameters
are in general not very affected by the reduced choice set. This is with the exception of
the PSC factor that takes on a much smaller value in model 9.1 and is furthermore highly
signiﬁcant. This indicates that when a smaller set of alternatives is offered the users pre-
fer to travel by more overlapping routes. Another attribute that is affected is the shop
level where the level without any shops is now not signiﬁcant.

Rates of substitution
In Table 7.16 the rates of substitutions scaled to IVT for bus are presented. Considering
model 9 it shows that a low information transfer without any shops and with wait time at
large metro shelter (causing 1 ascend) the traveller would prefer to stay 9.9 + 1 + 1.4 +
1.4 = 13.8 minutes in the bus rather than making the transfer. If, on the other hand the
transfer is with a high information level (level 0 ) and there are several shops available
and the wait time is under a large bus cover (causing same level transfer), the number is
only 5.2 −0.7 + 0.1 + 5 = 9.6 minutes. These calculations do, though, indicate that the
value for the same level transfer is high compared to the other level changes.
7.2.3.6
Frequency
An attribute that often inﬂuences the route choice for travellers is the frequency. Different
methods for measuring this variable was tested in Anderson (2013) and the best found to
be the largest headway met on the route. This is based on the assumption that travellers
often plan their route with the transport mode with the highest headway as a starting
point. The frequency has a piecewise linear utility function which changes the slope
whenever a headway is larger than 6 minutes. The utility function for this model would
look like this:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βPSC · PSCkn
+ βHeadway>6min · THeadway>6min,kn + βHeadway<6min · THeadway<6min,kn
+ βN,In f o0 · NIn f o0,kn + βN,In f o1 · NIn f o1,kn + βN,In f o2 · NIn f o2,kn
+ βN,In f o3 · NIn f o3,kn + βD,SeveralShops · DSeveralShops,kn + βD,OneShop · DOneShop,kn
+ βD,NoShops · DNoShops,kn + βT,WaitBusNone · TWaitBusNone,kn
+ βT,WaitBusSmall · TWaitBusSmall,kn + βT,WaitBusLarge · TWaitBusLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitTrainAll · TWaitTrainAll,kn + βT,WaitMetroLarge · TWaitMetroLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitMetroUnderground · TWaitMetroUnderground,kn + βAscends · NAscends,kn
+ βDescends · NDescends,kn + βAscendsEsc · NAscendsEsc,kn
+ βDescendsEsc · NDescendsEsc,kn + βSameLevel · NSameLevel,kn
(7.46)
As seen in table Table 7.17 adding the frequency improves the model substantially. The
only estimated coefﬁcient that is affected by the additional parameters is PSC factor
which is now positive. A positive PSC factor indicates that travellers prefer routes with
less overlap. The reason why the frequency parameter might have changed the sign of
the PSC factor will be discussed in section 7.3.

7.2.3.7
Final PSC models
In this section the ﬁnal ﬁne-tuning of the estimated models will take place. Considering
model 10 in Table 7.17 it could be noticed that not all estimated parameter coefﬁcients
are signiﬁcant at a 95 % conﬁdence level. This is the case for shop level 0 and 1, which
indicates that division in 3 shop levels is redundant, what mainly matters is whether a
shop is present or not. Furthermore are the number of assisted ascends as well as the
number of unassisted descends insigniﬁcant. This mean that on only the ”extremes”
of the level changes inﬂuence people utility. Without these four parameters the utility
function will look like this:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βPSC · PSCkn
+ βHeadway>6min · THeadway>6min,kn + βHeadway<6min · THeadway<6min,kn
+ βN,In f o0 · NIn f o0,kn + βN,In f o1 · NIn f o1,kn + βN,In f o2 · NIn f o2,kn
+ βN,In f o3 · NIn f o3,kn + +βD,NoShops · DNoShops,kn + βT,WaitBusNone · TWaitBusNone,kn
+ βT,WaitBusSmall · TWaitBusSmall,kn + βT,WaitBusLarge · TWaitBusLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitTrainAll · TWaitTrainAll,kn + βT,WaitMetroLarge · TWaitMetroLarge,kn
+ βT,WaitMetroUnderground · TWaitMetroUnderground,kn + βAscends · NAscends,kn
+ βDescendsEsc · NDescendsEsc,kn + βSameLevel · NSameLevel,kn
(7.47)
The results of this model (model 11) can be seen in Table 7.18. This simpler version of the
model maintains the same level of ﬁnal log-likelihood despite the fact that four variables
were dropped.
Considering purposes
It was furthermore tested whether model 11 would improve by being split into several
purposes. The two purposes here presented are leisure and work, where work represents
commuter trips, business trips and trips to or from education. The two models have more
or less the same data foundation, but when comparing model 12.1 and 12.2 in Table 7.18
it is clear that the model estimation for the work purpose is better. Probably travellers
who have to be somewhere at a certain point will choose their routes more consciously
and thereby more by the rules. An interesting point is that for leisure travellers both the
parameters indicating whether a shop is present and the count of unassisted ascends
become insigniﬁcant. The ﬁrst is probably due to the fact that where they are going they
will probably have the option of getting food or whatever they need. The latter can partly
be explained by the fact that the general trend is that the estimated values are lower for
leisure travellers.

Dismissing wait time
As seen in many of the above model results, adding the wait time parameters seem to
worsen the model despite the higher number of parameters. On the other hand they are
all signiﬁcant and they do seem to explain something while maintaining a rather high
==PAGE:143==
CHAPTER 7
129
adjusted rho-square and log-likelihood. However, it was decided to test the ﬁnal model
shown as model 11 in Table 7.18 without the wait time attributes. The utility function for
this model would take the following form:
Vkn =βIVT,Bus · IVTBus,kn + βIVT,LocalTrain · IVTLocalTrain,kn
+ βIVT,Metro · IVTMetro,kn + βIVT,RegIC>20km · IVTRegIC>20km,kn
+ βIVT,RegIC<20km · IVTRegIC<20km,kn + βIVT,Strain · IVTStrain,kn
+ βT,Acc/Egr · TAcc/Egr,kn + βT,Walk · TWalk,kn + βT,Wait · TWait,kn + βPSC · PSCkn
+ βHeadway>6min · THeadway>6min,kn + βHeadway<6min · THeadway<6min,kn
+ βN,In f o0 · NIn f o0,kn + βN,In f o1 · NIn f o1,kn + βN,In f o2 · NIn f o2,kn
+ βN,In f o3 · NIn f o3,kn + +βD,NoShops · DNoShops,kn + βAscends · NAscends,kn
+ βDescendsEsc · NDescendsEsc,kn + βSameLevel · NSameLevel,kn
(7.48)
In model 11.1 (Table 7.18) the results of this estimation is shown and it is clear that the
overall model is vastly improved by not splitting the wait time up into where it is done.
The adjusted rho-square as well as the log-likelihood have better values than in any other
model estimated. However, the testing of whether some of the parameters could tend to
have mixed β-distributions will be done on model 11. A small test test of whether that
wait time in this model could tend to be piecewise linear was also conducted. This is
described further in subsection 7.3.4 and the results are presented in Appendix E.
Rates of substitution
In Table 7.19 the rates of substitution for the four ﬁnal models are presented. Comparing
the model representing the purposes (leisure and work) it is interesting to see how leisure
travellers prefer to avoid transfers more. This is probably due to the fact that actual time
spend is not a major concern for these travellers.

7.2.4
Mixed modelling
In this section the dataset will be investigated further in order to see whether heterogene-
ity exist among the travellers. A mixed model have been used for this purpose, where
each attribute have been tested to see whether heterogeneity exist among the travellers
for the tested transfer attribute. The test was done on the full dataset, which was not split
into purpose in order to capture all factors. Due to time constraints it was not possible
to estimate a mixed model where the parameters that showed tendencies towards being
mixed, were estimated as such. To save time, the individual tests of the attributes were
furthermore only tested with 60 alternatives, but it was decided that this was enough for
==PAGE:145==
CHAPTER 7
131
indicating whether a given parameter might be mixed. Even with the reduced choice set
each of the estimations took 1 day and 17 hours to estimate. This high computation time
was as mentioned also the reason that it was not possible to ﬁnish estimating the ﬁnal
mixed model within the time limit.
All attributes were tested for whether they tend to follow a log-normal distribution, since
the log-normal distribution is bounded in zero. Using a log-normal distribution implies
that it is assumed that all travellers have the same perception of whether the variable
causes a decrease or an increase of the utility. Only the attribute for transfers at the same
level was furthermore tested for whether it followed a normal distribution. The reason
for this will be discussed in subsection 7.3.5. When testing this it should be noted that
the distributed numbers are randomly drawn which means that there is no connection
between the travellers that perceive the attributes the same way. It could be considered
whether some attributes would be perceived the same by travellers with similar socioe-
conomic backgrounds, but this has not been taken into account.
The attributes that have tendencies towards being mixed, are presented in Table 7.20
along with the estimated values as well as the values for converting into a normal distri-
bution.
Table 7.20: Attributes with mixed tendency tested with a log-normal distribution

7.3
Discussion of results
In this section a discussion of the estimated parameter coefﬁcients for the route choice
model will be discussed on the basis of the result from model 11 in Table 7.18. This will be
done with a discussion of each of the added variables. Initially the base parameters will
be discussed and compared to the estimates found in Anderson (2013). During the dis-
cussion of each of the estimated parameters the values will be compared to the literature
presented in chapter 2.
7.3.1
Base parameters
Some of the basic attributes estimated in this report were deﬁned based on the ﬁndings in
Anderson (2013) and it is therefore highly relevant to compare the values of the estimated
parameters in this thesis to those. In this thesis it was not tested whether the frequency
and the in-vehicle time for regional train were piecewise linear in the parameters. Fur-
thermore, it was not tested which method to use for measuring the frequency. Instead,
since the data is build on the data from Anderson (2013), it was assumed that these vari-
ables could be added the same way that they were in Anderson (2013). It should although
be noted that the choice sets the models are based on are not the same and the choice set
in this thesis has a larger number of alternatives. In Table 7.22 the estimated parameters
found in this thesis are compared to the ones found in Anderson (2013).
In general, the scales of the parameters are more or less the same. In both models the
values for local train, regional train (>20 km) and S-train are rather similar. In Anderson
(2013) this is also the case for the metro, whereas in this thesis the time value for the metro
is lower. Another difference is the value for access and egress which is substantially lower
in this thesis. In Anderson (2013) it was evident that the best way to include the headway
variable was to estimate one parameter for the ﬁrst 6 minutes, and then another one after
that. Considering the estimated results in this thesis this division of the parameter into
==PAGE:147==
CHAPTER 7

two linear parts might not necessarily produce the best model for this thesis. Overall the
two compared models are deﬁnitely considered comparable.
Mixed tendencies:
Subsection 7.2.4 describes how all IVT-parameters except for metro
have mixed tendencies. This means that for all of these, travellers perceive the time spent
differently. The value for time spent in the metro is extremely low and appears to be
perceived as that by all travellers. The transfer walk time does not seem to have ten-
dencies towards being mixed which again indicates that traveller perceive the time spent
walking between transfers the same. The access/egress parameter also has a tendency
towards being mixed, which again means that the travellers perceive the distance for ac-
cess/egress differently. This could be caused by age difference among travellers or that
some travellers tend to be more risk averse than others. Risk averse travellers might leave
their origin early in order to be sure to catch the ﬁrst transport mode, whereas other trav-
ellers leave their origin just in time. The same traveller can also be risk averse sometimes
and sometimes not, depending on their purpose. Furthermore, it is seen that headway
above 6 minutes have mixed tendencies whereas the headway below 6 minutes do not
have mixed tendencies. It makes sense, that all travellers perceive a headway below 6
minutes more or less equal, because people agree that it is a high frequency. The head-
way above 6 minutes is perceived differently among the travellers. This could be due
to the fact that the data include trips with different purpose, where travellers with the
purpose of going to work tend to value their time higher than trips with leisure pur-
pose. It is therefore reasonable to assume that people travelling for work purpose ﬁnd a
headway of 20 minutes less convenient than people travelling for the purpose of leisure.
Furthermore, people with a high income often value their time higher.
==PAGE:148==
134
MODEL ESTIMATION
Path Size Correction factor
An estimated value of the PSC factor which is negative indicates a preference towards
routes with high overlap with other routes in the choice set.
For road networks the PSC factor is generally positive (Prato, 2009) because routes with
fewer other travellers are preferred, everything else equal. In public transport however,
the interpretation of the PSC factor is not as straightforward. This is due to the fact that
a trip in a public transport system is more ﬂexible if the traveller has several options of
getting from O to D. This can both have positive effects because the traveller will not
have to plan his or her itinerary as thoroughly but also because it can avoid further delay
for the traveller if one public transport vehicle is delayed. On the other hand the links
with the most overlap are often bus routes which are not usually preferred by travellers.
The PSC factor is in this thesis slightly positive whereas it in comparison is negative in
Anderson (2013). The interpretation that travellers in public transport are more attracted
to overlapping paths is furthermore described in Hoogendoorn-Lanser (2005).
The PSC factor estimated in this thesis only became positive as from the model where
the frequency was added to the model. In the models before that it was negative. A
possible explanation to this could be that the frequency causes the same added disutility
for transport modes with the same frequency. This means that high frequent trains and
metro are compare to high frequent buses where buses usually have more overlapping
routes which perhaps pushes the preference towards less overlapping routes (trains and
metro).
7.3.2
Information level
Despite the fact that the transfers in this thesis were subject to a somewhat subjective
evaluation of their information levels, the parameters have proofed signiﬁcantly differ-
ent from each other. Of course a detailed description of how the level were applied was
produced, but with a variable like that it cannot be avoided that some form of subjectiv-
ity inﬂuences the values.
The information level has not previously in the literature been estimated in the manner in
which it was done in this thesis. In Chowdhury et al. (2013) a parameter for information
was estimated based on an SP survey for traveller at a planned and an unplanned transfer.
As described in chapter 2 the values of the parameters indicated that a traveller would be
twice as likely to make a transfer provided high-quality information compared to no in-
formation. And for the case where the respondents were used to unplanned transfers the
odds ratio was almost 3. Compared to the results in this thesis in seems sensible that the
information level at a transfer certainly inﬂuences the route choice. From the estimates
in this thesis the travellers would rather stay in the bus for 5.3 minutes if the transfer
(that they were doing either way) had a high information level compared to the lowest
==PAGE:149==
CHAPTER 7
135
information level.
It should be noted that longer transfers were often evaluated as having a worse infor-
mation level due to the fact that there would be more places to get lost. This however,
creates the risk of capturing a part of the walk time of a transfer instead of just the in-
formation level. This suspicion is supported by the fact that the walk time has a lower
rate of substitution in this thesis than in Anderson (2013) (see Table 7.22). On the other
hand that could also be explained by the fact the the walk time parameter in Anderson
(2013) is capturing more than just that. Iseki and Taylor (2009) also argue that the level
of information have an inﬂuence on the perceived walk time at a transfer, which is very
consistent with the results from this thesis.
Mixed tendencies:
Only the worst information level total confusion was tested to have
mixed tendencies. However, it has been decided to keep the parameter ﬁxed in a po-
tential future mixed model due to low number of observations for this attribute. For the
remaining tree levels of information; no confusion, little confusion and moderate con-
fusion the test indicated that travellers perceive the information level equal. It could,
though, have been expected that all levels of information had mixed tendencies since the
information level includes how familiar the traveller is with a given transfer, especially
for the higher confusion levels. Furthermore, travellers see level of information differ-
ently and younger people could tend to easier ﬁnd direction at an unfamiliar transfer
than elderly, who tends to get easier confused.
7.3.3
Shop level
Considering the shop level at transfers was ﬁrst mentioned in Anderson (2013) and then
implemented in the present thesis. Initially, three shop levels were introduced which
have been described in chapter 4. It turned out, though, that the perception of a transfer
was not inﬂuenced by whether there was only one shop or several shops at the transfer.
A reason for this could lie in the data collected in the TU survey which was described
in section 3.3. Only if the product bought in the shop is for the trip (coffee etc.) it will be
registered as one trip. A soon as the traveller buys groceries or goes shopping clothes,
which would often be the case when there are several shops, the trip will be split in two.
This means that trips where larger items are actually bought cannot be found in the data
and that might be why that no difference between one or more shops was found. It did,
however, play a role whether a shop was present or not. Actually, it turned out that trav-
ellers are willing to stay an additional minute on the bus in order to transfer at a place
where a shop is present.
The fact that there is a shop present often equals the fact that it is a larger station and
thereby has a lot of travellers transferring there. There is of course a risk that this is cap-
tured in the estimated value.
==PAGE:150==
136
MODEL ESTIMATION
An attribute that was not included in this thesis was the safety attribute. This was not
done because it was difﬁcult to measure. Especially at night it could be considered that
the station where a shop is present might feel a bit safer due to the fact that there will
always be some staff and perhaps better lighting. This means that the fact that no shops
are present decreases the utility can also be indication that transfer stations without any
shops are perceived more unsafe.
Mixed tendencies:
The presence of shop(s) does not show sign of being mixed, why
the data indicates that all travellers perceive the presence of shops the same. By testing it
with a log-normal distribution it was assumed that travellers either perceive the presence
of shop as an advantage at a transfer or simply do not care. Apparently most people
perceive the presence of a shop rather similar since no tendency towards being mixed
exist for this attribute. However, income could have an inﬂuence on travellers perception
for a kiosk, since travellers with a low income may not care as much as travellers with a
higher income, who probably visit the kiosk more frequently.
7.3.4
Shelters
In this project it was wished for to include the type of shelter as an attribute that could
possibly inﬂuence the route choice. From the beginning it was decided that the impor-
tance of the shelter would deﬁnitely depend on the time spent waiting. It was therefore
computed as the wait time of a trip split up depending on the shelter type that it was
spent at.
The wait times estimated in this project indicates that the most disutility is gained when
waiting for the metro which can be explained by the fact that the wait times for metro are
always really short, and therefore a small increase in wait time would relatively speak-
ing be quite large. Furthermore, for people who actually go by metro in the observed
data the wait time will always be small compared to the alternatives. This is supported
by the fact that Table 7.9 shows that the average wait time for the metro is around one
minute in the observed data and 8 minutes in the choice set. It was seen on the initially
estimated models that the wait time in the four different kinds of shelter related to trains
were rather similar. This meant that the wait time when waiting for trains was not split
depending on the type of shelter. The values for the bus shelters were on the contrary
kept separate and the estimated parameter values correspond to what could be expected,
namely that a wait where there is no shelter is the worst and a large shelter is the best.
Initially, the shelter types were only proposed based on their physical appearance which
for example meant that a large cover for train and metro was considered as one. It did,
however, turn out that a quite signiﬁcant difference between the the two could be found.
This was also the case for other shelter parameters that were split depending on the trans-
==PAGE:151==
CHAPTER 7
137
port mode that was served. This indicates that the attributes are not only capturing the
shelter, but that other factors might also play a role. It was furthermore seen how adding
the wait time attributes would worsen the overall model ﬁt. Despite this it was decided
to keep the parameters because they proved signiﬁcant and somewhat explainable.
A possible source of error regarding shelters could be that it is inﬂuenced by the weather.
Since it would be an enormous task to register the weather every day, everywhere, it
could perhaps improve the parameters to at least account for time of year. Perhaps a
shelter would be worth more on a windy autumn day than on a hot summer day.
Further, an interesting aspect to consider, is the wait time within each shelter type as
a piecewise linear function, since it could be assumed that the importance of cover fa-
cilities becomes more crucial when the wait time is high. That would also test whether
travellers actually prefer a few minutes wait time compared to none as suggested in An-
derson (2013). Such test can be found in Appendix E where the wait time without inﬂu-
ence of shelter type has been tested for different piecewise linear functions. The results
indicates that a piecewise linear function is a very good ﬁt for the wait time and that a
wait time between 2-4 minutes is preferred.
The inﬂuence of shelter was also estimated in Chowdhury et al. (2013) described in chap-
ter 2. In the mentioned article it was concluded that having a shelter when doing the
transfer would result in traveller being 5-12 times more likely make the transfer. It could
although, be questioned whether these ﬁndings seem realistic. On the other hand, both
the ﬁndings in the present thesis as well as in Chowdhury et al. (2013) seem to indicate
that a shelter at a transfer could inﬂuence the route choice.
Mixed tendencies:
Wait time within a given shelter type did not seem to have mixed
tendencies for any of the shelter types. This indicates that both type of shelter and wait
time is perceived equally among the travellers. It could have been expected that the wait
time had mixed tendencies due to the fact that the data include different purposes and
travellers going to work tend to value their time higher than leisure travellers since they
have to be at somewhere at a given time. Further, some people tend to be more impatient
than others, why a wait time of ﬁve minutes can seem long to some travellers and not to
others.
7.3.5
Level changes
Three level changes were found to be signiﬁcant in the present thesis; number of ascends,
number of assisted descends and same level transfers. An ascend during a transfer adds
further disutility whereas an assisted descend increases the utility. The latter can seem
counter intuitive, because it seem unlikely that having more assisted descends should be
better than just having one. In connection to this it should, though, be noted that there
==PAGE:152==
138
MODEL ESTIMATION
are not that many transfers with descending escalators in the Copenhagen region and
more importantly; the only transfers where two assisted descends are made is when the
transfer is made to take the metro. This means that the fact that the marginal utility of
having more assisted descends only play a role when the traveller is transferring to a
metro which could cause that parameter to be capturing more than just the level change.
This fact can perhaps also cancel out some of the disutility that is gained when waiting
for a metro as mentioned in the section just above.
The estimation of the inﬂuence of a same level transfer is both estimated as a very high
value and highly signiﬁcant. Intuitively this is difﬁcult to comprehend since, all other
things equal, it was expected that changing levels would be increase the disutility more
than staying on the same level. It is hard to exactly decipher why this is the outcome, but
it seems that the same level-variable absorbs part of the No. of transfers-variable. This can
be caused by two factors. One is that, as seen in Table 5.6, the same level transfers are
highly overrepresented in the choice set. The second is that the total number of transfers
is highly correlated to the number of same level transfers (0.93) which is of course partly
due to the fact that there are so many same level transfers in the choice set. It should
here be noted that once the number of transfers-variable is replaced with the number of
transfers in each information level, the correlation decreases. It is also worth noticing
that more than 70 % of the observed trip have level changes why they might seem to be
preferred. Another source of error which has been mentioned several times throughout
the thesis is the fact that none of the attributes have been included in non-transfer trips.
This might especially affect this particular attribute because several level changes could
be present in non-transfer trips for instance with train.
Due to the fact that the level changes have been set up in different manners it is not
possible to directly compare the number, but in this paragraph they will be compared
based on their relative scales and their rate of substitution. One of the main differences is
that both in Raveau et al. (2014) and in Guo and Wilson (2011) the models are estimated
with data from just one transport mode in one closed system, namely the London Under-
ground and the Santiago Metro. The fact that the trips in this thesis are multi-modal and
also include transfers between buses could perhaps inﬂuence the value of a same level
transfer.
In Raveau et al. (2014) it was found that the transfer that entails least disutility is the same
level transfer, whereas the most disutility stems from a non-assisted ascending transfer.
The rate of substitution for an ascend in the London Underground (compared to assisted
descend) is 0.83 minutes of in-vehicle time (Raveau et al., 2014) which can be compared
to 2.3 minutes of in-vehicle time for bus in this thesis. A difference here is though, that in
this thesis the ascend-parameter is a count and in Raveau et al. (2014) it is a dummy. In
Guo and Wilson (2011) the presence of an escalator increases the utility of a transfer, and
==PAGE:153==
CHAPTER 7
139
the same is the case for same-level transfers. Stairs on the other hand increase disutility
slightly.
In conclusion it should be noted that both papers seem to agree with the results of this
thesis that level changes play a role when transferring. Relatively speaking they further-
more all present an ascend as being the worst and an assisted descend as being better
than that. What is not agreed upon is the value of same level changes as discussed in the
above.
Mixed tendencies:
The attributes ascending and descending with an escalator does not
seem to have mixed tendencies. However, the attribute for same level transfers show
signiﬁcant results towards being mixed. Due to the very high standard deviation for the
equivalent normal distribution and due to the assumption that some travellers actually
prefer same level transfers, this attribute was also tested to follow a normal distribution.
Table 7.21 shows the results from where the attribute for same level transfer was tested
to follow a normal distribution, which seem to have a better ﬁt than the log-normal dis-
tribution. The results show that a small percentage (approximately 6 %) tend to prefer
a same level transfer. This could especially be walking-impaired and elderly travellers,
who prefer a same level transfer compared to transfers with level changes.
7.3.6
Aggregated transfer penalty
In this thesis the main purpose is to dissect the transfer penalty into several parameters to
be able to explain the different preferences for different transfers. However, the research
in this area is not comprehensive and it is therefore not trivial to ﬁgure out whether the
scale of the estimated parameters are in the same scale as what has been found in the
research. The comparison has, though, been made in the section above.
The research in the transfer penalty as one bulk has been present longer. In Table 2.1
it was seen how a selection of studies would estimate that transfer penalty to be between
5 and 20 minutes of in-vehicle time. In this thesis the range of transfer penalties go from
2.4 minutes of but in-vehicle time for the best transfer to 17.2 minutes for the worst.
This shows that for the best possible transfer travellers are only willing to sit 2.4 minutes
longer in the bus in order to avoid the transfer, whereas for the worst transfer, travellers
are willing to stay put in the bus 17.2 more minutes. The estimates are in line with the
estimates presented in subsection 2.1.1, although a bit to the smaller side.
Considering wait time, this thesis has also considered several types of wait time which
causes travellers more disutility in some cases than in others. The range estimated in this
thesis goes from 0.1 minute of bus in-vehicle time to 1.5 minutes. In subsection 2.1.1 Ta-
ble 2.1 substitution rate for wait time compared to in-vehicle time in other studies ranges
from 0.5 to 2.2 minutes. Once again the estimates from this thesis are in the lower end
==PAGE:154==
140
MODEL ESTIMATION
but not completely off.
7.4
Summary of the chapter
In this chapter all the model estimations were presented which amounted to 3 MNL mod-
els, 25 PSC models and further 8 PSC models presented in Appendix D. Additionally, 25
mixed PSC models has been estimated, whereas it has only been presented whether each
attribute had mixed tendencies or not. Initially, the theory behind the estimated models
was presented and here it was described how the models that will be estimated are MNL
and PSC models based on the ﬁndings in Anderson (2013). Furthermore, it was tested
for the ﬁnal model, which of the attributes which had tendencies towards being mixed.
The test was done on the full data set not separating purposes.
The models were then presented with the structure that was laid out on the ﬂow chart
in Appendix C. The three MNL models provided the ﬁrst overview of the attributes and
conﬁrmed that in general they had the correct sign and were in a correct relative order.
After that the base PSC model was presented which improved the model ﬁt.
The attributes were added one by one and each time several methods for how to include
the variable were tested and the best one kept. For the attribute concerning the infor-
mation level it was decided to measure the variable as the number of transfers within
each of the four information levels on a trip. This method was the one providing the
highest level of detail in the data. The shop level attribute was also tested and it was
found that the best way to describe this was to include the best shop level found on a
trip. As opposed to what was assumed regarding information level it was here regarded
unimportant whether a shop was present several times during a transfer. The attribute
concerning shelter was assumed highly relevant with regards to the wait time. This was
therefore registered to replace the total wait time and instead split out depending on the
shelter type where the waiting was done. The ﬁnal added attribute was the level change
attribute. Here, it was realised that in order to obtain negative values for level changes, a
dummy-variable counting same level changes had to be added.
After all four attributes had been tested separately it was now time to combine them.
Several combinations were tested but the best was with the methods described above.
Adding more variables to the model in general improved the ﬁt of the model except
when the wait time attributes were added. It was however decided to keep them because
they were signiﬁcant and explained some of the data.
The model where the four attributes were all included was also estimated with a smaller
choice set. This smaller choice set was generated by reducing the original choice set to
only contain trips with less than 10 transfers. The only parameter values that changed
==PAGE:155==
CHAPTER 7
141
signiﬁcantly caused by the smaller choice set were the PSC factor and the shop parameter.
The ﬁnal model that was tested was also split into the purposes of leisure and work.
These estimations show a general picture that travellers who are going to work has a
higher preference for time saving and are thus more likely to perform a transfer to save
time. Most parameter values were more signiﬁcant for the work travellers and the over-
all model had a signiﬁcantly better ﬁt. This is probably caused by the fact that travellers
with non-leisure purpose travel more by the rules.
Furthermore, the data was tested to see whether heterogeneity exist among the travellers.
Each attribute was tested individually to see if the perception for each of the attributes
tend to follow a log-normal distribution. The log-normal distribution was chosen due to
the fact that the distribution is bounded in zero. The following attributes showed ten-
dencies towards being mixed:
• IVT Bus
• IVT Local train
• IVT Regional trains up to 20 km
• IVT Regional trains above 20 km
• IVT S-trains
• Access/Egress
• Headway above 6 minutes
• Information level 3 total confusion
• Same level transfer
However, due to a very low number of observations for information level 3, it was de-
cided to keep this parameter not distributed for a potential future mixed model.
Following the ﬁnal model estimations a thorough discussion of each of the added at-
tributes was conducted.
==PAGE:156==
142
MODEL ESTIMATION
==PAGE:157==
Chapter 8
Application of results
The present chapter considers the options for applying the results discovered throughout
this thesis. Three different contributions will be discussed; how do the results enrich the
general knowledge of the ﬁeld of research, how the results can be used to improve route
choice modelling, and how the results can help provide more information for policy-
makers in order to plan and improve transfer terminals. These three types of applications
of course go hand in hand as it will often also be relevant for policy-makers to apply route
choice models for decision support. However, the implementation phase of adding the
the estimated results to a route choice model can be rather time demanding and the point
is therefore that the gain of general knowledge in the area can beneﬁt planners too. The
applications will be discussed on an overall level in order to to give an idea on how the
results can be used and/or implemented. This will be supported by a few examples
where possible actions will be proposed in order to minimize the transfer penalty.
8.1
General understanding of transfer penalties
The research in the ﬁeld of transfer penalties in route choice models has until now been
limited and therefore results are valuable in themselves. The reason for this being that
the estimated parameter values alone contain valuable information for planners. It is im-
portant to have knowledge on the ﬁeld and even just the estimates can point planners in
the right direction when they are planning a transfer with regards to, for instance, how
much an escalator is worth compared to a shorter walking distance.
In this section it will therefore be discussed how the results found throughout this thesis
can be used for improving terminals in order to increase the number of users for the pub-
lic transport system. In chapter 7 the results show that all tested attributes (information,
shop, wait time in shelter, and level changes) inﬂuence on how passengers perceive a
transfer. For the attribute concerning shops it turned out only to be important whether a
shop is available or not and the number of available shops was not signiﬁcant. For level
changes only the two extreme values; ascending and descending with escalator had an
143
==PAGE:158==
144
APPLICATION OF RESULTS
inﬂuence along with the same level transfer variable. A descending escalator have a pos-
itive estimated parameter, which most likely describes travellers preference for metro as
discussed in subsection 7.3.5 but also that assisted level changes are preferred.
Even without the implementation in a route choice model this knowledge can be used
to improve transfers.
8.1.1
General planning perspective
When planning public transport a lot of effort is usually put into ﬁnding the best route,
the best locations for stops, and the best connection to the existing network. A lesser
effort has been put into how the transfers should be designed in order to minimize the
transfer penalty. This thesis seeks to quantify which investments that are the most reason-
able when it comes to improving transfers. In terms of the deﬁnition in Chowdhury et al.
(2013) of planned and unplanned transfers, these estimates can help transport planners
gain knowledge about what a planned transfer is. Transport planners could use the esti-
mated results for improving the design of already existing planned transfers and further
to create new planned transfers. For a description of planned and unplanned transfers,
see subsection 2.1.2.
The estimates can also be a tool for future projects, where the aim is to improve the
terminals. According to the results small changes can lead to a gain in utility for trav-
ellers using the public transport system. Changing travellers perception of a transfer,
by improving the transfer according to the results found throughout this thesis, will not
only beneﬁt existing users of the public transport system, but also increase the number
of users in the public transport system.
Improvement of terminals and stops is highly inﬂuenced by the policy-makers, why it is
important to provide the policy-makers a reason to make these improvements. Not all of
the examined attributes are considered policy-variables, but shelter type and availability
of escalator can bot be inﬂuenced by the policy-makers. The availability of shops is also
affected by the policy-makers, but a considerable part of opening a shop also depends on
proﬁt. Furthermore, opening a shop requires an investor, staff and ongoing work, where
putting up a shelter to cover a waiting area is more of a one time investment. The level of
information can also be inﬂuenced by policy-makers in the sense that they can have an
inﬂuence on the information screens and information signs put up at a given station.
The fact that most of the variables can be considered policy-variables means that it is
actually possible for the policy-makers to improve these attributes and thereby minimize
the disutility perceived by travellers. By increasing the level of the attributes with a
high impact on how travellers perceive transfers, it would most likely be possible to plan
==PAGE:159==
CHAPTER 8
145
Figure 8.1: An example of a large transfer location (Ryparken Station) where two small bus shel-
ters were put up recently. Here it would have been very relevant to consider a large bus shelter
and thereby minimize disutility.
which stations would be the large transfer stations. In a larger perspective this could
mean a decrease in the overall disutility that a traveller experiences and cause a shift to
public transport.
This section leads to the conclusion that the present thesis contribute to the general
knowledge in the area of transfer penalties which is both valuable for modellers but also
for transport planners even before the results are implemented in a transport model. The
following section will focus on the implementation in a transport model and following
that a more elaborated description of how the transport planners can use the results will
be presented along with a few examples.
8.2
Improving route choice models
When planning infrastructure investments it is important to be able to forecast the so-
cioeconomic consequences of the investment. This is important in order to support the
decision making carried out by politicians. However, no one can predict the future and
sometimes the forecasts are not entirely correct which is often pointed out by critics. The
general opinion is, though, that trafﬁc models play a role as an important tool that should
be interpreted with care. For trafﬁc researchers the main goal is therefore to improve the
models as much as possible in order to provide detailed analysis of the projects. A main
contribution of this thesis is therefore considered to be the improvement of route choice
==PAGE:160==
146
APPLICATION OF RESULTS
models for public transport. This section will describe how the results from this thesis
can be implemented in NTM in order to improve route choice modelling in a more tech-
nical manner.
The results of this thesis show that a transfer is perceived differently depending on a
number of attributes. Some of the attributes are linked to the walking time, whereas oth-
ers are linked to the waiting time within a given transfer. Common to all of them are that
they contribute to the explanation of what makes a transfer more or less inconvenient.
As described in the above, these results can be very valuable for route choice modellers
in order to be able to forecast route choice for public transport better. Therefore, it would
be useful to implement these attributes in the Danish National Transport Model (NTM),
so that they could be included in route choice modelling for public transport. Hope-
fully, this would result in a more precise forecasting, because the explanation of what
matters to public transport travellers would be more detailed. In this section a review
of how such implementation could be conducted is provided, and since NTM has been
presented in chapter 3 this section will not go into depth with the structure and functions
of the model.
8.2.1
Implementation in NTM
Below are the attributes listed according to whether they are linked to the walking time
or the wait time at a transfer.
• Attributes connected to the transfer walk:
– Information level
– Level changes
• Attributes, which are linked to the waiting time:
– Shop level
– Shelter type at stop where next transport mode departs.
In the Danish National Transport Model public transport routes consist of stop-nodes, line
variant elements and change edges. The stop-nodes correspond to a given stop for a given
service type, meaning that at a given station, there will be a number of stop-node cor-
responding to the number of service types serving this station. The line variant element
indicates the route of a given service type and links all the stop-nodes together. The
change edges deﬁne possible and meaningful transfers between stop-nodes and are most
often used at stations. A number of attributes are already attached to each stop-node,
change edge, and line variant element to describe the impedance of these.
There are three different situations for which it would be interesting to implement the
attributes regarding transfers in NTM:
==PAGE:161==
CHAPTER 8
147
A Transfers within the same stop-node. That is, transfers within the same system (e.g.
bus-bus or train-train).
B Transfers between two stop-nodes, represented by a change edge.
C First stop on the route, where the traveller access the ﬁrst stop by a private transport
mode (car, walk, bike etc.).
The three situations are sketched in Figure 8.2, where situation A is the pink route indi-
cating a route with a transfer within same stop-node from bus to bus. Situation B is the
green route, indicating a transfer between train and bus and thereby a transfer between
two stop-nodes. Last situation C is the blue route, which implies a route where the trav-
eller arrives at the station, which is the ﬁrst stop on the route. The broken line indicates
the part of the transfer where the traveller does not use a public transport mode. This
can either be, when the traveller access the station for the ﬁrst stop (situation C) or the
walking distance between two transport modes (situation A and B).
The change edges in NTM between two stop-nodes indicate meaningful and possible
transfers and represent a transfer in both directions. This means that if a change edge
between two stop-nodes exist, the traveller can perform transfers both in the backward
and forward direction using the same change edge. However, during the data collection
for the study of this thesis is was clear that the attributes between two stop-nodes could
vary depending on the direction of the transfer. A simple example could be a transfer
from bus to train, where the travellers will have to ascend the stairs in order to reach
the platform where the train is departing. In the opposite direction, from the train to the
bus, the traveller would have to descend in order to reach the bus stop. Even though this
example is between the same two stop-nodes, the attributes for level change are different
for the two directions. It will therefore be necessary to distinguish between the directions
in order to assign the correct attributes to each transfer. This will only apply for transfers
between two stop-nodes (situation B). For a transfer within the same stop-node (situation
A) all attributes can be assigned to the given stop-node. Lastly, situation C, would need
a solution similar to solution A, where the attributes are assigned to the stop-node ﬁrst
met on the route, provided that these attributes are only considered when it is the ﬁrst
stop of the route.
As described in the above an implementation of the found results in the Danish Na-
tional Transport Model would not be trivial, but it would deﬁnitely be possible. This
implementation is not within the scope of this project due to limited time but should be
included in future work.
==PAGE:162==
148
APPLICATION OF RESULTS
Figure 8.2: Example of different transfer types at Hellerup Station.
8.3
Improving transfer planning
The two sections above have provided some thoughts on how the results found could
be applied. The ﬁrst section focused on the fact that the estimations in this thesis causes
a gain in the general knowledge on the ﬁeld of transfer penalties. In the section above
==PAGE:163==
CHAPTER 8
149
the importance of detailed route choice models in public transport was described and
an outline of how the implementation of the estimated results should be carried out was
presented. Improved route choice models are of course important due to the fact that
planners can use them as a prediction tool to evaluate future infrastructure projects. And
that is what this section will concern.
The estimated models in thesis indicate that improving the examined transfer attributes
will lead to a decrease in the disutility for passengers travelling with public transport.
The use of transport models when evaluating a new infrastructure project is crucial and
therefore it is of utter importance that the models can make predictions as precise as pos-
sible. The following section will consider some of the actual cases mentioned in chapter 2.
This will be done with the parameters estimated in the thesis as a foundation to exem-
plify the use of the estimated parameters. Of course the parameters should have been
implemented in a route choice model, but the section below will just consider the change
in utility that can be caused by the changes based on the estimated parameter values.
8.3.1
Real case examples revisited
This section will brieﬂy describe the cases of Taastrup and Herlev Stations in the light of
the parameter values estimated.
Taastrup Station
Taastrup Station is the only complete project out of the real case projects presented in
section 2.2. The renovation of Taastrup Station included among other things improve-
ments of the bus facilities, such as moving the bus terminal approximately 200 meters
away from Taastrup Station. Furthermore, the renovation lead to a number of changed
attributes. Table 8.1 below provides an example on how the facilities and attributes for a
transfer between train and bus have changed after the renovation.
Table 8.1: Attributes for transfers between train and bus before and after the renovation.
Before renovation
After renovation
Attributes
Train-bus
Train-bus
Walking distance
50 meters
200-250 meters
Shelter at bus stop
No cover/Large cover
Large cover
Information level
Easy to ﬁnd buses in front of station, but
confusing to a ﬁnd speciﬁc bus line since
each bus had separate bus stops, consid-

Relatively easy to ﬁnd buses due to in-

In this speciﬁc case, the results (see subsection 2.2.1) from the passenger analysis before
and after the renovation indicated that the distance between train and bus plays a sig-
==PAGE:164==
150
APPLICATION OF RESULTS
niﬁcant role with regards to the average number of passengers per month transferring at
Taastrup Station. The example from Taastrup Station gives a clear picture of how chang-
ing a number of attributes inﬂuence passengers’ travel behaviour in the public transport
system, which further support the estimated results derived throughout this thesis. The
estimated results show that information level have a great inﬂuence on how passengers
perceive a transfer. When the bus terminal was moved away from Taastrup Station, a
number of information screens were put up in order to compensate for the longer walk-
ing distance. The information screens improved the information level at the transfer, why
it is considered level 1 after the renovation. However, from the results of the passenger
analysis it seems that an increase in level of information was not enough to compensate
for the longer walking distance, which still seem to have the highest impact on the pas-
senger load.
Furthermore, it did not seem to have an impact that the number of shops increased from
one to several. The latter was also the case when considering the estimated results in this
thesis. The utility for the transfer was calculated with the estimated parameter values
from this thesis for the situations before and after the transfer. The input for this is pre-
sented in Table 2.9. The result of the calculations was that the the renovation had caused
a decrease in the utility which is in accordance with the decrease in passenger loads pre-
sented in subsection 2.2.1. From the estimated values in this thesis it can be calculated
that improving the information level from 2 to 1 will only compensate for a 1.8 minutes
longer walk time. The complication in this is of course that the presence of walk time
and wait time is neglected because these are hard to compare in two scenarios. A longer
walk time may for example cause a shorter wait time but it can also make the traveller
miss the connection. These issues are of course part of the reason why it is more relevant
to use the parameters as an input to a route choice model.
Herlev Station
Without going into details, this paragraph will brieﬂy describe how the estimated num-
ber can be used for assessing the need of a smoother transfer at Herlev Station at de-
scribed in subsection 2.2.2.
The change that is considered at Herlev Station is to build a stairway that leads directly
to the bridge where the bus stops are. If the change were to be evaluated based on the
estimates from this thesis, it would imply that in total there would be one less ascend or
descend, depending on direction. Furthermore, the transfer walk time would decrease,
and ﬁnally, the confusion would be smaller due the fact that the bus top/train Station
would be visible from the stop where the traveller alights. These are all factors that (ex-
cept for the descend) would be evaluated as an increase in the utility for the transfer and
thereby a larger willingness to make a transfer at that exact place.
==PAGE:165==
CHAPTER 8
151
Both examples described, indicated that it is hard to consider an entire transfer just by
considering the estimated parameter values. For this a route choice model is needed. In
both examples the changes in walk time can cause a traveller to catch another connection
than previously and a model is needed to consider the effects of these changes. How-
ever, the examples merely sought to indicate how the contributions of this thesis could
be used.
8.4
Summery of the chapter
The application of the results of this thesis can be considered in three steps. Firstly, the es-
timated parameter values were described in the light of adding to the general knowledge
on the ﬁeld of transfer penalties in route choice models. Secondly, the implementation of
the estimates in the Danish National Transport Model was discussed where the technical
parts were brieﬂy touched upon. Thirdly, it is the described how transport planners can
use the route choice model as a prediction tool in public transport planning.
The chapter describes how planners can make use of the knowledge that the thesis pro-
vides about preferences just by considering the estimated parameter values. This can
help to deﬁne what a planned transfer is compared to an unplanned transfer. In the fu-
ture, in the planning of new public transport routes and Stations, this knowledge will
probably be applied.
The concept of how the data could be implemented into a transport model was further-
more described. It is, though, not within the scope of this thesis to actually implement
and test how the values would affect the route choices in public transport.
The ﬁnal section focuses on the future application of the results once they have been
implemented in route choice models. Despite the fact that they are not yet implemented,
the examples from section 2.2 are shown and the change in utility based on the esti-
mates from this thesis are considered. The estimated results indicate passengers’ travel
behaviour and how they perceive a transfer, which can be used by the policy-makers in
order to improve transfers and thereby lessen the disutility for travellers within the pub-
lic transport system. The gain in utility can both beneﬁt the existing passengers within
the public transport system as well as increase the number of users for the public trans-
port system.
