152
APPLICATION OF RESULTS
==PAGE:167==
Chapter 9
Discussion and perspectives
The subject of this thesis is transfers in public route choice models. Recalling the division
of planned and unplanned transfers the main goal of this thesis has been to deﬁne what
is important when a planned transfer is wanted. This has been done through estimation
of route choice models where more attributes than the regular transfer penalty, trans-
fer walk time, and transfer wait time have been estimated. There are, though, several
components of this thesis that should be discussed and this will be done in the current
section. On an overall level the exact results from a model estimation should always
be questioned. One of the main assumptions behind these models are that the traveller
posses a full overview of the network and is aware of all possible alternatives and know
their preferences. The stochastic part of the utility function is then added to account for
the fact that this assumption might not hold and that the modeller does not know the
utility of the traveller. However, this chapter will not focus on the modelling issues that
are inherent to the models used but rather on the speciﬁc issues that were encountered in
this thesis.
Registration
The selection of attributes was done on the basis of four chosen criteria that were used to
evaluate a long-list of attributes. Several of the attributes were not chosen due to the fact
that the registering of them would require a visit to all stations. A large amount of time
spent on data collection was not a possibility in this project. However, it might have pro-
vided more details about the transfer penalty to have included variables such as safety,
security, occupancy, and seats at a station. Whether these attributes would contribute to
the explanation of what makes a transfer a burden is difﬁcult to say, but it would have
been possible to test them if the time frame had been larger.
The method used for data collection was to thoroughly register all possible transfers at
the 20 largest transfer stations and for the remaining transfer stations and stops a more
general, but still station based, method was used. For the remaining stations and stops,
each station/stop was put into the most suitable station type. The different types of
153
==PAGE:168==
154
DISCUSSION AND PERSPECTIVES
stations could include whether the platforms are elevated or not, what kind of trains it
services and how the shelters look. This entailed that the level change and the shelter
attributes were registered correctly, but both information level and shop level were as-
signed with the values that were most likely. It is worth considering that the 20 stations
with the most transfers in the observed data made up for 65 % of all transfers whereas the
same 20 stations only made up for 46 % of transfers in the choice set. This means that the
uncertainty of the registration is even larger in the choice set. A source of error is there-
fore that some of the registered data is approximated which can, of course, affect how
the attributes are valued. If time had allowed for it, detailed registrations of all stations
would have been possible.
Choice set
In general, it is worth noticing how the observed data differ signiﬁcantly from the choice
set. This was assumed not to inﬂuence the model estimation since irrelevant alternatives
would only contribute to the variance of the data. When one of the models were tested
with a reduced (and supposedly more sensible) choice set it did, however, not affect the
estimation very much. As it will be discussed below, somehow the large amount of in-
sensible transfers in the choice set are expected to play a role. A general discussion about
choice sets is to consider whether only the subjective choice set should be included. In
reality travellers probably only consider maximum 4-5 different routes. However, in An-
derson (2013) several choice sets were tested and the one that produced the best results
was the one with the most variance.
As described in chapter 6 there were some issues that had to be addressed due to the
fact that the choice set generation was done on the base of a network that was different
from the one used for map-matching the observed data. This caused a problem with the
change edges in the network which meant that more routes were available in the choice
set than in the observed data set. This can have distorted the model estimation slightly if
there are cases were the ”best” route is available in the choice set but not in the observed
data. Overall it is not suspected to inﬂuence the model estimations. It could, however,
since it is assumed that the traveller will always choose the best route in terms of utility
and if the best route is not the one outlined in the observed data it would affect the model
estimation.
Network
The public transport network of Greater Copenhagen Area was described in chapter 3,
where an example was provided for a given OD-pair, where it was possible to choose
trips with and without transfers. The layout of the area based on the Fingerplan and
the high number of different transport modes provides several options for route choice
in the Greater Copenhagen Area. However, for some routes there are not necessarily
any alternatives to the chosen route that actually includes transfers. It can be speculated
whether the network presents the traveller with enough alternatives to actually consider
==PAGE:169==
CHAPTER 9
155
other routes. A network like the London Underground which has a more dense grid
would offer travellers more route options. However, the results point towards the fact
that transfers do play an important role in route choice and the network used does not
seem to inﬂict on that.
PSC factor
Most of the models were estimated using the Path Size Correction Logit where the PSC
factor is included in order to account for similarity across alternatives. In road trafﬁc
modelling the PSC factor is, based on the theory, always positive. This is due to the
fact that routes that share links with many other routes are less desirable for car drivers
which intuitively makes sense. For public transport route choice models, the meaning of
the PSC factor is not as straightforward. In Anderson (2013) and Hoogendoorn-Lanser
(2005) it was estimated to be negative which means that travellers prefer routes with
a high number of overlaps. This can be explained by the fact that having overlapping
routes in public transport also entail a higher degree of ﬂexibility in case of delays. On
the other hand, the links that usually have the highest degree of overlaps are often bus
routes where several buses share routes between the same stops. And in general, buses
are not preferred compared to trains.
In this thesis the PSC factor was negative (ie. travellers prefer overlapping routes) until
the parameter measuring frequency was added after which it became positive. In the
ﬁnal model, the PSC factor is quite small and is therefore not weighted high in the utility
function. However, it is positive which indicates that travellers prefer routes with less
overlap. This could, as mentioned just above, implicitly explain travellers preferences for
train and metro compared to bus. The fact that the PSC factor is positive in this thesis
and negative in Anderson (2013) and Hoogendoorn-Lanser (2005) raises the question of
how relevant the PSC factor is in public transport route choice models. In a way trips in
public transport models are done in ”pipes” where one traveller does not affect the other.
However, this is not the case in reality and therefore travellers might be affected by the
number of overlapping routes. From the estimations in this thesis it seems that the PSC
factor is not important but it indicates a preference towards route with less overlapping.
Attributes
The ﬁnal model contained parameters for all four tested attributes. The estimated values
were compared to the literature presented in chapter 2 but it is, however, worth notic-
ing that it was not possible to directly compare the results. This is due to the fact that
the methods used both for measuring the attributes and for estimating the models differ.
Furthermore, it is not always straightforward to decipher what methods that have been
used in the literature. However, on an overall level it seems that the estimated parameter
values are consistent with previous estimations in the literature.
There are, though, some issues with the estimated parameters. One is, that adding shelter
==PAGE:170==
156
DISCUSSION AND PERSPECTIVES
type to the parameter for wait time does not improve the overall model. The estimated
values are, though, signiﬁcant and compared to each other they do make sense, e.g. wait-
ing for a bus without a shelter is considered worse than waiting for a bus with a large
cover.
Furthermore, the level changes are positive relative to a same level transfer. This issue was
discussed thoroughly in the report and several models were tested to see how it could
be explained. There are probably four issues that could partly cause this. Firstly, the
network is multi-modal which means that transfers between buses and trains both rep-
resent a same level transfer. Secondly, the representation of same level transfers is much
higher in the choice set than in the observed data which means that most transfers in the
observed data include level changes. Because of the fact that the number of same level
transfers in the choice set is so high it also turns out that there is a strong correlation
between the number of transfers and the number of same level transfers on a trip. This
entails that a part of the transfer penalty is captured in the same level parameter which
is the third issue that should be considered. Finally, it should be considered is that level
changes are also often included in trips without any transfers. The fact that these level
changes are not registered could perhaps distort the variable. In general the effect of
not having registered any of the attributes for non-transfer trips could be discussed. This
means that the reference is a trip without a transfer and the fact that it is attempted to reg-
ister details about transfers complicates that. Adding the level changes for non-transfer
trips would be straightforward but it is uncertain whether the parameter would still mea-
sure the effect that level changes has on transfers. This leads to a discussion about how
it would be an important task to add attributes describing the access and egress parts of
trips in future works.
It should be mentioned that the attribute considering shops only turned out signiﬁcant
about whether a shop was present at a transfer or not. This could be due to the the fact
that if a traveller goes grocery shopping during a transfer, the trip will be split into two
separate trips. With some data processing it might somehow be possible to include these
trips as journeys and this could possibly affect the estimated shop parameters.
The information level turned out to have quite an inﬂuence on the transfer penalty, de-
spite the fact that it was evaluated subjectively. The fact that the information levels did
not have mixed tendencies indicates that travellers actually perceive information level
similarly. Is was seen, that when level changes were added to the model, the same level
parameter captures a part of the disutility from each of the four information levels. This
is caused by the fact that the information level substitutes the parameter for number of
transfers. The relation between number of transfers and the same level transfer is dis-
cussed just above.
==PAGE:171==
CHAPTER 9
157
Mixed attributes
All parameters were tested for mixed tendencies in separate models. Only IVT for bus,
local train, regional trains and S-trains seem to have mixed tendencies along with ac-
cess/egress, headway above 6 minutes, and transfers without level changes. It should,
though, be mentioned that the attributes were tested without considering trip purpose or
socioeconomic aspects, which could have inﬂuenced the results. It would have been in-
teresting to take these two aspects into account when testing for heterogeneity among the
travellers in order to capture the heterogeneity within the different groups of travellers
that uses the public transport system.
9.1
Perspectives and future work
This thesis can be considered a starting point for the elaboration of existing research re-
garding public transport route choice and transfer penalty. Based on the attributes al-
ready considered in this thesis an obvious continuation of this would be to estimate a
mixed model where the attributes that showed tendencies towards being mixed were
added as distributed parameters. This model has already been created but the long com-
putation time means that the estimation in Biogeme (Bierlaire, 2003) is still running while
this thesis is being ﬁnished.
As described in chapter 8 the parameter estimates could also be implemented in a route
choice model like the Danish National Transport Model in the future. This would hope-
fully entail more precise predictions of travellers’ route choices. However, as already
mentioned, an obvious addition to the data, before including it in the Danish National
Transport Model, would be to include attributes concerning the access and egress of trips.
This leads to how a relevant expansion of the present thesis would be, to add data con-
cerning access and egress. This could be done by registering some of the attributes ex-
amined in this thesis for the access and egress as well. For instance, on a trip without
any transfers a traveller could still experience several level changes at the access and
egress stations. However, it should be discussed whether these could all be added in the
same model or whether the two models should be somehow detached from one another.
Where the transfer attributes can inﬂuence the travellers’ choice of where to transfer,
the access/egress attributes would inﬂuence where to board and alight the ﬁrst and last
transport mode. However, these two choices could in principle be interdependent of one
another.
Other attributes such a bike parking facilities could furthermore be added as an attribute
for access and egress. This would demand for an expansion of the data collected in this
thesis. Such data would help describe travellers’ preferences regarding facilities at access
and egress stations.
==PAGE:172==
158
DISCUSSION AND PERSPECTIVES
Seeing this thesis as a starting point for further investigation in the details of transfer
penalties it could also be considered to add more attributes to the model. The long-list of
attributes presented in chapter 4 could be relevant for this purpose.
==PAGE:173==
Chapter 10
Conclusion
From a traveller’s point of view, transfers are perceived inconvenient and lead to an in-
creased disutility of a trip in public transport. This statement is based on the numerous
studies where the existence of the so-called transfer penalty is evident. Much less atten-
tion has been paid to research on why the traveller penalty exists.
The focus in this thesis was to evaluate a number of attributes in order to determine
whether these could play a role in the route choice of travellers. All four evaluated at-
tributes actually turned out signiﬁcant for the travellers. This means that not all trans-
fers are perceived equally bad. Based on the estimations a traveller is willing to spend
2.4 minutes in a bus to avoid a good transfer and up to 17.2 minutes to avoid the worst
transfer. This is a substantial difference that supports the fact that some transfers are not
perceived as bad as others.
The information level at a transfer is rather important to the travellers and the results
show that travellers strongly prefer a transfer where navigating between the two trans-
port modes is fairly easy. Furthermore, a transfer is much worse if there is no access to
any kinds of shops at the transfer location. Level changes at a transfer can also inﬂu-
ence the perception; where an unassisted ascend adds to the disutility of the transfer,
an assisted descend on the other hand reduces the inconvenience perceived. The type
of shelters at transfer locations were also registered, and where the aforementioned at-
tributes played a role in the size of the transfer penalty, these rather inﬂuence the transfer
wait time. The results showed tendencies that wait time is actually perceived differently
depending on the type of shelter that the traveller waits under.
The research on this topic has merely begun, and a lot of other attributes could be con-
sidered for estimation. This should also include attributes for access and egress which
are thought to play a great role in route choice as well. As this and other studies show,
the overall disutility of a route can be decreased by considering changes at the transfer
locations. These changes might prove more worthwhile than, for instance, an upgrade of
159
==PAGE:174==
160
CONCLUSION
existing transport vehicles to save travel time. The time spent researching how to save
travel time might turn out to be disproportionate compared to the time spend research-
ing transfers. That is, there might be socioeconomic beneﬁts by reducing the perceived
transfer time compared to the perceived travel time.
The most important goal is to improve current route choice models in order to enable
more precise prediction of passenger ﬂows. This will entail a more focused framework
for how to plan transfers in a way that minimises the disutility experienced. By improv-
ing the attractiveness of public transport trips a shift from private to public transport is
expected.
Today’s focus on sustainability is often seen conceptualised in speciﬁc goals and some-
times these include improving public transport. With a higher level of detail in the public
transport route choice models the decision makers will have a tool for evaluating initia-
tives and implement the most cost-effective.

Appendix A
Learning objectives
Names
Mette Berner Dyrberg, s092817
Charlotte Christensen, s132446
Title
Transfers in public transport route choice models
Learning objectives
• Organize and carry out a project work with a clear structure including project state-
ment, relevant theory, analyses and discussions
• Describe the concept and importance of transfer penalties in trafﬁc models
• Review and systematize existing research on estimation of transfer penalties
• Explore the methodologies made use of in estimation of route choice models
• Construct a list of attributes explaining transfer penalties
• Collect data describing transfer attributes in the Greater Copenhagen Area
• Apply relevant route choice models such as Multinomial Logit and others to esti-
mate the effect of attributes related to transfer penalties
• Verify obtained results and modify the estimated model accordingly
• Propose possible actions to minimize transfer penalties in the network of Greater
Copenhagen Area
• Discuss the results obtained and the pitfalls of the methods applied
• Write a technical report and give an oral presentation, both in an understandable,
well structured and well documented form

ESTIMATED MODELS
==PAGE:196==
Appendix E
Wait time with piecewise linear
utility function
This appendix present the results from three models testing different piecewise linear
utility functions for wait time. The tests are based on model 11.1, where the shelter types
are not included. This was done to test the piecewise linear function on the wait time
alone in order to see, whether it would ﬁt the model better or not. The intention was to
further test on the wait time including shelter type, but due to limited time of this thesis
it was not possible. However, it is assumed that the relation between the shelter types
would remain the same, since the piecewise linear utility function is linked to the wait
time and not the shelter type.
Three models were tested, where all three models include piecewise linear utility for
wait time. The intervals for each of the three models are presented below followed by the
estimated results for each of the three models.
Table E.1: Interval for the three models tested for piecewise linear utility function for wait time.
Intervals
Model
1. interval
2. interval
3. interval

above 7 minutes

0-2 minutes
